The author of this book, having completed for you, my children, this book, which is a complete part of the science of medicine, and having reached its limit in clarity and explanation, has seen fit to complete it for you with this chapter, which is a part of practical application by hand.
Because practical application by hand is utterly lacking in our country and our time, to the point that the study of its science nearly ceased and its trace disappeared. But only a few traces of it remained in the books of the ancients, which have been distorted by hands, affected by error and confusion, until its meanings became obscure and its benefit distanced itself.
Therefore, I have seen fit to revive it and compose this chapter on the path of explanation, clarity, and brevity, and to present images of cauterizing irons and other tools of the work, for it is an addition to clarity and a collection of what is needed.
The reason there are no skilled practitioners with their hands in our time is that the practice of medicine is long, and its practitioner must first exert himself in the science of anatomy, as described by Galen. So that he may grasp the benefits of the organs, their preparations, temperaments, connections, and disconnections, and the knowledge of bones, nerves, muscles, their number and exits, pulsating and stagnant veins, and their points of exit.
Therefore, Hippocrates said that physicians are many in name but few in deed, especially in the practical craft. We have mentioned a portion of this in the introduction to this book.
Because he who is not knowledgeable about the anatomy we have mentioned is likely to make mistakes that will kill people, as I have witnessed often in those who imagine themselves knowledgeable in this field without knowledge or understanding.
I have seen an ignorant physician who incised a swine-like tumor on a woman's neck, then severed some of the neck's arteries, so the woman bled to death in his hands.
I saw another physician, attempting to remove a stone from an elderly man's bladder, the stone being large. He acted rashly, and removed it by cutting a piece of the bladder itself. The man died about three days later.  I was called to remove it, and having seen the size of the stone and the patient's condition, I was aware of the danger.
And I saw another physician who earned his living practicing medicine with some merchants of our city.
A dark-skinned boy had a fracture near his heel, along with a wound.  The physician, in his ignorance, quickly bound the fracture to the wound tightly with splints and bandages, leaving no room for the wound to breathe.
Then he indulged his desires, and left him for days, telling him not to loosen the bandages until his leg and foot had swollen and he was close to death.
I was summoned to him.  I quickly loosened the bandages, and he found relief and lessened his pain, but the damage had already set in firmly in the limb, and I could not stop it.
The damage continued to spread throughout the limb until he died.
I saw another physician treat a large, cancerous tumor.  After a few days, it ulcerated, and the patient's condition worsened.  This is because if the cancer is purely black, it should never be treated with iron at all.
Unless it is in a limb that can be completely removed.
Therefore, my son, you should know that manual treatment can be divided into two categories: treatment that is accompanied by safety and treatment that is often accompanied by injury.
I have warned in every place in this book about work that carries risk and fear, and you should avoid and reject it.
So that the ignorant might not find a way to speak ill and criticize, use caution and firmness, and show kindness and certainty in your treatment.
Use the best path leading to safety and a good outcome, and avoid dangerous and difficult-to-cure diseases.
Purify yourselves from that which you fear might bring doubt to your religion and worldly affairs, for it is more enduring for your reputation and raises your standing in this world and the hereafter.
Galen said in some of his precepts: Do not treat a bad disease and call yourselves bad physicians.
This article has been divided into three chapters.
The first chapter.
In cauterization with fire and cauterization with sharp instruments, a chapter arranged from the heel to the foot, with illustrations of the instruments and cauterizing tools, and everything necessary for manual operation.
The second chapter.
On incisions, punctures, venesection, cupping, surgeries, the removal of arrows, and similar procedures, all arranged in chapters, with illustrations of the instruments.
The third chapter.
On fractures, dislocations, the treatment of sprains, and similar conditions, arranged in chapters from the heel to the foot, with illustrations of the instruments.
The first chapter.
On cauterization.
Before describing the procedure, it is necessary to discuss its benefits and drawbacks, and in which temperament it should be used.
I say that the discussion of the benefits and harms of cauterization, its subtleties, and hidden secrets, is a lengthy and detailed matter; many wise men have spoken upon it.
Their opinions differed, and I have concisely summarized the essential points from their discussions, fearing to prolong the text.
I say that cauterization is generally beneficial for every ill temperament, with or without material cause, except for two temperaments: the hot temperament without material cause, and the dry temperament without material cause.
As for the dry temperament with a material cause, they differed. Some said that cauterization is beneficial in it, while others argued the opposite, stating that cauterization is not suitable for illnesses caused by heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
It is impossible to be cured of a hot, dry illness with a hot, dry remedy.  The one who argues against this says that cauterization with fire may indeed benefit from a hot, dry illness that occurs in people's bodies. Because when you add the human body and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you make the human body cold.
And I say with him, because experience has revealed this to me repeatedly, although this should only be considered by someone who has practiced and trained extensively in the art of cauterization. And to understand the diverse temperaments of people and the nature of illnesses themselves, their causes, symptoms, and duration.
As for other temperaments, there is no fear of them, especially cold, moist illnesses.  All physicians agree on this and do not disagree on the benefit of cauterization in their case.
Know, my children, that the secret of treating with cauterization by fire lies in its superiority over cauterization with a burning medicine, because fire is a simple element whose effect is limited to the organ cauterized and does not harm another connected organ except to a slight degree.
Cauterization with burning medicine may extend its effect to other organs and possibly cause an incurable illness in the organ, and perhaps even death. Fire, because of its noble and precious nature, does not do this unless it is excessive.
This has become clear to us through experience, due to the length of our service and care in the craft, and our understanding of the facts.
Therefore, I have dispensed with lengthy discourse; if it were not unbecoming to this book, I would have presented to you the secret and method of fire's action on bodies, and its removal of diseases, through a rigorous philosophical argument that delves into your understanding.
Know, my children, that they differ on the time suitable for cauterization, and have deemed the best time to be spring.
I say that cauterization may be suitable at any time, for the harm caused by the season is outweighed by the benefit obtained through cauterization. Especially if the cauterization is for a pressing, severe, and dangerous ailment that cannot tolerate delay, lest a greater calamity result than the slight harm caused by the season.
Do not think, my children, what the common people and ignorant physicians imagine: that a cauterization which cures a disease will never return and that you make it permanent.
And the matter is not as you suppose, for cauterization is like a medicine that changes the temperament and dries the moisture that is the cause of the ailments.
However, cauterization is preferable to medicine due to its speed of success, strength of action, and intense power.
The disease may return at some time, depending on the patient's temperament, the severity and strength of the disease, and the accumulation of impurities within the body, and neglect of acquiring proper foods and similar causes.
Unless the disease for which cauterization is used is a mild one in a part of the body with few impurities and moistures. Like cauterizing a tooth for pain and the like; in such cases, the pain may not return, and this will be the least.
Also, the common saying that cauterization is the last resort in medicine is a correct statement, not in the sense they believe, because they believe that no treatment will help after cauterization, but the matter is different.
The meaning of cauterization being the last resort in medicine is that when we have employed various treatments for a disease, and those medications have not been effective, and then we use cauterization as the last resort, and it is successful, then in this case, cauterization is said to be the last resort in medicine. Not in the sense understood by the general public and many ignorant physicians.
The ancients said that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, because they said that gold is balanced and noble in its nature, and that the cauterized spot does not suppurate, but this is not universally true.
Because I have experienced it, and found that it only does this on some bodies, and not others, and cauterization with it is better and superior to iron, as they said, but if you heat the gold cautery in the fire, you will not be able to ascertain when it is heated to the desired degree of gold's redness.
Because cold quickly affects it, and if you increase its heat in the fire, it melts and fuses, which puts the craftsman in trouble; therefore, cauterization with iron is, in our opinion, faster and more accurate for use, God willing.
I have organized this chapter on cauterization into sections arranged from the head to the foot, so that the student can easily find what he needs, God willing.
Chapter One.
Cauterization of the head is single cauterization.
This cauterization is beneficial for excessive moisture and cold in the brain, which are the cause of headaches, frequent colds from the head to the eye and ear area, excessive sleep, toothache, and sore throat. And generally, for all diseases resulting from cold, such as paralysis, epilepsy, stupor, and similar ailments.
The procedure for this cauterization is to first instruct the patient to induce vomiting with a laxative medicine that is good for the head, for three or four nights, depending on the patient's strength, age, and usual condition.
Then, instruct him to shave his head with a razor, then have him sit before you, squatting, with his hands on his chest.
Then, place the base of your palm on the base of his nose, between his eyes. Where your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then, heat the olive oil cautery, which has this form.
If the bone is exposed to the size of a needle's head or a carob seed, remove your hand. Otherwise, reheat your hand with the same iron or another if it cools down. Until you see the bone as I described to you. Then take some salt, dissolve it in water, and soak a piece of cotton in it. Place it on the affected area and leave it for three days.
Then apply a piece of cotton soaked in ghee and leave it there until the burning sensation subsides.  Then treat it with the four-component ointment until it heals, God willing.
They say that the longer a wound remains open, the more pus it produces, and that this is better and more beneficial.
Some say to cauterize the skin down to the bone, holding the cautery until some of the bone's thickness is burned.  Then to remove the burned bone and subsequently treat it.
Others say that one should cauterize intensely, affecting the bone profoundly, so that it falls away like a small piece of quartz or a small fragment.  They claim that this expels vapors from the head from that area, leaving the wound open for a long time.
Then it is treated until it heals.  I do not at all consider these two types of cauterization except in some people and with a degree of risk.  Leaving it alone is preferable in my opinion.  And with safety, if it is so, the head weakens when its natural connection is broken apart, as we have seen in all the other organs.
Especially when the patient's head is naturally weak. The first type of cauterization is safer and preferable in my opinion. Use it, and you will be safe, God willing.
Second Chapter.
On cauterization of the head.
If a persistent pain occurs in the entire head, and this affliction has lasted a long time for the patient, and they have used poultices, cataplasms, flagellations, ointments, and bandages, and especially if a single cauterization (as we described) has not helped, then consider... If the patient's head is strong by nature and not weak, and if they experience intense cold, cauterize again slightly above the previous cauterization. Then cauterize each horn of the head with a cauterization until the thickness of the skin is gone and the bone is exposed to the extent we described. And cauterize with a cauterization at the back of his head, in the place known as the axe; be gentle here, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will experience intense pain there, unlike the pain of all other head cauterizations. I will mention this cauterization in its proper place.
The cautery used for the horns and back of the head should be gentler than the cautery used for the middle of the head.  This is its form.
Chapter three.
In cauterization of the chronic sister.
If pain occurs in the skull fissure, accompanied by headache and extending to the eye, let the patient be purged with head-clearing medications, and use all the other treatments I have mentioned in the classifications of diseases.  If that is not successful, the cauterization is of two types. Either by cauterization with a strong, burning medication, or by iron.
As for cauterization with the burning medication, take one clove of garlic, peel it, and cut its ends from both sides. Then slit the painful area of the temple with a wide scalpel, until there is a space under the skin large enough to accommodate the clove.
Then insert it under the skin until it disappears, then compress it firmly with a bandage for fifteen hours.  Then remove it and take out the garlic. Leave the wound for two or three days, then apply a dressing.
Apply a cotton swab dipped in clarified butter until the area becomes suppurated. Then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If you wish, you can do this with some of the burning medications I have provided in the eighteenth chapter on burning medications.
As for cauterizing with iron, do it in this way: heat the cautery iron, whose shape is as follows:
It is called the nail-shaped cautery because its head resembles a nail, with some grooves and a small projection in the center.
Then place it on the painful area and hold your hand while rotating the iron slightly, until the burn on the skin's thickness is about half of it. Then remove your hand so as not to burn the artery underneath, causing bleeding, and soak a cotton swab in salt water and place it on the area, leaving it for three days. Then cover the cotton with oil, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize this vein with the pointed, knife-like part of the cautery, and be careful not to cut the artery in this vein, especially the chronic one.
Fourth Chapter.
Cauterizing a chronic vein.
If you have treated the vein using the previous treatments and the treatments mentioned in the divisions of illnesses, and the treatment proves ineffective, and you see a cause that the previously mentioned cauterization, either by medicine or fire, cannot address. Then you should heat the knife-like cautery until it turns white, after marking the location of the pain with a line half a finger in length or so.  Leave your hand once, pulling it steadily until the artery is cut and it reaches the bone.
However, you must be careful not to touch the jaw that moves during chewing, lest you burn the muscle or nerve that moves it, causing spasm.
Be cautious and vigilant of the bleeding from the artery you have cut, for there is risk in cutting it. Especially for those who do not know what they are doing and have not been a practiced hand. To abandon the procedure is better.
We will discuss the treatment of accidental arterial bleeding in its proper place in the book, God willing, if you see a condition for which this cauterization is not suitable.
If you see the patient's body capable of it, cauterize a spot in the middle of the head, as described, and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
If you wish, you may use the cautery mentioned in the chapter on severing the artery with the two-bladed cautery; it is a better and more effective cautery than this one.
Fifth Chapter.
On cauterizing earaches.
If an earache occurs due to cold and is treated with laxatives and other treatments mentioned in the classification, but the pain does not subside, then heat the cautery, called the point, which has this image.
Then, after heating it, drip it around the entire ear, or around both ears if the pain is in both. Move the cautery slightly from the base of the ear after marking the area with ink. The cautery should be about ten drops in each ear, or thereabouts. Then treat the areas until healed, God willing.
Chapter Six.
On cauterizing the ear.
The ear ailments treated by cauterization are of the type caused by phlegm, as I mentioned in the classifications of diseases.
One should avoid cauterizing the type that arises from dryness and nerve spasms.
If, having treated this type of earache with irrigations, poultices, and gargles, your treatment is unsuccessful, then the afflicted person should be cauterized three times. One at the base of the ear, the second slightly below the temple, and the third at the junction of the lips.
Make your cauterization on the opposite side of the affected area, because relaxation occurs on the side that appears healthy.
The cauterization should be done in the form of a cauterization opposite the upper part of the ear, slightly below the temple, and another one in the temple, with a length equal to the thumb.
Bring your cautery hand down until you burn about half the thickness of the skin. This is the image of the cautery iron, which is a kind of knife, whose image was previously presented, but it is slightly milder than it, as you see. The knife should have a slight excess of thickness, then treat the area as previously mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
On cauterization for chronic stroke.
If the stroke has become chronic and you have treated it with what we have mentioned and your treatment has not been effective, and there is no fever in the patient, cauterize four times. A cauterization on each temple, a cauterization in the middle of the head as we mentioned, and a cauterization at the back of the head, as previously stated.
The method of cauterization, as previously mentioned, and a cauterization may also be applied to the mouth of the stomach, which would be more effective. Then treat with what has been mentioned previously.
Chapter Eight.
On cauterization for forgetfulness.
The one that arises from phlegm, the patient should first be given large doses of purgatives and herbs that purify the brain.
Then, his entire head is shaved, and a mustard poultice, as described in the chapter on dressings, is applied to his back multiple times.  This is a type of cauterization.
And do this in the same manner as I mentioned there. If he recovers from this, then cauterize him with three cauterizations on the back of his head, arranged from the top of the head to the lower neck.
Place the thickness of a finger between each cauterization. Then, treat the cauterization as previously described. If you desire further treatment, and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle cauterization.
If you want further treatment, cauterize him on the temples, and then treat him until he is healed.  The cautery should be olive-oil based, in the manner previously described.
Chapter Nine.
On cauterizing paralysis and relaxation of the entire body.
First, cleanse the head with the remedies we've described. Then shave the patient's head and cauterize one point in the middle of the head, one on each temple, and one on the back of the head.
And three on the cervical vertebrae. If the ailment of relaxation of the body requires more than that, and the patient can tolerate it, and the illness is severe and entrenched, cauterize four points on the vertebrae of his back. Cauterize until most of the skin is burned, and then raise your hand. Treat it as described earlier until, God willing, it is healed. Use an olive-oil cautery.
Chapter Ten.
On cauterization for epilepsy.
The epileptic, whose epilepsy is due to phlegm, should first have his brain cleansed with large leeches and with all other treatments mentioned in the section, if the patient is strong and can tolerate taking medications.
But if he is a child who cannot tolerate medications, then he should use gargles and mouthwashes to cleanse the brain many days before that, while also improving his diet.
Then shave his head, and cauterize him with one cauterization in the middle of the head, as was described previously, and another cauterization at the back of his head. And on each temple of his head, a cauterization. If the patient is strong and can tolerate it, cauterize him with the cauterizations mentioned in the section on paralysis and body relaxation at the cervical and dorsal vertebrae, and the cauterization should be olive-oil based, as described previously.
If the patient is a child, make the cauterization gentle in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
On cauterizing melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is corrupt humours and thick phlegm, cauterize him with the cauterizations we described for paralysis.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess of black bile and the patient's body is moist and corrupt, purge what will purify his brain, as mentioned in the classification.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a tight cake of flax like a circle, then place it in the middle of his head. The patient sits crouched, holding it from all sides. Then take one pound of old sheep's lard.
Then heat it over the fire to a moderate heat, as much as the finger can tolerate if inserted into it. Then empty it into the circle on his head and let it cool. Do this to the patient once a week with the rest of his good care, until he is healed, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize him with many small, scattered points without holding the cautery in your hand, but rather by a kind of fumigation.  This type of cauterization moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply a cloth soaked in lard or chicken fat to him.
The twelfth chapter.
On cauterizing the water that flows from the eye.
If you perceive the water flowing from the eye initially by the signs mentioned in the classification, then quickly treat the patient, cleanse his head, and protect it from all moisture.  Sweat him in the bath on an empty stomach for days.
Then shave his head and cauterize it once in the center of the head, and cauterize twice on the temples, if the water is flowing from both eyes or from one side if it is only one eye.
Then, with the cautery, cut all the veins and arteries under the skin, and let the cauterizations extend lengthwise and across the temples, while preventing any blood loss.
If you see anything of it, cut it in place with any treatment possible, and we will come to the wisdom of dealing with arteries, cutting them, and preventing bleeding.
And it may be cauterized twice deeply in the nape of the neck, under the two bones.
Chapter Thirteen.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic tears.
If the tears of the eye are chronic and persistent, and they originate from veins and arteries on the surface of the head, and you are certain that this is from superfluous, thick, phlegmatic coldness, then cauterize with the cautery described by itself in the beginning of the descending water. A cautery in the center of the head, and two cauteries on the temples, and two cauteries in the nape of the neck under the bones.  And if you need more, cauterize with a cautery on each side of the eye's corner at the edge of the eyebrow with a small cautery iron.
Chapter Fourteen.
Concerning the cauterization of foul-smelling noses.
If you have treated it with what we mentioned in the classification and the treatment is ineffective, then immediately shave the patient's head and cauterize the middle part with the olive oil cautery. Then cauterize with the nail-shaped cautery iron, two cauteries above the eyebrows, slightly under the hair, and be careful of the artery, do not cut it.  This is a depiction of the nail-shaped cautery iron.
Chapter Fifteen.
Concerning the cauterization of eyelid relaxation.
If the eyelid is relaxed due to disease or moisture, cauterize the eyelid once with this crescent-shaped cautery iron.
And if you wish, cauterize slightly above the eyebrows, two cauteries on each side, spaced from the temples, and the length of each cautery should be equal to the length of the eyebrow.
Do not overdo the cauterization, but only to the point where one-third of the skin is burned.  And the shape of the cautery iron should be like this.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterizing the eyelid.
If its eyelashes turn inward and injure the eye, cauterization is of two types. Either cauterization by fire or cauterization by a burning medicine.  As for cauterizing it with fire, you should first instruct the patient to remove his eyelashes, if he is one who plucks them, so that they grow long and even.
If it injured it while growing, bind his eyes with a bandage to prevent movement until they grow, and when they have grown and are even, place the patient's head on your lap, then make a mark on the eyelid with ink in the shape of a leaf.
Begin the mark near the eyelashes, then place a cotton swab soaked in egg white or the saliva of the badger under the eyelid. Then heat an iron.  This is its image.
Then cauterize it in the shape you marked, little by little, many times, until the surface of the skin burns completely, like a whole, visible leaf of the mugwort. A sign of your successful work is that you see the eyelid has drawn back, and the hair has risen from the eye's flesh.
Then lift your hand, and leave him for three days.  Then apply a cotton swab with ghee, until the scab is removed. Then treat with ointment until he is healed, God willing. If some hair grows back later and the eyelid relaxes,
Then repeat the cauterization at that spot as you did at first. If the hair is in the lower eyelid, cauterize it until it returns to its natural position and does not injure the eye. As for cauterization with the burning medicine, tell the patient to leave the eyelashes to grow long and even.
Then make a picture of a mugwort leaf from paper. Then take from the usual soap and quicklime, one dirham or similar weight of each. Grind them both thoroughly, doing so quickly so they do not dry. Then spread it on the paper, shaped like a mugwort leaf, and place it on the one or both eyelids. Place a cotton swab soaked in egg white under the eye, and the patient's head in your lap.
Place your index finger on the medicine and gently press it as you move it whenever the patient feels the medicine burning, because it feels like fire. As long as he feels the burning, leave the medicine and move it with your finger.  Once the burning subsides, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And see if you see the eyelid has risen, as it rises when cauterized by fire or cut. Otherwise, apply more medicine to the spot where the medicine did not take effect and did not turn black, until you complete your work and the eye is cauterized. Then place cotton with ghee on it, until the heat's irritation subsides.
Then treat it with the palm ointment or another, until it heals.  You should, when working, take the utmost precaution that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye.  If the eyelid relaxes after a few days and you fear any hair in the eye.
Then reapply the medicine to that part of the eyelid that has relaxed, just as you did at first, and treat it until it heals.  This is a depiction of a mugwort leaf.
Know that people's eyes differ in size and age.  Therefore, let your cauterization be appropriate to this.  The path to correctness is not hidden from those who have experience in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cauterizing the fistula that appears in the eye's corner.
If you treated the fistula with the methods described in the sections on diseases, and your treatment was not successful, then it is necessary to cauterize it in this manner.
Order the patient to place his head in your lap, and have a servant hold the head firmly, without movement or shaking.
Then place a cotton pad soaked in egg white or the juice of a  *barzquṭūn* (possibly a type of plant) on his eyes, and then heat the cautery iron, whose shape is as illustrated.
It should be hollow, like the shaft of a bird of prey's feather, on the one side where the cautery is applied.  If you wish, the other end can be pierced or, if you prefer, solid like a rod. However, the hollow one is better for your procedure, God willing.
Then, squeeze the fistula if it is open, and remove the pus and dry it, or if it is not open, open it and remove its pus.  Then apply the cautery iron, very hot, and hold it with your hand until you reach the bone.
Keep your hand slightly away from the eye towards the nose when cauterizing, so that you do not miss the mark or disturb the patient, and the cautery iron does not fall on the eyelid and damage it. If you reach the bone on the first cauterization, then stop. Otherwise, reheat the cautery iron if necessary.
Leave it for three days, then apply a cotton pad with clarified butter and treat it with the desiccated ointment until it heals. If, after forty days, it has not healed, apply the strong, corrosive medicine to expose the bone and pare it according to what will be described in its chapter, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing the fistula, described by some of the ancients, is to carefully cut open the fistula and insert a thin funnel into the incision—this is its image.
Pour in molten lead, weighing about a dirham, and hold the funnel firmly in your hand; the patient must remain absolutely still, so the molten lead does not flow into their eye. You should place a cotton pad soaked in egg white or water over the patient's eye.
The lead will burn the fistula and cure it remarkably. If the fistula heals with the cauterization and treatment described, then well and good. Otherwise, it is necessary to use the nasal speculum and reposition the fistula into the nasal passage, as will be described in its specific section.
Chapter Eighteen.
Cauterization of cleft lip.
A cleft in the lip, often called a "hairlip," frequently occurs, especially in the lips of young boys.  If treatment using the methods described in the classification is unsuccessful, apply a small, knife-like cautery iron in this manner.
Make its cavity as thin as a knife, then apply it hot immediately to the cleft itself until the cauterization reaches the depth of the cleft. Then treat it with *qiyruți* until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Nineteen.
Cauterization of a fistula occurring in the mouth.
If a swelling develops at the base of the gums, or on the palate, or at the roots of the teeth, then ulcerates and bursts, forming a fistula from the discharge of pus, and if treatment is unsuccessful, then a cautery iron, sized to fit inside the fistula, should be heated.
Then, introduce it hot into the opening of the fistula, and hold it until the heated iron reaches its depths and furthest point. Do this once or twice.  Then treat it afterward with the treatment we previously mentioned until it heals, God willing.
If the matter [pus/discharge] stops and heals, then well and good. Otherwise, it is necessary to examine the area and remove the corrupted bone, as will be explained in its section, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
For loosened teeth and gums.
If the gums have become loose due to moisture and the teeth have shifted, and treatment with medicine has been ineffective, then place the patient's head in your lap.  Then heat the cautery iron, the image of which will come after this, and place the tube onto the tooth.
Quickly insert the hot cautery iron, and hold it for a little while until the patient feels the heat of the fire reach the root of the tooth. Then remove your hand. Then repeat the cautery iron several times as desired.  Afterwards, have the patient rinse his mouth with salt water.
He should hold it for an hour and then spit it out.  The loose tooth will become firm, and the relaxed gums will tighten, and the corrupt moisture will dry up.
Chapter Twenty-one.
On cauterizing toothache.
If the toothache is due to cold or worms, and the medication is ineffective, cauterization can be done in two ways: either with oil or with fire.
As for cauterization with butter, take cow's butter and boil it in an iron spoon or a shell.  Then, take a cotton ball, wrap it around the end of a probe, dip it in the boiling butter, and place it quickly on the aching tooth, holding it until it cools.
Then repeat this several times until the heat reaches the root of the tooth.  Alternatively, you can dip wool or cotton into cold butter and place it on the aching tooth, then place a heated iron on top of it, until the fire reaches the base of the tooth.
As for cauterization with fire, use a brass or iron tube, somewhat thick to prevent the heat of the fire from reaching the patient's mouth.  Then, heat the cautery iron and place it on the tooth itself, holding it until the iron cools. Do this repeatedly.
The pain will disappear either on the same day or the next.  Following the cauterization, the patient should fill their mouth with good butter and hold it for an hour, then spit it out. This is the image of the cautery iron.
Cauterize with whichever end you choose, according to what is possible. This is an image of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On cauterizing pigs.
If pigs are affected by phlegm and cold moisture, and do not respond to maturation with medication, and you want rapid maturation, then heat the hollow cautery iron, whose image is shown, with both ends perforated.
To allow smoke to exit from the other end during cauterization, hold it on the same tumor for a time, and again if necessary, until it reaches the depth of the tumor. If the tumor is small, make the cautery iron the same size as the tumor.
Then leave it for three days and apply a cotton swab soaked in clarified butter until what was burned by the fire disappears, then treat it with ointment and lint until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-three.
On cauterization for hoarseness of voice and shortness of breath.
If phlegm dominates the trachea, especially if accompanied by a cold temperament, the patient should first be purged with laxative medications, then cauterized with a cautery at the notch of the throat, near the base of the gullet, in the low-lying area.
Be careful not to reach the gullet with the cauterization, and burn only half of the skin.  Then cauterize another time at the joint of the neck, at the end of the last bead of it, with a strong cauterization.  Let the cautery be a pointed one, as described previously.
Then treat him/her with what was previously mentioned until he/she is healed, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On cauterizing lung disease and coughs.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold moisture, and the patient does not have a fever or consumption, and the illness is chronic, cauterize twice above the collarbones, in the soft, empty, low-lying areas, and once more in the middle of the chest between the breasts.
The cautery should be a pointed one, as previously described.  If you wish, let the cauterization be by dripping from a cautery called the "point," the image of which was previously shown in the section on treating ear pain.  The points should number from thirty or thereabouts.
Then treat him with what has been previously mentioned until he is healed.  You may also use a three-pronged cautery, in the manner described,  and hasten the cauterization, as you cauterize three areas at once with this.  This is the image of the cautery.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On cauterizing the armpit.
If the head of the humerus dislocates due to slippery moisture or does not stay in place when returned from dislocation, so that it becomes habitual to dislocate and then dislocates at the slightest movement, as we have seen, the jaw must first be repositioned. Then the patient lies on his back or on his right side, and the skin inside the armpit is lifted upward with the fingers of your left hand if the joint has dislocated inward.
Then the double-bladed cautery, which is depicted in this picture, is heated.
Then, cauterize the skin with it until it penetrates to the other side.  The cauterization pattern is four cauterizations.  It may be cauterized with a three-bladed cautery in which case the pattern will be six cauterizations, and the blades are the thickness of the vessel.
This is a picture of the three-bladed cautery.
One more may be added to this number, making eight cauterizations.  Then, the pounded balls of medicaments mixed with salt are applied to the cauterization. The patient must rest and not move the limb for a time until it strengthens.  Dislocation upward is infrequent.
Apply one good, nail-like cauterization or multiple small cauterizations above the shoulder blade.  Then, the joint will become stronger and the moisture will disappear, and the patient will recover.
Chapter Twenty-six.
On cauterizing the stomach.
If the stomach is afflicted with cold and excessive moisture, disturbing its temperament, and frequent discharges occur despite treatment with various remedies, the patient should lie on their back, extend their legs and arms, and then be cauterized three times. Apply one cauterization beneath the breastbone, sized like a finger, with a nail-shaped cautery iron, and two more below, each alongside the initial cauterization to create a triangular pattern.  Space them appropriately so that they do not join if they become infected. The depth of the cauterizations should be two-thirds the thickness of the skin, and the shape of the cauterizations should be triangular.
In this manner and to this extent, no more.
And if you wish, cauterize it with one large cauterization in the center of the stomach, in this manner.
And the cautery iron used for this single cauterization should be circular in shape, in this manner.
The stomach may also be cauterized by puncturing.  For those who fear this method, one may apply dots to the stomach, as desired, with ink, then cauterize them with a pointed cautery iron, and then treat them with the previously described treatment until healed.
Chapter Twenty-seven.
On cauterizing the cold liver.
If a pain in the liver arises from cold and moisture, or from a hot wind, to the point that it has departed from its natural temperament in excess, and the patient has been treated as described in the classification, but that treatment has been ineffective. The patient should lie on their back, and three cauterizations should be applied with ink to the liver, exactly like this shape and size, beneath the collarbones, where the human arm's bend ends.
After each cauterization, there should be the thickness of a finger's width between them. The cauterization should be along the body in a straight line, and do not press the iron with your hand forcefully.
The amount of skin to be burned should be about half its thickness, no more.  The patient should stand on their feet; if unable, they should lie down with legs extended and arms raised. This is the shape of the cauterizing iron.
You may be able to perform these cauterizations with a knife-like cauterizing iron if you are skillful and careful in your technique, taking precautions not to overdo the cauterization, burning the entire skin and reaching the belly and intestines; the skin there is thin, so know this.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
Cauterization of a liver tumor.
If an abscess appears in the liver, and you wish to know if the tumor is in the liver's flesh or its lining, if it is in the liver's flesh, the patient will experience heaviness and pain, but not severe pain.
But if it is in the liver's lining, the pain will be intense and severe.  If the physicians have exhausted their treatment options, the patient should lie on their back, and then mark the swollen area with ink.
Then heat the cautery in the fire, the cautery resembling a spatula, and this is its image. Cauterize it with a single cauterization until all the skin is burned and the cauterization reaches the lining, so that all the matter is expelled, then treat it with the treatment for abscesses until it heals.
This type of cauterization should only be performed by someone with extensive experience in the medical arts, who has treated these illnesses repeatedly. Only then should they undertake such a procedure.  To leave it to someone else would be better.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On cauterizing the fistula.
The ancients described cauterizing cold fistulas with the roots of the zeraوند plant, as follows: take one long, dry root of the zeraوند, as thick as a finger, then dip it in oil and heat it in fire. Then cauterize once between the collarbone and the neck, and two small cauterizations slightly angled towards the area beneath the chin, and two larger cauterizations above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
And two more cauterizations between the fifth and sixth ribs, angled slightly backward, another cauterization in the middle of the chest, and one above the stomach. Three cauterizations on the back, one between the shoulders and two on the flanks, below the cauterization that is between the shoulder blades.
One should not insert the cauterizing instrument deeply, but rather use it on the surface of the skin, lightly touching it. Some of the ancients stated that some people used an iron cauterizing tool similar to a spatula, heating it and using it to reach the tumor between the ribs.
The pus will discharge, as we mentioned in the case of a liver tumor, and in this instance, cauterizing the abscess carries the risk that the patient will die immediately or that an incurable fistula will form at the site.
Chapter Thirty.
On cauterizing the spleen.
If the treatment of the spleen, as described in the previous section, is ineffective, cauterization can be applied in three ways, all of which are correct: one is to cauterize it three or four times, in a line along the spleen.
In the shape of the liver cauterizations previously described, and there should be a space between each cauterization the width of a finger, or slightly more.  Use the same cautery as described for liver cauterization.  Do not make the cauterization deep.  The patient should be lying on their back.
The other method is at the elbow, lifting the skin opposite the spleen, where the patient's left elbow ends.  Lift the skin across the width of the body so that the cauterizations run lengthwise along the body.
Then, insert the cauterizing instruments, very carefully, until they penetrate the skin on the other side; then, withdraw the cautery, leaving four cauterizations. If desired, use the three-pronged cautery instead.
Then treat the cauterized area after allowing it to discharge pus for many days. This is more effective than any other treatment described previously.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On cauterizing dropsy.
Cauterization is only beneficial in ascites (dropsy of the abdomen) in particular. If you have treated the person suffering from dropsy with the various treatments we have previously mentioned in the classification and your treatment has been ineffective, then you should cauterize four times around the navel and once on the stomach.
Another cauterization should be performed on the liver, and one on the spleen, and two on his back between the ribs, one opposite his chest and the other opposite his stomach.  The depth of the cauterization should be nearly equal to the thickness of the skin. Then leave the cauterization open to discharge pus for a long time.
Do not neglect the patient with further treatment after cauterization, as is necessary for the gathering of the relevant factors to accelerate his recovery.  The shape of the cautery used for the back is pointed, as was previously described, and the one used for the front is olive-shaped.
Chapter Thirty-two.
On cauterizing the feet and legs.
If the feet swell in dropsy and become filled with yellow water, it is necessary to cauterize the back of the foot at the point between the little finger and the ring finger.
Hold your hand with the cautery and do not bend it; then raise your hand and do not lower it again, for this will allow the yellow water to drain out, and the cautery should be of this shape.
Then cauterize the legs, two cauterizations on each leg. The cauterization should be with the sharp tip of the cautery, and the cauterizations should be along the length of the leg, one below the knee and another below that, towards the middle of the leg.  Do this twice on each thigh.
Leave the cauterization open without treatment for a long time, allowing the water to drain out, then treat it with the rest of the methods we have described.
Chapter Thirty-three.
On cauterizing diarrhea.
If the diarrhea is due to cold and dampness, weakening the binding and digestive forces in the stomach and intestines, and if other treatments have failed to cure it, and if you see the patient is strong enough to tolerate a cauterization, then cauterize a large area on his stomach. As previously mentioned, cauterize the stomach with a circular cautery iron.  Perform four cauterizations around the navel using a fine pointed cautery iron, and cauterize the area on the buttock above the coccyx with a large cautery, or two smaller ones.
If you see that the dampness is abundant, and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the pubic area, and cauterize each flank.  Perhaps add two small cauterizations near the larger one on the stomach.  This is a successful and unfailing treatment, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-four.
On cauterizing hemorrhoids of the anus.
If there are many chronic hemorrhoids in the anus, or one, and they are composed of thick, cold humors or corrupt moisture, and the treatment mentioned in the classification was unsuccessful, cauterize the patient with three cauterizations on the lower part of the lumbar spine, slightly below the table, in a triangular shape.
And a cauterization under the navel, about two fingers in size.  If you judge that the patient's stomach is cold and food is not digesting, and you see the face swollen, cauterize the stomach with a large cauterization as described previously, and a cauterization on the liver. And another one on the spleen with a nail-shaped cautery. Leave the cauterization open for a while, then treat him until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-five.
On the cauterization of warts.
After cutting it, if the wart is cut, heat the cautery, which resembles a needle, then insert it hot into the same cut wart and press your hand firmly until the cautery reaches the mouth of the blood vessel from which blood flows. Do this once or twice.
If the warts are numerous, cauterize each one as described, then treat them with appropriate ointments until they heal. If a large cauterization is applied to the cotton, it will be more beneficial.
Chapter Thirty-six.
On cauterizing the fistula that occurs in the perineum and its surroundings.
If the patient does not respond to the incision and the procedure described in its place, and avoids it, he may be cured by cauterization.  If someone has a fistula and it has lasted, discharging pus and corrupt humors, the first thing you should do is to measure it with a thin probe.
Then determine the depth of its penetration with the probe, then heat the cautery, which resembles a stylus, and insert it, hot, into the fistula itself, directly along the path of the fistula's penetration and the depth reached by the probe.  Apply the cautery to it repeatedly, one or two or three times, as needed, until all the corrupt matter is burned.
And be careful not to burn a nerve, if one exists, or a large blood vessel, if the fistula empties into the bladder or the intestines.
And be cautious of all these locations.  Only do this if the fistula is in a fleshy area, and you determine it is not penetrative.  Then treat the area until it heals. If the area seals over, the discharge stops, and it remains that way for a while, know that it is completely cured.
If the discharge does not stop, know that it is penetrative, or there is corrupt bone within its depth, or something similar, which will be mentioned in its place.
Chapter Thirty-seven.
Concerning the cauterization of the kidney.
If pain occurs in the kidney due to cold or thick wind, and the patient's sexual potency is thereby diminished, it is appropriate to cauterize the loins, over the kidneys themselves, cauterizing each kidney with the nail-shaped cautery described previously.  It may be cauterized a third time on the same area, resulting in three consecutive cauterizations for greater benefit.
Chapter Thirty-eight.
Concerning the cauterization of the bladder.
If weakness and relaxation of the bladder occur due to cold and moisture, so that the patient cannot control urination, cauterize the bladder with one cauterization below the navel, where the pubic hair begins, and with another cauterization to the right of the navel and another to the left.
After cauterization on each side, the cauterization should be the size of a thumb's breadth.  A cauterization should be made at the base of the spine, or two if needed. The cautery should be nail-shaped, as previously described.
Chapter Thirty-nine.
Concerning the cauterization of the uterus.
If the uterus contains a substance of cold and moisture, preventing conception, corrupting the menstrual flow, or making it difficult during menstruation, or if pain occurs during menstruation, then three cauterizations should be made around the navel, as previously described for cauterizing the bladder. One cauterization on the lower back, over the lumbar region, or two, using a nail-shaped cautery.
Chapter Forty.
Concerning the cauterization of a dislocated hip.
Erroneous moistures may accumulate in the acetabulum, causing dislocation.  A sign is that one leg is longer than the other when the legs are compared, and a hollow is found at the site of the dislocation.  It is necessary to cauterize the affected area, directly on the acetabulum, with a roughly circular cautery.
After marking the acetabulum with ink, in the same way it would be drawn into the center of a circle, and using the cautery shown in the example of stomach cauterizations; if this cautery is not available, cauterize with three applications of the large olive-shaped cautery.
Make the cauterization deep to the full thickness of the skin, and then treat it until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Forty-one.
In cauterizing sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint due to cold and dampness, and the treatment described in the classification was ineffective and prolonged, it is necessary to thin the thick humors with rotten seeds or prickly pear seeds, and the like.
Then cauterize, and the cauterization has two types: either cauterization with burning medications or cauterization with fire. Cauterization with fire has many types, one of which is to cauterize the hip joint itself with three triangular cauterizations in this manner.
This produces a beneficial depth, and the space between each cauterization is equal to the thickness of a finger. The cautery is olive-shaped.  A cauterization might also be made in the center, on the head of the hip itself, creating four cauterizations. If desired, cauterize with the circle previously mentioned for hip dislocation, using one cauterization.
So that the circle surrounds the entire hip and burns the entire thickness of the skin. This is the method of cauterization in sciatica, if the pain is confined to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
If it extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize with two cauterizations on the thigh in the location where the patient points to the pain, and one cauterization above the ankle, slightly towards the outer side, using a knife-shaped cautery. The depth of the cauterization should only be the thickness of the skin.
If he indicates that the pain extends toward the toes, cauterize there with a pinpoint cautery three or four times, or more if necessary. If he indicates pain below the knee towards the leg, cauterize there with one knife-shaped cauterization.
Take care in all your cauterizations not to burn a major nerve or artery, lest you cause the patient a grave injury or long-lasting harm.
I have seen one and another who cauterized above the ankle, and overdid the cauterization, causing gangrene of the leg, even reaching the foot, causing it to fester entirely and ruining the whole leg, followed by diarrhea and death afterward.  If the pain is in both areas, cauterize them in exactly the same manner.
Some scholars among the wise men have described a hip cauterization using an instrument shaped like a cup, made of iron, with a diameter of half a span.  The rim of this cup is as thick as a date pit, or slightly less.  Within this cup are another cup, and a third.
The space between each cup should be the width of the thumb.  The cups should be open on both sides, and their height should be about one or two thumbs.  A handle of iron is fitted to the cups, and this is its form.
Then it is heated in the fire until it glows red and sparks fly, then placed on the hip joint, the patient resting on the sound side.  Three circular cauterizations are made in one instance.  Then, the patient is left for three days and bandaged with ghee, allowing the wound to remain open for several days.
Then, it is treated with ointment until healed.  The author of this book states that this type of cauterization is rarely used due to its severity and frightening appearance, and few can tolerate it.  However, it is a good cauterization for those who can endure it and who are affected in that location.
As for cauterization with burning medicines, it is to make two cups like the neck through which the latch of the door descends, made of copper or iron.
They will have a height of about two thumbs, and the space between them will be the thickness of a finger.  The bottoms and tops of the cups will be open, and they will be fastened together in this fashion.
Then, they are placed on the hip joint, the patient lying on their sound side.  Hold your hand gently, and then pour a small amount of hot, sharp water between the circles.  Hold it for about the time of an hourglass. The patient will endure the stinging and burning, as it feels like fire.
Until the stinging subsides, then lift your hand with the instrument and wipe the sharp water from the entire hip with fresh water. Then leave it for three days and apply grease to it, until the blackness of the burn disappears. Allow the pus to drain for several days; this will be more beneficial.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals. If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make a mold for it, to contain water, in the same way you made one for the hip. This is a description of the sharp water, and its efficacy is established in my treatise on the preparation of medicines.
Take one part of potash and one part of quicklime, grind them, and place them in a new pot with a single small hole at the bottom, just large enough for a rod to enter. Place another glazed pot under the base of the first pot.
Pour fresh water over the potash and lime to cover them by a finger's breadth, after firmly pressing them with your hand. Leave the pot until the sharp water drips into the glazed pot below. Collect all this water and pour it over fresh lime and potash.
Then it will be extremely sharp and potent, effective in many medical procedures and cauterizing other parts of the body, because it acts like fire itself.  Some medicines, such as *thāfisīyā*, *balādhur* honey, and lime mixed with soap, can also cauterize the hip.
Galen, quoting an ancient physician, claimed a cure for hip pain and sciatica, greatly praising it and claiming that no other treatment was needed and that it would heal in a single application.
To the point that a person might enter a bath carried and exit cured. This is done by taking fresh, green *shiṭraj*, or if green is unavailable, fresh dry *shiṭraj*.  Grind it with some fat, and apply it to the hip where the pain is, or to the leg or thigh.
Then bind it tightly and leave it for three hours, or as long as the patient feels the burning subside. Afterward, have him enter a bath.  Once his body is moist, have him enter a basin.  The pain will depart, and he will be healed, God willing. If not, repeat the dressing after ten days.
He will be healed, God willing.  Know that none of these cauterizing medications should be used until the body has emptied itself.  Dioscorides mentioned that goat's dung, when used to cauterize sciatica, is beneficial.
The cauterization should be done like this: take wool, soak it in old oil, and place it on the deep area between the thumb and the elbow, closer to the elbow. Then take dry goat's dung.
Then ignite it with fire until it becomes ember, place it on the wool, and leave it until it dies out.  Then take another and repeat until the sensation extends from the middle of the upper arm to the hip, and the pain subsides, God willing.  This type of cauterization is called cauterization with dung.
Chapter Forty-two.
Concerning cauterization for back pain.
Pain in the back may arise from various causes, such as a fall, blow, excessive vomiting, and similar occurrences.  It may result from the effusion of cold, moist matter.  Cauterization is applied only in this type of case.
In cases of effusion of cold matter, after the patient has vomited, using rotten herbs or similar remedies, one should cauterize the back where the pain is, creating three rows, each the width of a table, after marking the area with ink. In each row, cauterize five or more times, depending on the patient's tolerance and strength.
The cauterization should be done with a pointed cautery. Or, if you prefer, three or four cauterizations with a medium-sized nail cautery, in this manner.
Chapter Forty-three.
On cauterizing the initial stage of a swelling.
This ailment often affects young children.  A sign of its initial stage in a child is shortness of breath upon standing and moving.  At the end of the spinal vertebrae, a pearl-like protrusion is found, standing out from the other vertebrae.
If you see this and wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery, in this way. Apply the cautery to each side of the vertebra evenly.  If you wish, cauterize around the vertebra with a pinpoint cautery, in two or three rows.  Let the points be close together.  Then treat the area until it is healed as described.
And remember to use cautery on swellings resulting from nerve spasm.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On the cauterization of gout and joint pain.
If joint pain is due to cold dampness affecting any part of the body, and if the pain occurs in the legs, physicians usually call it gout, specifically.
If the cold gouty cause is treated with the methods we mentioned in the classification and the pain does not subside, then cauterization removes it.  Cauterize the area around the joints of the legs with many cauteries after inducing vomiting. The cautery should be olive-oil based, of medium intensity, in this manner.
And if it requires puncturing the leg, do so with a pointed cautery.  If the pain ascends to the knees or other joints, as often happens, cauterize each knee three or four times on each side with the same olive oil cautery.
If more cauterization is needed, cauterize, but do not delve deep with the cautery; it should only be about the thickness of the skin. If the pain ascends to the hips or back, use the cauterization methods described in that section.
If the pain is only in the hands, puncture around the forearms in a row-like fashion. If the pain remains in the fingers, puncture each joint and the palm of the hand. If, after a few days, the pain ascends to the elbows or shoulders,
Cauterize both (elbows/shoulders) on each side.  The patient should not neglect good treatment and taking medications. If the patient improves their diet and expels phlegm, then he will recover with this cauterization, God willing.
Chapter forty-five.
On the cauterization of hernias.
If a hernia appears in the groin, and some of the intestines and omentum descend into the testicle, and this is a recent development, the patient should be instructed to abstain from eating that day and to use laxatives to expel feces from his body.
Then, he should lie on his back before you, and you should instruct him to hold himself still until the omentum or intestines protrude.  Then, you should return them with your finger.  Then, mark the area beneath the hernia on the pubic bone with ink, making a mark resembling half a circle, its tips toward the upper part of the body.
Then, heat a cautery iron—this is its form.
Until it becomes white, throwing sparks. Then, return the intestines or omentum to their place. Then, a servant should place his hand over the area to prevent the intestines from protruding again.  His legs should be spread apart, and a cushion should be placed under him. Another servant should hold his legs together.
And another should hold his hands, while you place the cautery iron on the same mark. Your hand holding the cautery iron should be straight and firm, until the cautery reaches the bone, or repeat the procedure if it didn't reach the bone on the first attempt.
Be careful to prevent the intestines from protruding while cauterizing, so that you do not burn him, causing either death or a great calamity. Know that if your cauterization does not reach the bone, your effort will not be successful. The cautery iron should be gentle in proportion to the patient's age, whether child or adult.
Then, treat the cauterized area with ghee after three days, to remove the scorching of the fire, and then treat it with other ointments until it heals. Let the patient lie on his back for forty days until the wound is sealed.
His food during his treatment should be such as to soften his bowels, to prevent the intestines from protruding during bowel movements.  Then, if he wishes to stand after forty days, use a tight bandage and keep it on for another forty days.  Minimize exertion, and the intake of food, drink, and loud noises.
If he follows this treatment, he will heal completely, God willing.  I will discuss the treatment of hernias by incision in its appropriate section, God willing.  As for hernias occurring in the rest of the abdomen, if you want to prevent them from growing, cauterize a circular area around the hernia, in proportion to its size. The burned skin should be about a third of the hernia's size. Then treat it as described above; it will not increase, God willing. Some earlier physicians have mentioned cauterizing the hernia with a triangular cautery iron in this manner.
After marking the hernia with ink, making a line across the upper side of the hernia and another to the lower side, place your hand in the middle to cauterize with a single, nail-like cautery. The first cautery is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter Forty-Six.
On cauterizing the fistula.
If, in any limb, a fall or blow causes pain that persists for a long time, and treatment with medication is ineffective, then it is necessary to cauterize with a small cautery iron.  The cauterization of each limb should be in accordance with its size, smallness, weakness, and strength.
And if the pain and fistula yield to it [the cauterization], then he will be healed from the first cauterization; otherwise, repeat the cauterization, for it is the nature of these pains to spread from the limb to the area near it; therefore, it is necessary to follow it up with cauterization until the patient is cured.
Chapter Forty-Seven.
On cauterizing leprosy.
As for those with leprosy, they may benefit greatly from cauterization, especially the type of leprosy that arises from the putrefaction of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cauterize him, observe. If the leprosy is in its early stages and you have treated it as described in the section on classification, and it has not declined, nor stopped, and you fear that corruption will affect all his humors, cauterize him five times on the head. One in the usual center of the head, the second below it, towards the forehead at the end of the hair, two on the temples, and one behind, on the occiput.  Employ your cauterizing tool a little more forcefully so that it makes a slight impression on the bone.
Scales will peel off, making it easier for the thick vapors to escape. Also cauterize the spleen with a cauterization as previously described. However, if the leprosy has persisted and become evident on the patient, you should cauterize with the described methods on the head. One cauterization on the tip of the nose, two on each cheek, two on the cervical vertebrae, six on the thoracic vertebrae, one large one on the coccyx at the base of the tail, another above it in the same region, and two on each thigh, one on each. Two on each knee, one on each, two on the shoulders, two on the elbows, two on the ribs of the chest, and cauterize each joint of his fingers and toes, and each heel of his feet and each wrist.
And be cautious of the nerve behind the heels, so as not to burn them.  A cauterization can also be applied to the pubic bone, the stomach opening, and the liver.  Know that the more cauterizations you apply, the more effective and beneficial they will be.
Know that the patient experiences less pain from the cauterization than a healthy person, because their body is numb.  The size of the cauterizing iron should vary according to the affected organs and joints, as described in the properties of the irons.
Then treat the cauterized area with fine crushed carob, honey, and other remedies until it heals, God willing.
The Forty-eighth Chapter.
Concerning cauterizing numbness: When a part of the body has been numb and treated with medications, ointments, and dressings, but still does not heal, cauterize the numb area with cauterizations appropriate to the size of the affected area; make the cauterizations slightly deeper in the thickness of the skin.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  Cauterization can also be used for some numbness that affects the hand or foot in the spinal column's vertebrae at the point where the nerve that moves that limb exits, thereby relieving the numbness.
Only those knowledgeable about the anatomy of the limbs and the exits of the nerves that move the body should undertake this.
The Forty-ninth Chapter.
On cauterizing leprosy.
If leprosy persists despite all medical attempts, cauterize it with a slight depth, proportionate to the thickness of the skin, until the whiteness disappears and the color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour, rose oil,  orchis root, dove's blood, or the blood of hooked birds, one part of each, mixed together and applied to a cloth, which is then bound to the affected area until it heals.
The Fiftieth Chapter.
On cauterizing cancer.
If the cancer is in its early stages and you wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, moving in a circle.  Some scholars have suggested a strong cauterization in the center, but I do not favor this, for I anticipate ulceration, and have observed such instances repeatedly.
Therefore, the correct approach is to cauterize around it in a circle, as we said, or with many small cauterizations.
The Fifty-first Chapter.
On cauterizing the abscess.
If an abscess has formed and is slow to mature, either because of the agent causing it, or due to the age of the patient, if they are elderly with little blood, or due to time, and you want to hasten the abscess's maturation, cauterize around it with many small, scattered cauterizations.
Then leave it, for it will hasten maturation. If you wish to cauterize it further, heat the cautery iron, which is depicted in this illustration.
Place it in the center of the abscess until it penetrates the skin.  Let the cauterization be from below so that the discharge of pus is easier. Then treat it as appropriate until healed.
Chapter Fifty-Two.
On cauterizing the ulcer.
An ulcer is nothing but corruption that spreads in a limb and consumes it, just as fire consumes dry wood. If you see an ulcer in a place suitable for cauterization with fire, heat a cautery iron with many small and large nails, according to what is appropriate for the location of the ulcer.
Then cauterize it from every side until all corruption is eradicated and nothing remains.  Then leave it for three days and apply powdered sulfur mixed with oil to the cauterized areas until all scabs and all corruption are removed.
Then treat it with healing ointments that promote flesh growth. If you see after three days that the flesh grows healthily without corruption, then leave it be. Otherwise, repeat the cauterization on the remaining corrupt areas.  The ulcer may also be treated with a strong medicine.
For it (the strong medicine) is quicker in its benefit than fire.  I have mentioned its treatment with strong medicine in the classification, so take it from there when you need it.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
On cauterizing inverted and non-inverted nails.
This affliction, a rough and painful condition, often occurs on the soles of the feet.  Cauterization for it can be done in two ways: either by fire or by strong water.
As for cauterization by fire, one must heat a hollowed-out iron cautery, resembling an eagle's beak, to a size that encompasses the nail on all sides. Its edges should be thin.  Then, bring it down onto the nail, and rotate it around the nail until it reaches the nail's depth.
Leave it for three days, allowing pus to form.  Then, dress it with ground wild basil mixed with salt, leaving the dressing on for a night.  It will then detach from its roots.  The wound site should then be treated with a flesh-producing ointment until it heals.  This applies for non-inverted nails.
This often occurs on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet.  One should take a tube of copper, iron, or eagle's beak, and place it on the nail or wart.  Then, pour a small amount of strong water into the tube.
Hold the tube while rotating it, gently pricking the skin to allow the tube's edge to reach the nail's base.  The strong water should penetrate to the root of the nail. The patient should endure the stinging sensation of the strong water for a little while.
Then leave it; the nail will detach at its roots.  Do this to each one until you have treated all of them on the body.  Then, treat the areas after their detachment with flesh-producing ointments.
Chapter Fifty-Four.
In the treatment of the scabies.
If someone is affected by scabies from cold, or fever, or other such causes, then it is necessary to cauterize them four or five times on the back, with a cauterization between each spot on the back, and also one on the chest. And on their stomach, cauterize with an olive oil cautery, as this will ease the scabies and quickly ripen the cold disease.
Chapter Fifty-Five.
In the cauterization of a wound that has occurred on the body.
Ugly boils may appear on the body, caused by thick, corrupt, cold materials.  At their first appearance, a gentle cauterization should be applied to the head of each boil, using a stick whose end has been ignited by fire, or with a long zeraound stem, or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And carbuncles, in their initial eruption, should be cauterized in this manner.  Thus, the causative agent will not increase and will dissipate, and the patient will recover, but this should only occur after the patient has been bled by venesection.
Chapter Fifty-Six.
In the cauterization of bleeding occurring when an artery is cut.
Bleeding often occurs from an artery severed by an external wound, or when a tumor or a body part is incised or cauterized, and similar instances, making it difficult to stop.
If this happens to someone, quickly apply your index finger to the mouth of the artery and press firmly until the blood is contained under your finger and nothing more comes out. Then, place several small and large olive-wood cauteries in the fire, and blow on them until they are extremely hot.
Then, take one, either small or large, depending on the wound and the location where the artery has ruptured, and apply the cauterizing iron to the same vein after quickly removing your finger. Hold the cauterizing iron until the bleeding stops.
If the bleeding resumes when you remove your finger from the mouth of the artery and the cauterizing iron cools, quickly take another cauterizing iron from the prepared fire and continue doing this one after another until the bleeding stops.
Be cautious, so you do not burn a nerve, for this would cause another affliction to the patient. Know that if an artery is bleeding, it cannot be cut, especially if it is large, except by one of four means: either by cauterization, as we have said, or by amputation, if it has not already been severed. If it is severed, its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop; or it may be tied with strong threads; or the medications that will stop the bleeding and firmly compress the area may be applied.
As for those who attempt to cut it with a ligature, or by compressing with cloth, or by applying burning substances, and the like, it will not stop in that way, except rarely.  If one experiences this and there is no physician or medicine available, they should immediately place their index finger over the mouth of the wound itself, as we have described.
He should press firmly until the blood is contained and flows out above the wound and onto the artery and finger.  The finger must remain on the wound continuously, applying very cold water, until the blood solidifies, thickens, and stops.  During this time, one should consider what cauterization or medication is required.
The second chapter.
On incision, cupping, bloodletting, and other surgical procedures.
Khalaf said: We have previously mentioned in the first chapter all illnesses in which cauterization by fire and burning medicines are suitable, their causes, reasons, and instruments, as well as the various types of cauterization.  We have organized this into chapters, from the neck to the feet.
And I will follow the same approach in this chapter so that the student can easily achieve their goal.
Before I begin, you children should know that this chapter contains more uncertainty than the first chapter on cauterization.
Therefore, the warnings must be stronger, for in this chapter, the practitioner frequently depletes the vital blood when opening a vein or incising a tumor, cupping an abscess, treating a surgical wound, extracting an arrow, or incising a stone, and similar procedures. All of these are fraught with danger and fear, and death often results.
I advise you to avoid anything questionable, for in this practice, you will encounter diverse patients with various illnesses.  Some may be weary of their illness and find death preferable.
Because of the severity of their illness and the prolonged suffering, some exhibit symptoms that indicate death. Others offer you their wealth, hoping for recovery, but their illness is fatal.  You should not take any help from those who are in this condition.
Let your caution be stronger than your desire and eagerness.  Do not undertake anything without certain knowledge of the likely outcome.  In all your treatment of patients, employ thorough knowledge and warn them of the possible consequences for their well-being.
For in this, you will gain praise, glory, and renown. May God inspire you, my children, with wisdom, and keep you from error and misfortune.  For all this is in His power.  There is no god but Him.
I have arranged this chapter into sections, following the previous chapter on cauterization, from the crown to the feet, so that you may find what you need more easily, God willing.
The First Chapter.
On the treatment of water that collects in the heads of young children.
This affliction often occurs in infants at birth if the midwife applies pressure to the infant's head without gentleness.  It may also arise from a hidden, unknown cause.
I have seen this affliction only in infants, and all those I have seen have died quickly.  Therefore, I have found it best to avoid treatment. I have seen an infant whose head filled with water and whose head grew larger each day. Until the infant could no longer sit up because of the size of his head, and the moisture increased until he perished.
This moisture either collects between the skin and bone, or beneath the bone on the peritoneum.  If the moisture is between the skin and bone, and the swelling is small, then a single, horizontal incision should be made in the middle of the head, about two fingers in length, to allow the moisture to drain.  This is the image of the lancet.
If the moisture is more extensive and the swelling greater, make two intersecting incisions in this manner.
If the moisture is beneath the bone, and the sign is that the sutures of the head are open on all sides, and the water flows inward when pressed with your hand, this is not unknown to you; then three incisions should be made in the middle of the head, in this manner.
After the incision, all the moisture will drain. Then the incisions should be bound with cloth and bandages.  Then apply dressings of herbs and oil from above, until the fifth day. Then, the bandages should be loosened, and the wound treated with ointments and compresses.  Maintain moderate head support.  The patient should be nourished with all dry foods, low in moisture.
Until the limb strengthens and heals. Another method of incision is to observe where the bone of the swelling and the accumulation of water appear, as it may be more prominent in the back of the head, or the front, or the right, or the left.
Aim the incision where the swelling and the accumulation of water appear to you; incise it as best you can, taking care not to cut a blood vessel, lest bleeding occur and the patient die from that bleeding, while expelling the moisture.
The Second Chapter.
Severing the two arteries behind the ears, known as the *hassisin*.
When someone suffers a severe affliction of the eyes or chest, and prolonged treatment with medication proves ineffective, the best treatment in such cases is to sever these two arteries.
