Therefore, if you wish to sever them, you should shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth to reveal the artery, then tighten the patient's neck using the patient's own clothes, then look for where the veins pulse, and their two locations, the two sunken spots behind the ears.  It is rare that they are hidden, except in some people.
Then mark them with ink, then cut them with a dissecting scalpel, severing them to the bone.  This should be done with the head facing you. If you wish, insert the scalpel beneath the artery and cut it upwards.  The length of the cut should be about two clenched fingers.
If the artery is severed, the blood will flow out with a pulsating, forward, and continuous stream.  If the artery is not visible, then one should estimate a distance of approximately three fingers from the ear, mark it with ink, and cut to the bone.  What is necessary to...
Approximately six ounces of blood will be released immediately; sometimes more or less, depending on the patient's strength and the fullness of their arteries. Then, examine the wound. If any part of the peritoneum remains on the bone, sever it.
So that an inflammatory swelling does not arise, then bandage the wound with a linen bandage and treat with ointments until healed.
Chapter Three.
On the vessels of the temples.
If a person suffers from chronic headaches or acute illnesses caused by sharp humidity and heat in the muscles of the temples, or from severe chronic headaches, and other similar conditions, and various medical treatments have been ineffective, then we have tried in these illnesses to cauterize or dissect the vessels of the temples, as described.
The method for dissecting them is that the patient should shave the hair of the temples, and locate the visible artery of the temple; its pulsation will make it clear, and it is rarely hidden, except in some individuals.
Or, if it is obscured by the cold, the patient should tighten his neck with his clothing. Then, you should rub the area with a cloth or apply warm water until the artery becomes clearly visible. Then take the scalpel that has this image. Then, gently dissect the skin with the scalpel until you reach the artery. Then, insert a forceps and pull it upwards until you extract it from the skin and remove any membranes underneath from all sides. If the artery is thin, twist it with the forceps. Then, cut a portion of it, long enough to separate its ends and contract it, without causing bleeding.  If it is not severed and cut, the blood will not recede.  Then, empty from six to three ounces of blood.
If the artery is large, it should be tied in two places with strong, doubled thread, either silk or bowstring, so that putrefaction does not quickly affect the wound before it heals, causing bleeding.
Then, cut off the excess between the ligatures. Do this at that moment or after a different time. If you wish, cauterize it to the bone with a scalpel-like cauterizing iron, so that its ends are severed. This fulfills the same action, or is even better, as we said.
However, if the patient has a fever or a heated temperament, cauterization is beneficial for eliminating moisture, and thus is more effective.  After dissecting the arteries, the area should be packed with old cotton, and firm dressings should be applied.  After healing, it should be treated with dry medicines that promote flesh growth and with compresses until healed, God willing.
If bleeding occurs from the artery during your procedure, immediately stop it, either by cauterization or by filling the area with alum and holding your hand firmly until the bleeding stops. If you lack any of these, place your finger on it to clot the blood.
Apply very cold water to the area until the inflammation subsides and it tightens as it should.  An easier and less difficult method than dissecting the artery is to cauterize the vein with this double-bladed cauterizing iron after marking the two locations with ink.
Apply it very hot, until it reaches the bone, and sever the vein at two points so the distance between the ends is increased; it will never adhere together with this cauterization. This is the image of the cauterizing iron.
It has two sharp blades, resembling small razors, but much less sharp than a knife. If they were as sharp as a knife, the cold would reach them quickly and the flesh would not detach quickly.  If they have some thickness, the fire's heat is contained.
Cutting the flesh quickly, this method is better than any other, easier and simpler. Let the distance between the blades be the thickness of a finger.
Fourth Chapter.
For treating the constant, hot tearing of the eyes.
If the tears are constant, and flowing from the veins on the skull from the outside, and no medical treatment with drugs is effective, and you see the patient's face has become red. He feels a crawling sensation in his forehead, like ants crawling, and his eyes are sunken and moist, their edges eroded and their eyelids stuck together by the intensity of the tears.  The ancients, in treating this, resorted to this procedure, calling it treatment by the sword:  ordering the patient to shave the hair from his forehead.
Then, three parallel incisions are made in the forehead, along the length of the forehead. Each incision is about two fingers long, parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second slightly offset from the movement of the muscle in the temple, and the third on the opposite side.
And be careful not to cut the arteries on either side, and keep your hand away from the contact between the jaws. After each incision, there should be a space of approximately three fingers clenched together. Have with you prepared sponges or many dry cloths to absorb the blood.
Then, introduce the sharp, two-pronged scalpel, which is of this form. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, use it to dissect all the skin between the incisions, along with the periosteum on the bone. Then, do the same from the middle incision to the third incision, and then remove this scalpel. And introduce into the first incision another instrument, called a one-sided sharp, one-sided smooth, non-sharp knife, which is like this (image).
Place the sharp side of the knife towards the flesh adhering to the skin, and the smooth side towards the bone. Push it until it reaches the middle incision, and cut all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes, without the cut reaching the surface of the skin.
Then do the same from the middle incision to the other incision.  After a moderate amount of blood has flowed, compress the areas of solidified blood.  Then, place a wick of cotton in each incision and apply a dressing soaked in a mixture of broth and oil, or vinegar and oil.
To prevent a hot swelling.  On the third day, the ligaments heal.  Use abundant lukewarm water compresses. Then treat with basil ointment, rose oil, and other remedies used for wounds until healed, God willing.
Chapter Five.
In treating tears and discharges from the interior of the head to the eyes.
When someone experiences frequent, severe, persistent discharges, and their eyes appear sunken and small, with diminished vision and ulcerated eyelids, and loss of eyelashes. And there will be a sharp, painful ache deep within the head, accompanied by repeated sneezing.  Know, from these symptoms, that these materials and discharges originate from numerous deep veins.  The best treatment for this is to order the afflicted person to shave their forehead, then make a single incision in the center of the forehead.
And there will be a sharp, painful ache deep within the head, accompanied by repeated sneezing.  Know, from these symptoms, that these materials and discharges originate from numerous deep veins.  The best treatment for this is to order the afflicted person to shave their forehead, then make a single incision in the center of the forehead. or slightly raise it laterally, beginning the incision from the left temple to the right temple, and let the incision be towards the bone. Keep your hand away from the chewing muscles of the temples, until the bone is exposed and all blood is wiped away with a sponge.
Then separate the edges of the incision with used cotton or flax thread, and secure it from above with bandages.  Saturate the bandages with medicine and oil, to prevent inflammation.  Once the inflammation subsides, the bone must be rubbed to stimulate the growth of flesh.
Then treat it with a desiccated preparation that promotes flesh growth, such as taking two parts wheat flour and four parts  *qalfuniya* (likely a type of resin or gum), preparing an ointment from them, and using it to promote flesh growth in such wounds.
Sixth Chapter.
For the treatment of what falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic stone or a semi-metallic stone such as iron and glass; or a plant seed, such as chickpeas, kernels, and the like; or a liquid substance, such as water and vinegar.
Or it may be an animal. If a stone or a similar small object that does not grow within the ear falls in, expose the ear to sunlight. If you see the stone, put some violet or scerge oil on it, then try to remove it by moving the head or inducing sneezing with a stick. And plug the nostrils when sneezing happens, after placing a band of lint or wool around the ear and tilting the ear upwards. This treatment often removes it. If not, then try to remove it with a gentle cloth, which has this shape.
If it comes out with the cloth, otherwise, try to remove it with a delicate, slightly bent, blind hook.  If it doesn't come out with that, make a copper tube and insert the end of the tube into the ear hole, gently.
Seal the area around the tube with softened pitch mixed with oil, so that the wind has no path other than through the tube. Then, pull it out with a strong pull.  This often removes it. If it doesn't come out as described, take a small piece of Nabataean gum or prepared gum used to catch birds, and place it at the end of the tube.
After wrapping it tightly with cotton, gently insert it into the ear hole after the ear has dried from moisture. If it doesn't come out with all the methods described, quickly perform the incision before a hot swelling or inflammation occurs.
The method of incision is to first bleed the patient from the temples, to the extent of their strength. Then, sit the patient before you and tilt their ear upwards, and make a small incision at the base of the ear, near the fat in the lowest part of it.
The incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone, then remove it with any tools possible.  Then, quickly stitch the incision and treat it until it heals.
If the object that fell into the ear is one of the grains that grow and swell, try to remove it using the methods mentioned. If it does not come out, then take a thin, gentle lancet, like this one.
Try to cut that type of fallen grain in the ear with it, but only do this if you are certain that the grain has become moistened by the ear's vapor, until it becomes many small pieces.  Then remove it with a blind probe, a gentle forceps, or by suction.
As we have mentioned, it is easy to remove it.  Regarding water in the ear, the patient should first use a censer to induce sneezing.  They should lie on the ear with water, filling it with some old cotton. If it comes out in this way.
Otherwise, take many small, smooth pebbles, warm them slightly by fire, and have the patient insert one into the ear canal.  The patient should lean on one leg on that side, and strike another stone against the stone in the ear.
He should continue doing this with pebble after pebble until all the water is removed.  Water may also be removed by taking a piece of papyrus or a feather, inserting one end into the ear, and heating the other end by fire until most of it burns, and then replacing it with another feather.
Do this repeatedly until all the water is removed, or draw it out with a tube, as previously described with the pebbles.  As for removing an animal inside, if it is small like a flea or similar, treat it with what I mentioned in the classification.  As for...
If it is large enough to be felt, try to remove it with lint and tweezers. This is easier than everything else that occurs in the ear.  As for removing worms that develop in the ear, if treatment with what we mentioned in the classification in the chapter on worms has not been successful.
Then you should look into the ear in the sun. If you see any worms, remove them with lint or delicate tweezers. If you do not see any, take a tube—this is its form.
Narrow at the bottom, wide at the top. Insert the narrow end into the ear as far as the patient can tolerate, then suck forcefully. Do this repeatedly until all the worms are removed. If they do not come out, seal around the tube with wax, as I mentioned to you in the case of the pebble.
If it does not come out with what we have mentioned, use the worms that I have mentioned.  The ancients have tested them for killing worms.  You will find that in the chapter on worms.  You will use the applicators to place ointments and medications into the ear.  Here is its picture/form.
As you see, make it of silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, wide at the top.  If you wish, the projectile within the tube can be made of tight copper. Or, if you prefer, take a rod and wrap cotton tightly around one end.
Then put the ointment, or juice, or whatever medicine you want, into the tube and place it in the ear. Then insert the rod with the cotton from above and squeeze it with your hand moderately until the ointment is driven into the cavity of the ear and the patient feels it.
Have the patient be inside, and let whatever you put in the ear be warmed a little in the fire.  Beware that the substance you are dripping in is not too cold or too hot, as the ear cannot tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
In the treatment of ear obstructions.
Some children may be born from their mothers with their ear canals unpierced.
Some people may also experience an obstruction in the ear canal due to a wound or growing flesh.  This obstruction may be deep within the ear canal and unseen.
Occasionally, it may also appear slightly outside the canal, and be visible.  Obstructions deep within the ear, unseen, are usually more difficult to treat.  Those that are visible should be placed in the sun. And examine it. If you see the obstruction outwardly, then open it with a gentle scalpel. This is its image:
Its tip should have a small, limited protrusion, while the rest of the scalpel should be smooth on both sides so as not to injure the ear. If the obstruction is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a gentle forceps and carefully cut it away until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is deep within the ear, take a smooth, gentle probe, warm it slightly in fire, then insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the obstruction, along with the patient's experience of hearing difficulty, gently remove it.
Be careful not to injure the eardrum. Then, place a wick into the ear, sized to fit the canal, that has been soaked in Egyptian ointment.  Repeat this for several days until the wound is healed. Alternatively, soak a wick in water and sprinkle powdered alum over it.
Use this. If you see that the ear has become swollen with a hot swelling, it is necessary to remove the wick and replace it with another soaked in a preparation of rose oil, so that the hot swelling may subside. Then continue treatment until it is healed.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear. Use the other treatments for stopping bleeding described in the section on hæmostasis.
Chapter Eight.
On the treatment of warts appearing on the eyelids.
These warts appearing on the eyelids may be moist or dry.  They should be grasped with a forceps or tweezers, and cut off at their roots with a scalpel.  Then, powdered antimony should be applied to the area.
If bleeding occurs, cauterize it with a gentle lentil-shaped cauterizing iron.  Cauterization after cutting is better.  It often returns if any part of the roots remain.  If cauterized with fire, these roots are burned and it will not return.
Chapter Nine.
On the treatment of colds affecting the eyelids.
A condition resembling a cold, in its intensity and hardness, often appears on the eyelids.
Therefore, it is called "cold," which is the accumulation of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids.  If the cold is protruding on the surface of the eyelid, moving in every direction, the treatment is simple: slit it transversely.
Then gently scrape it away from all sides until it is removed.  Then suspend it with a hook and cut it.  If cutting it is not possible without penetrating the eyelid, that will not harm the patient.  If the slit is large, stitch it together and treat it until it heals.
If it is small, no harm will come to you from it; the ointment will strengthen and heal it. If the cold is leaning towards the inner surface of the eyelid, turn the eyelid inside out, suspend the cold with a hook without needing to slit it, and trim it from all sides.
If the eyelid is penetrated during the cutting, no harm will result.  Then wash the eye with salt water after cutting the cold, and treat the area with something that will heal it until the patient is well.
Chapter Ten.
On the treatment of the pimple that appears on the eyelid.
The pimple is a swelling that occurs in the layers of the upper eyelid.  It is most common in young children, and it weighs down their eyes, causing them to experience colds in the night and making it difficult for them to look at sunlight. Because tears rush to them.
Therefore, you see them leaning on their faces constantly, and they sleep on them.  Their eyelids under their eyebrows are wet, possibly swollen, and the pimple of the swelling is readily apparent.  When you press the area with your fingers, you feel the pimple between them.  The method of treatment is to have the patient rest their head on your lap.
Then, take a linen cloth, prepare a wick from it, and make a circle from that wick, large enough to encompass the pimple on all sides. Then place it over it and press with your fingers on all sides to gather the pimple into the center of the circle.
Then, with a lancet, make a transverse incision in the middle of that moisture, not larger than an incision made in bloodletting.  As for depth, you should cut through all the skin until you reach the pimple.
In most cases, the pimple emerges from the incision as a white piece of fat.  Grasp it with a cloth wrapped around your fingers, pulling it outwards while rotating your hand in both directions, until it heals.  Be careful not to enlarge the incision, lest the lancet damage the eye.
If the pimple does not appear upon the initial incision, you should slightly enlarge the incision gently until the pimple appears. Then pull it out as described.  Then, dip a cloth in vinegar and water and apply it to the area, binding it firmly. Some people crush salt.
Place it [the salt] in the incision to dissolve any remaining moisture. Then treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs at the site, treat it with cooling dressings until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On methods of treating eye injuries.
If excess eyelashes grow in the eyelid, beyond the natural eyelid line, and become persistent, they harm the eye and cause various ailments such as constant tearing, eyelid relaxation, and cloudiness and thickening, which can lead to blindness.
Treatment of eye injuries can be approached in four ways: by cauterization with fire, by a strong medicine as previously described in the chapter on cauterization, or by cutting and stitching, or by using a cautery, as I will describe.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand; if it turns, then you should insert a needle with thread from below the eyelid and pierce the eyelid with the thread above, close to the eyelashes themselves, and pull the thread upward through the eyelid.
Turn it with a rod, then cut the inside of the eyelid, excluding the extra hair, with a sharp lancet from the larger to smaller eyelid, then remove the thread and place a small padding of cotton or cloth under the eyelid, and then seal the eyelid with medicinal paste.
The shape should resemble a mint leaf, but the shape should be adjusted to the required elevation of the eyelid, as it varies between individuals. Some people may need a certain amount of the eyelid to be cut, corresponding to the degree of eyelid relaxation.
Some need a smaller cut, all depending on the relaxation of the eyelid.  Then, with the lancet, make incisions along the marked lines, starting from the larger to the smaller eyelid.  Each incision should be near the natural eyelashes, about the thickness of a rod.
Then insert a hook into one corner of the skin, and peel away the entire skin.  Then, suture the edges together with a fine wool needle and thread, cleaning away the blood.  Stick any excess threads to the eyebrows with some sticky substance.
If you wish to do it, do it. Otherwise, don't bother.  The sutures and threads remain for about three or four days, then treat it.  If you prefer, leave the wound unsutured and treat it with something that dries and contracts, for the eyelid rises when the wound is closed and joined together.  Suturing is better.
This method of lifting the eyelid, mentioned by the ancients, is laborious for the patient, yet good work with no danger. Another method of lifting the eyelid is to shape the eyelid like a mint leaf, as described. Then lift the eyelid with three hooks, separate or grouped in this manner.
Then cut any excess eyelid with a small pair of scissors, in this manner, making a balanced cut.
If you cannot hold the hooks securely, or if the shape is not correct, take a needle with thread, insert it into the center of the shape, insert another thread near the larger eye-lid, and a third thread near the smaller eye-lid.  Gently gather the threads with your fingers. Then lift your hand evenly using these threads.
Lift the entire marked skin, as described.  Then close the wound edges with stitches and treat it until healed. If a hot swelling appears after the cut or incision, soothe the swelling with qirrot and similar soothing ointments.
The lower eyelid may also have its edges turn inward.  In this case, the methods mentioned—cutting, stitching, cauterization with fire or with a sharp medicine, and cauterization with fire or sharp medicine—are more effective and gentler on the patient than cutting and stitching.  Cutting and stitching are more difficult to perform on these.
Cauterization with reeds is performed in this manner: turn the eyelid over, cut the incision from the inside, as described.  Then make two thin reeds or pieces of wood, their length the same as the eyelid, and their width less than the width of a scalpel.
And having nibbled them on both ends where the threads hold, gently gather them onto the remaining eyelid and firmly tighten the reeds on both sides. Leave it for several days.  The tightened skin will die, turn black, and fester.
Until it falls off of its own accord.  If it is slow, nibble it with the shears, then treat it until healed.  When it is joined, the eyelid will rise and the edges will not irritate the eye. This is the shape and size of the reeds, made in this way:
Chapter Twelve.
In removing irritating hair from the eye with a needle.
If it is one or two hairs, the procedure is to take a thin needle, thread it with a fine, smooth silk thread, then tie the ends in a very gentle knot.  The thread should be about a span in length.
Then, attach another, shorter, thin thread to the needle holder, and tie its ends if desired.  The patient should place their head in your lap, close to the light so the work is clear.  It is precise work. Then, using the needle and needle holder, insert the needle into the root of the extra hair.
And carefully pierce the eyelid until you draw it out from above the natural hair.  Then pull the thread upward towards the needle holder, and remove the hair(s) - one, two, or three at most - from the needle holder.
Then, pull gently until the hair is removed from the eyelid with the needle holder along with the natural hair.  If pulling the needle holder doesn't remove the hair, pull the needle holder downward with the thread you attached to it, so that the needle holder exits the opening from above.
Then reinsert the hair into the needle holder and pull both out through the opening from above, so you see them clearly.  At that point, remove the needle holder and thread, bandage the eye and keep it bandaged for two or three days until the hair is firmly in place and flesh grows around it.
If the hair is short, either add a longer piece of natural hair to help it rise, or leave it until it grows longer and then pull it out.
Chapter Thirteen.
On treating the stye that occurs in the upper eyelid.
This stye, which is called arnbīya, can be either natural or accidental.  The accidental stye results from the inflammation of a wound, a cut, a burn, or similar causes.
The treatment involves incising the inflammation, separating its edges, and placing a linen thread between them, tying it off until healed.  Avoid using things that dry and constrict.
If you do this, the stye will return worse.  Instead, use things that relax it, like fenugreek, soaked in boiled water with the addition of honey, flaxseed, and a diachylon ointment dissolved with some oils.  Apply it to the thread and use it for treatment.
The key to its treatment is to strive by all means to prevent it from adhering in the same way as before. This method we described is only a part of correcting the stye, not restoring the area to its original condition.
Chapter Fourteen.
On treating styes in the lower eyelid.
This stye, which forms from below, is the true stye, and it is either natural or accidental.  The accidental stye arises from a wound, incision, cauterization, or similar causes.
The method is to take a needle with a doubled thread, insert it into the flesh, and pierce it from the left to the right side of the affected area until the thread is on both sides of the flesh.  Then, using the needle, pull the flesh upward and cut it with a broad scalpel.
If the eyelid returns to its proper shape, well and good.  Otherwise, take a probe and place it on the site of the cut. Use it to turn the eyelid and make two cuts on the inside of the eyelid, positioning the ends of the cuts at the corners of the incision you made, until they meet to form an angle. So that when they meet, its shape will resemble this form.
Then, remove the flesh as much as the sharp side of it is below the eyelid, then stitch the separated parts together with two stitches of wool thread.  Then treat it with the relaxing and softening medications as mentioned previously, until it heals.
If the ulcer has occurred from a cut, suture, or cauterization, a small incision should be made, also without the eyelashes, as previously described. Then separate the lips with a poultice, as mentioned before.
In short, in treating an ulcer, whether from above or below, perform the procedure according to the appearance of the ulcer, for its form can vary greatly. The skilled surgeon employs whatever method is possible.
Until the shape returns to, or approximates, its natural form. The disease itself indicates the appropriate treatment and tools in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion can affect many people, causing the upper eyelid to adhere to the white of the eye, hindering its movement and normal function.  A slender instrument should then be inserted under the eyelid.
Raise it upwards or extend it with a hook, then sever the adhesion with a gentle scalpel, not as sharp as ordinary scalpel blades, but rather somewhat blunt, lest the patient be disturbed during the procedure and the scalpel slip, injuring the eye with its sharpness.  Your incision should be as if you are peeling away the adhesion.
And wait until the eyelid returns to its normal position and the entire adhesion heals.  Then instill saline into the eye, wash it with it, or instill a solution of al-zangari (possibly a specific type of copper sulfate) in water through a dropper.  Then separate the eyelid and the eye with a linen thread.
Place moistened egg white over the eye.  After three days, use astringent eye drops until healed.
Chapter Sixteen.
In cutting the nail and deep flesh growths.
The nail is of two types: either nervous, resembling a tough, thin membrane, or non-nervous, resembling a hard, white moisture; if touched by iron or attempted to be grasped by tongs, it breaks.
A tongs will not hold it. Both types, beginning from the largest part, gradually spread until they cover the eye and obstruct light and movement.  The procedure for cutting it involves the patient resting their head on a cushion.
Then, open their eyes and raise the eyelid with your hand, then grasp the nail with a slightly bent tongs and pull it upwards.  Next, take a needle, thread a horse or cow hair and strong thread through it, slightly bend the tip of the needle, and pierce the nail's center.
Pierce it with the needle, tie the nail to the thread, pull it upwards, and peel the side of the nail next to the pupil with the hair, as if spreading it to the end. Then, cut the remaining part at the root of the growth with a delicate scalpel or small scissors, leaving the natural flesh of the growth.
So that cutting it will not cause constant weeping.  The nail may be pulled out with the tongs alone, or with the thread, as we said, and then the smooth scalpel peels the part that looks like this:.
Care must be taken that the scalpel does not touch the cornea, causing a rupture in the pupil.  After the cut, place some powdered salt or green vitriol in the eye and leave it for another day. Then treat it as necessary until it heals.
If the nail is not sensitive and you cannot insert a needle or secure tongs, this type of nail is often treated with medication and heals as described in the section.  If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a gentle, smooth, and blunt scalpel, shaped like this:. Peel the nail gently from above with this scalpel. If you see that it is crumbling and only the peeling has affected it, place a drop of green vitriol, red vitriol, or powdered salt into the eye and compress the eye for a day.
Then continue the procedure until all of it is gone, unless a hot tumor appears in the eye. Leave it, and treat the hot tumor until it heals, then resume the peeling until the nail heals.  And as for the fleshy protrusions...
If the protrusion is causing excessive pain to the eye, grasp it with the forceps and remove some of it.  Do not cut too deeply to avoid tearing.  Then apply a drop of red or green vitriol to the depths until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cutting the fleshy growths and excess flesh in the eye.
In some people's eyes, red, piled-up flesh may grow to cover the pupil or nearly cover it, or even spill over the eyelids, and sometimes the eyelids may turn outward, resembling a rose.
The procedure is to have the patient place their head on your lap, then open their eyes, and carefully remove all the red flesh with suitable forceps.
Or grasp it with a scalpel or a spatula, then cut the flesh piece by piece until it is all removed, and protect the eye from harm during the procedure.  The cutting should be done either with the scalpel that we have described for cutting the nail or with small scissors. Like the way you would remove thorns, as its image will appear in the following chapter.
If the cutting is complete and all the flesh is gone, fill the eye with pounded salt or drop into it red or similar corrosive medicines, and apply a white of egg bandage externally to control the inflammation.
If any flesh remains, and blood flows and you fear inflammation, leave the eye and treat it to ease the inflammation, then return to the procedure until it heals.  Similarly, treat any excess flesh that appears in the eye of this type.
However, you should avoid treating any weak eye by nature or by appearance with surgery or by using a sharp object, and do not insert your hand in any of these procedures until you see that the organ is capable of tolerating the action.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the Extraction of Objects from the Eye.
The threads are red blood vessels woven over the eye, hindering its vision and weakening the eye over time.  Therefore, first you must observe; if the eye containing the threads is strong, and has no other ailment besides the threads...
Then, the extraction involves instructing the patient to place their head on your lap.  Then, using one or two fine, flexible hooks, you should carefully grasp the threads.  (The illustration then shows the method).
Or, two hooks joined together as shown in this image.
Then, gently extract the threads with a fine pair of scissors, wiping away the blood occasionally until you see the eye clear of the threads and the bleeding has stopped.  Carefully avoid injuring the eye with the scissors' blades.  Work in the afternoon sun.
Be very careful in your work to avoid cutting anything other than the threads.  When you are finished, instill green or red ophthalmic drops into the eye to further consume any remaining threads.  If you are unable to remove them all at that time, bandage the eye to relieve its pain.
Leave it for a few days until the pain subsides and any inflammation disappears.  Then, repeat the procedure exactly as described until the eye heals.  This is the image of the scissors' care of the eye.
And leave it [and let the patient's head rest in the student's lap, and another student wipe away tears and examine the front and back of the eye]. The proper procedure is to open the eye with fixed or triangular instruments that a student holds.
Look closely, and let the patient's head rest in the student's lap, while another student wipes away tears and examines the front and back of the eye.  You have the choice of starting to extract threads from the top or bottom of the eye; however, it is better to remove two threads from the upper part, one at the end of the eyebrow and the other at the beginning.
Hold it between the middle and index fingers, then attach hooks underneath, opposite them. Then, hold it between the ring and little fingers, and gently remove your hand so that you see all the threads have risen.  Cut them off with small scissors, or with a gentle scalpel if you wish.
Insert a needle and thread through all the threads until they rise above the membrane. Do not cut the threads while they are still damp, for tears and blood will run onto you.
Then, cut with scissors any threads extending above and below the eyebrow, so that only those within the cornea remain taut. If you can lift them from the cornea, do so with the needle and thread; this is better, and it might be possible in some cases.
If you cut them, the area will be painful for a few days, but it will subside and the inflammation will be relieved.  Then, resume treatment.
Chapter Nineteen.
In replacing the nasal septum.
Doctors call the septum a "nasoor" if, having treated it with cauterization or caustic medication as previously described, it does not heal.  Then, the only recourse is to incise the tumor when it is ripe and to remove all moisture or pus from it.
Until the bone is exposed.  If the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness on it, scrape it with an instrument of this form:
This is called a "coarse-headed" instrument.  It is made of Indian iron, and its head is round like a button.  It is engraved with a fine carving, like a file or a scalpel.  Place it on the area of corruption in the bone, then rotate it between your fingers while slightly squeezing your hand, until you know that the corruption has been removed.
And do this several times, then bind the area with drying, astringent medicines.  If the area is sealed, flesh grows there, the discharge stops, and forty days pass without recurrence, discharge, or incident.
Then know that it has healed.  Otherwise, there is no other recourse but to return the nasoor to the nasal opening in this manner: expose the bone again with iron or a caustic agent.  Once the bone is exposed, take a probe of this form:
The tip of the iron is triangular, and its shaft tapers to a point, as you see.  Place it on the bone itself, near the orifice, and keep your hand a little further from the eye.  Then rotate your hand with the probe until it penetrates the bone and the patient feels it.
By feeling the air escaping from it when the hand is held on the nose.  Then, bind the area with drying, astringent medicines, as I have instructed you. If flesh grows and the area hardens, the matter which was projecting outward will return to the nose, making it easier on the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
On returning the protruding eye.
If the entire eye protrudes, and there is no damage or deficiency in vision, then the patient should be eased, then straightened in the cranium.  Then, place a cupping glass on the back of the head without conditions, and gently cup.
Then place on the eye a compress made from acacia, aloe, frankincense, and cypress.  Then the eye is bandaged from above with many layers of fabric; the description of the layers is that you take many soft bandages, folding each piece into four layers, making many.
Then place them one upon the other as needed by the eye, then firmly bandage them with a strong bandage.  Replace the cupping glass for gentle suction without conditions.  Then remove the cupping glass and leave the eye bandaged for one day and one night.
If the ligament relaxes during this time, it should be tightened again.  Then remove the bandage after one day and one night, as we said. If you see that the eye has returned, then do so; otherwise, repeat the bandage, layers, tightening, and cupping until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In the section on cutting the tunica.
If a rupture occurs in the tunica, and it protrudes outside the eyelids like a visible tunica, disfiguring the person's appearance, and you wish to cut it, the procedure is as described.
To do this, insert a needle into the base of the tunica from below to above, then insert another needle with a doubled thread from the medial side, penetrating it, and leave the first needle as it is.
Then cut the point where the doubled thread bends, and tie some of the tunica around the needle and tighten it firmly.  Then remove the needle and apply wool soaked in egg white to the eye, allowing the threads and tunica to fall away.  Then treat the eye with something that strengthens it.
Until the wound heals.  The tunica may be tightened from all sides with a circular piece of cloth until the eye is drawn firmly.  Then, a thin scalpel is used to reach the depths of the eye, drawing out the white moisture and causing the eye to droop and dry out.  Then, tightening it further, until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
Concerning the treatment of the abscess.
This ailment, called an abscess, is a collection within the eye, resembling falling water but without it.  The treatment involves having the patient sit upright on a chair, and then using both hands to grasp their head at both sides.
And move it, so that you can see the collection moving downwards.  Then, hold it still; it will not move.  The light will be released, and the patient will see objects as they used to.  If it does not descend, we know it is water. If the collection does not descend as described,
Otherwise, have the patient sit before you.  Then, take a thin scalpel and make a small incision just above the corneal membrane, where the diseased tissue joins the cornea at the limbus, so as to remove the matter.
Then, after the matter is removed, instill warm water into the eye, which may have been mixed with honey or water with boiled licorice and honey. Then, continue treatment in other ways until the patient recovers.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have already described the types of water in our classification, and which are suitable for instillation, in an extensive and explanatory manner.  Take this information from there for accuracy.  Then, the patient should be seated before you, squatting in front of a light source, near the sun.
Tie and firmly hold the healthy eye, then raise the eyelid with your left hand if the watery eye is the left eye, or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then, place the tip of the cup near the limbus, with a thickness like a date stone, on the white part of the eye near the smaller angle, and push the cup forcefully while rotating it with your hand until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel the cup has reached an empty space.
The depth to which the cup should penetrate should be equal to the distance from the pupil to the edge of the iris (limbus of the eye).  The copper will be visible to the observer, through the clarity of the cornea, in the same way as the eye sees. Then, raise the cup to the location of the water.
Then press it downwards repeatedly. If the water descends immediately, the patient will see what was previously hidden to his sight, the cup inside his eye.  Then allow a brief rest. If the water rises, lower it again without removing the cup.
If it settles and does not rise, remove the cup gently, rotating your hand slightly.  Then dissolve a little pure, finely ground rock salt in water, and wash the inside of the eye with it. Then apply a moistened compress or wool to the entire outside of the eye.
Apply rosewater and egg white, and bandage the healthy eye. As for us and those of our time, we use pounded cumin mixed with egg white.  If the cup does not enter the eye due to its hardness (some people have very hard eyes). One should take the lancet called the *bidr*, which is of this shape/form.
Pierce only the affected part with it, do not excessively enlarge the hole.  Rather, lightly pierce the area around the cup. Then insert the cup as previously described. Once completed, prepare a firm, dark bed for the patient to lie on his back.
He must refrain from all movement and coughing.  His diet should be something that softens his nature, and he should not move his head right or left at all. Keep the bandage in place for three days.  Then remove it in the same dark room and test his eyesight.
Show him objects, then replace the bandage for seven days.  Do not do this during the treatment or after the cup is applied at your time, but rather, refrain from it before the water rises quickly due to intense observation.
If a hot swelling appears, then the eye should be unbandaged before the seventh day and treated with something that relieves the swelling. Once it subsides, the patient's vision should be released. Place a cloth over his face so he trains his vision from beneath it for several days in the dark room.  Then...
He emerges from the room gradually and attends to his affairs.  Know that the learner cannot dispense with observing the cup repeatedly before proceeding with the work.  I have heard some Iraqis claim that they make a hollowed-out cup in Iraq that sucks up water.
I have not seen anyone in our country make that, nor have I read of it in any book of the ancients. It may be a recent innovation.  Here is an illustration of various types of cups, so he may understand them due to their lack of familiarity.
They are made of copper, especially, and their tip has precisely this fineness, a triangular, sharp point.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
Concerning the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
Various superfluous fleshy growths may develop in the nose, some resembling scorpions with many legs, others cancerous, hard, and dark-colored, and still others soft and not dark-colored.
If these growths are soft, not malignant or cancerous, the patient should sit facing the sun, open their nostril, insert the forceps into the flesh, pull it out, and then cut off what has been grasped with a gentle, sharp scalpel, from a single side.
Until you know that all the flesh has gone. If any remains that you cannot cut off, peel it away with one of the gentler instruments gently, so that nothing remains. If blood abounds or a hot swelling appears.
If it was a malignant tumor, then promptly cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh disappears. Then, after cutting, apply vinegar and water or a drink to the nose, so that the nose opens and moisture drains into the throat.
Then know that he is healed. If the moisture does not drain properly, know that there is a growing flesh inside the porous bones, which the instrument has not reached with the cutting. In that case, you should take a thread of thick linen.
Tie many knots in it, making each knot the size of a finger or smaller.  The patient should manipulate the end of the thread into their nose using a probe or whatever means possible, making it like a button, and pull it gently until it reaches the nostrils.
It will emerge at his throat.  Children often do a similar action in schools. This is an easy procedure for those who want to do it.  Then, gather the two ends of the thread—the one that came out of the mouth and the other that remained in the nose.
Then use the knots in the thread to dissect the flesh, continuing until you know that the flesh has been severed by the knots. Then remove the thread.  Place the thread in the nose, after wiping away the blood, and treat it with an Egyptian salve. Do this for three days or more.
Until the salve eats away any remaining flesh.  Then, finally, a lead tube is placed in the nose for several days until healed.  If further drying treatment is needed, use that.
This is an image of the instrument used to instill ointments and medicines into the nose.
A small, open, ointment-containing lamp-like device, made of silver or brass, is used.  Its channel is also open.
If you wish, you can make the tube closed, like a reed, and the applicator's ointment container flat.
It has a handle at the end, as you see, to hold it when heating ointments or other liquid substances in it.
Chapter Twenty-five.
Concerning warts growing on the tip of the nose.
Warts often grow on the tip of the nose, increasing in size and number over time until their appearance becomes unsightly.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut it off at its first appearance and completely remove it. Then apply either cauterization or a burning medicine in place of cauterization to the area.  If you delay cutting it off until it grows large, then if it is hard, stony, dark-colored, and insensitive, look into it further.
Do not use iron on it, for it is a cancerous tumor. I have often seen that cutting this tumor has resulted in great harm for its sufferer. If the tumor is soft to the touch, not dark-colored, and you see that cutting all of it is possible, then... Exhauste the patient and cut it without hesitation or delay. Treat the area with what dries and contracts until healed.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On the suturing of the nose, lip, and ear. If its connection is separated by a wound or similar, know that if a separation of connection occurs in one of these cartilages, action is rarely successful, except in some individuals.
Therefore, if any of this occurs, examine if the wound is fresh with blood. If so, bring the edges of the wound together with sutures and then treat it until healed. If the separated connection has separated its edges, and each section is sound, then it is necessary to remove the skin of each section, down to the visible layer until it bleeds.
Then bring the edges together with sutures, tighten them, and dust them with powdered alum and frankincense. Place a plaster of palm ointment or other adhesive ointment on top. Leave it tightened for two or three days, then remove it.
Then change the medicine and leave it until the stitches come out by themselves. Then treat it with the ointment until healed. The method of suturing is to bring the separated connection together, either with needles as we described in suturing the abdomen, or with thread as I explained to you there.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On removing the knots that appear on the lips.
Many people may develop small, hard tumors inside their lips, some resembling carbuncles, others smaller or larger.  It is necessary to turn back the lip, and incise each knot, and remove it with forceps.
Cut it from all sides, then fill the area after cutting with powdered alum until the bleeding stops.  Then rinse with vinegar and salt. Treat the affected areas with something astringent until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On cutting excess flesh from the gums.
Excess flesh often grows on the gums, which the ancients called *apulos*.  It should be grasped with forceps or a dissector, cut at its base, and allowed to bleed. Then, powdered alum should be placed on the area.
Or one of the astringent, dried powders. If that flesh returns after treatment, it often does; cut it again and cauterize it, as it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On cleaning the teeth with iron.
Rough, unsightly scales may accumulate on the surfaces of the teeth, both inside and out, and between the gums.  They may turn black, yellow, or green, and this decay may reach the gums, making the teeth unsightly.
The patient should be seated before you, head resting on your lap.  Clean the tooth or teeth showing scales and sand-like material thoroughly until none remains.  Likewise, remove any black, green, or yellow discolorations, and clean completely.
If the initial cleaning is successful, then stop. Otherwise, repeat the cleaning process on the following day, and again on the third, until the desired result is achieved.  Be aware that different shaped tools are needed for cleaning teeth, depending on the task.
For the instruments used to clean inside the teeth are different from those used to clean outside the teeth, and those used to clean between the teeth are different yet.  These various instruments should be readily available.
Chapter Thirty.
On the extraction of teeth.
One should attempt all possible remedies for toothache before resorting to extraction, for there is no substitute for a tooth once removed, as it is a precious substance; therefore, extraction should only be considered as a last resort.
Therefore, if the patient insists on extraction, ensure that the toothache is completely resolved. Often, the patient deceives himself regarding the location of the pain, believing it to be in a healthy tooth, and extracts it.  Then the pain does not subside until the diseased tooth is extracted.  We have witnessed this repeatedly in the actions of blood-letters.
If the source of the toothache is definitively identified, one should then use a strong scalpel to cut around the tooth, loosening the gum tissue on all sides.  Then, one should move the tooth, little by little, first with the fingers, or with delicate forceps, until it begins to loosen.
Then, firmly grasp the tooth with the large forceps, holding the patient's head between your knees to ensure stability, and pull the tooth straight to avoid breaking it. If it doesn't come out, take one of the tools mentioned, gently insert it under the tooth from all sides, and move it as you did before.
If the tooth is carious or decayed, you should fill the cavity with a cloth and seal it tightly with a thin, pliable material to prevent it from splintering when you use forceps to pull it.  You should meticulously cut around the gum tissue on all sides.
Use caution to avoid breaking the tooth, as fragments left behind can become a greater affliction for the patient than the initial pain.  Beware of the actions of unskilled blood-letters, who attempt tooth extraction without using the methods we have described.
They often inflict great harm on people, the least of which is breaking the tooth and leaving the roots entirely or partially embedded. Or, the extraction might pull out parts of the jawbone, as we have seen repeatedly.  After extraction, the patient should rinse the area with a liquid such as vinegar and salt.  If bleeding occurs, which is common, powder some alum and apply it to the area.
If alum is ineffective, then use the delicate forceps, which initially move the tooth.  The forceps should have long tips and a short, thick handle to avoid bending when grasping the tooth.  This is a depiction of the larger forceps.
The handles should be thick, so that when you grasp them they do not yield or bend. The tips should be short.  They should be made of Indian iron or tempered steel, with the tips sharply pointed and interlocked, providing a strong and secure grip.
The tips can also be shaped like a file, making them extra strong and precise.
Chapter Thirty-one.
On extracting tooth roots and removing fractured jawbones.
If, when extracting a tooth, a root has broken off, you should apply a piece of cloth soaked in oil to the area for a day or two to allow it to relax, then insert the forceps or pliers. Which resemble the beak of the bird called the blerja, and this is an image of the pliers.
If it has been made like a file inside or like a scraper, and if it does not come out with these pliers, then you should drill at the root and expose all the flesh with a scalpel, then insert the instrument that resembles a small lever, of which this is a picture.
Short at the end, slightly thick, and should not be pointed so as not to break; if the root comes out with this, otherwise use these other tools, of which this is an image; the first one is triangular at the end with some thickness.
Another delicate triangular image.
And we may also use this instrument with two branches, of which this is also a picture.
And with other tools and instruments that have been previously mentioned in the inventory of the teeth, and we may also use this instrument that resembles a large hook, of which this is a picture.
Triangular, curved end, with some slight thickness so as not to break; and let it not be pointed; and know that instruments for the teeth are numerous, and so are all the other instruments; and the skillful artisan in his craft may invent tools for himself according to what is indicated by the work and the diseases themselves.
For there are diseases for which the ancients did not describe instruments, due to the variety of their types; if a bone of the jaw or of one of the bones of the mouth breaks or becomes putrid, search for it in its place with what is suitable from one of these instruments and forceps that were mentioned for the removal of roots.
And we use a file, of which this is its image.
It should have some slight thickness to hold the bone so that it does not slip until the bone is extracted, and we treat the area with suitable medications. If the bone has a slight putrefaction, clean it of its putridity and blackness until it is cleansed, then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter Thirty-two.
In the eruption of teeth growing over others.
Teeth that erupt outside their natural course disfigure the appearance, especially in women and slaves.  Therefore, one should examine if the tooth has erupted behind another tooth.
If it cannot be moved or extracted, remove it. If it is attached to another tooth, cut it off with this instrument, which has this image/form.
It resembles a small beak, made of sharp Indian steel.  Cut it over many days, due to the tooth's hardness and to avoid disturbing other teeth.  If it is projecting and firmly placed, cool it with an Indian file, which has this image/form.
It should be entirely of Indian steel, with very fine carvings, like the file used for making needles.  Cool the tooth little by little over many days, gently, so as not to dislodge and cause it to fall.  Then, smooth it afterwards and pare it with various paring tools.
And if a tooth has broken off part of itself, causing pain to the tongue when speaking, it should also be cooled until the roughness of the fracture disappears, it becomes smooth and even, causing no harm to the tongue and not impairing speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On fastening loose teeth with silver or gold thread.
If old teeth become loose and displaced due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot eat anything because of fear of loss, and if treatment with astringent medications has been ineffective.
The remedy is to tie them with gold or silver thread, gold being preferable because silver tarnishes and rots after a few days, whereas gold remains unchanged. The thread should be of medium thickness and thinness, appropriate for the space between the teeth.
The method of fastening is to take the thread, pass its bend between the two sound teeth, and then weave the ends of the thread between the loose teeth, one or more, until the weaving reaches the sound tooth on the other side.
Then, weave back to the starting point, applying gentle and skillful pressure so as not to dislodge the teeth.  Tighten the thread at the tooth roots to prevent loosening, and then cut the excess thread ends with scissors.  Gather and twist the ends together with the floss, hiding them between the sound tooth and the loose tooth.
To prevent injuring the tongue. Then leave it tightened like this for as long as it remains.  If it loosens or breaks, tighten it with another thread.  In this way, the patient will enjoy the teeth for the rest of their life.  This is the manner and method of fastening two sound teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
A single or two teeth may be repositioned and fastened as described, remaining in place.  This is performed by a skilled dentist.  Sometimes, a skilled artisan will carve bone from an ox and fashion it into the shape of a tooth, placing it in the socket from which the tooth has been lost. And it is tightened, as we said, to remain in place.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On severing the ligament that lies under the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament under the tongue may be either natural, present from birth, or accidental, resulting from a healed wound.
The procedure is to open the patient's mouth, place their head on your lap, lift their tongue, and then sever the nerve ligament transversely so that the tongue is freed. If the ligament is somewhat firm and knotted, as might be from a healed wound, then insert a probe and split it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knot is loosened.
Beware of making the cut too deep into the flesh, as this could sever an artery and cause bleeding. Afterward, the patient should gargle with rosewater or a mixture of vinegar and cold water. Then place a linen wick under the tongue, which the patient should hold in place each night.
To prevent re-adhesion. If bleeding occurs, apply powdered alum to the area. If the bleeding is severe, cauterize the area with a suitable, lens-shaped cautery iron. Then treat it with other remedies until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-five.
Removing the toad that has developed under the tongue.
A tumor resembling a small toad may form under the tongue, hindering its normal function, and sometimes growing to fill the mouth.  To treat it, the patient's mouth is opened in front of the sun, and the tumor is examined.
If it appears dark or black, hard, and insensitive to the patient, do not attempt to treat it, for it is cancer.  If it leans towards a whitish color with moisture, use a probe to pierce it, and carefully slice it open with a scalpel, and remove it from all sides.
If bleeding occurs during the procedure, apply powdered alum until it stops, then continue the procedure until the tumor is completely removed.  Afterwards, the patient should gargle with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with other appropriate treatments until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-six.
On the treatment of tonsil tumors.
Any tumors that grow in the throat may present glands inside the throat resembling those appearing externally, called tonsils. If these are treated as described in the section but do not heal, ...
If the tumor is hard, dark-colored, and insensitive, do not use the iron on it.  If it is red and thick-rooted, also do not use iron for fear of blood loss.  Instead, let it mature, either by poulticing or by spontaneous rupture.
If it is white, round, and thin-rooted, this is the one that should be cut. Before doing so, observe if its inflammatory swelling has completely subsided or has lessened somewhat.
Then, have the patient sit facing the sun, with their head resting on your lap, and their mouth open.  Take an assistant, and have them hold the patient's tongue down with an instrument shaped like this:
Make it of silver or bronze, thin like a knife.  Once the tongue is held down and the tumor is clearly visible, take a probe and insert it into the tonsil, pulling it outward as much as possible without pulling any of the membranes along with it.
Then cut it with an instrument, shaped like scissors, but with the tips curved, each tip sharp at the other, made of Indian iron or Damascene steel.
If this instrument is unavailable, then cut it with a scalpel,  like this:
Sharp on one side and blunt on the other.  After cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner. Then, after cutting, have the patient gargle with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, have the patient gargle with water in which pomegranate peels or rue leaves, or similar astringents, have been boiled, until the bleeding stops. Then treat it until it heals.  Other tumors may grow in the throat besides the tonsils; cut these as described for cutting the tonsils.
I treated a woman whose throat tumor, resembling a small lump, almost completely blocked her throat.  She could only breathe through a narrow passage and could not eat or drink.
She was close to death if she had lived another day or two. Two branches had grown from the tumor and protruded out of her nostrils. I quickly inserted a hook into one and pulled it out. A piece was detached.
Then I cut it where I could reach it from the nostril.  I did the same with the other protruding part.  Then I opened her mouth, pressed her tongue down, and inserted the hook into the tumor itself.  I cut off some of it and only a little blood came out.
The woman's throat opened up, and she immediately began drinking water and eating.  We repeatedly cut away from that tumor over a long period, and it kept growing back.  This went on for a long time for both of us.
I cauterized the tumor inside her throat, which stopped it from growing further.  Then I left, and I do not know what happened to her after I left.
Chapter Thirty-seven.
In cutting a tumor of the larynx.
If a tumor descends to the larynx, and swells, and is white and oblong, it is called a column.  If it is thick at the bottom and round, it is called an uva.  If treated with what we have described in the classification, and the treatment is unsuccessful.
If a hot tumor has subsided and is thin, it should be cut.  If it is a mass that is round and has no length, and is reddish, or the color of blood, or dark, or does not cause sensation, it should not be cut.
In this, there is danger for the patient.  Therefore, if you see it with the characteristics we mentioned, of whiteness and length, the patient should sit facing the sun, press their tongue with the instrument whose description was previously given, then insert the snare into the uva.
Pull it downwards and cut it with one of the two instruments that I mentioned in cutting the tonsils.  Only that which exceeds the natural measure should be cut, and no more, for if you cut more, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then, after the cut, use what we described in the cutting of the tonsils and treat it until it heals. If the patient hesitates to have it cut, use the cauterization method without fear or caution.  The method of cauterization is with a sharp medicine, placing the patient's head in your lap.
Then, press the patient's tongue with the instrument we mentioned, then take the sharp water mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, mix it with quicklime that is not extinguished, and make it neither thick nor thin. Fill the concavity of this instrument with it.  This is its form/image:.
Its end, where you place the medicine, should have a concavity like the concavity of a marble spoon. Place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself. The patient should be lying on their side so that saliva flows from their mouth from the inside of their throat, to prevent anything from descending into their throat and harming them.
Then hold the medicine in your hand, pressing it against the uvula for about half an hour, until you see it has blackened and the sting of the medicine has subsided.  If you wish, take a piece of cotton, wrap it around the end of a marble spoon, and soak the cotton in the medicine.
Insert the marble spoon with the cotton into the tube from above, so that the cotton adheres to the uvula.  Do this repeatedly until you achieve the desired cauterization of the uvula. Then leave it; it will wither and fall off after three or four days. If you need to repeat the application of the medicine, repeat it.
After the cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in ghee, and remove any remaining medicine from the surrounding area.  Then, rinse with cool water, and treat externally with ointments and internally with gargles until healed. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and less frightening.
The uvula can also be treated with something gentler than cauterization or cutting, using incense in this manner:  Take frankincense, saffron, sage, wormwood, chiretta, chamomile, and henna, or similar herbs, gathering them all or some of them in a pot and submerging them in vinegar.
Bring it to a boil, with the pot covered in earthenware.  A small hole is created in the center of the lid, through which a hollow device, crafted from silver or copper, is fitted.
Insert the end with the pomegranate into the patient's mouth so that the steam rises to the uvula through the tube, causing the uvula to contract. Repeat this several times until it withers.  Avoid using this treatment during the initial stages of the swelling.
For it often aggravates the swelling. Instead, it should be performed when the heat of the swelling subsides.  If this device is unavailable, take a reed and fit a hard-boiled egg shell to its end to prevent burning the patient's mouth.
Because the eggshell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth.  This is a good and safe treatment.
Chapter Thirty-eight.
On removing thorns and other foreign objects from the throat.
Thorns, fish bones, or other objects frequently lodge in the throat.  One should remove any visible ones with the aid of an instrument, pressing the tongue down with it, under the light of the sun.
To reveal what is in the throat.  If something is not visible and hidden in the throat, the patient should induce vomiting before their food has digested in the stomach.  Perhaps the foreign object will be expelled by vomiting, or the patient will swallow a piece of linen or a piece of lettuce.
Or, the patient might swallow a piece of dry bread, or a soft piece of sea sponge is tied to a thread and swallowed.  When it reaches the thorn, quickly pull the thread repeatedly.
Often, the thorn or bone adheres to it and is removed.  If it does not come out by the methods mentioned, use a lead instrument like this one:
It is slightly thicker than a stylus.  At its tip, there is a bend. The patient, with their head raised, gently inserts it into their throat, avoiding touching their larynx to prevent coughing.  This pushes the bone or thorn out.  Or the physician inserts it with their hand.
The patient's insertion is better, as they know the location of the foreign object.  Pushing it down or pulling up with the instrument, all this is done as much as possible until it is removed.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
Removing leeches lodged in the throat.
If treatment with the medications described in the previous section proves ineffective, then examine the patient's throat in the sunlight after depressing the tongue with the instrument described.
If you see the leech, pull it out with a small hook or a gentle, firm forceps.  If that is unsuccessful, take a hollow tube, insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, and then insert a heated, protected iron rod into the tube.
Repeat this several times.  The patient should abstain from drinking water all day.  Then, the patient should take a jar filled with cool water, open their mouth over it, rinse, and not swallow a drop.  The patient should move the water with their hand from time to time.
The leech will fall off if it senses the water.  If it does not come out by the methods described, fumigate the throat with urine and halit (a specific herb) using the instrument described for fumigation of the larynx.  Do this several times and the leech will fall off.
The method for fumigation is to take a container holding embers heated by fire, and the container is covered with a lid having a hole in the center.  Insert the end of the instrument into this hole.  Place the incense in the container and have the patient place their mouth at the end of the tube, closing their mouth to prevent the incense from escaping until the patient knows it has reached their throat.
The leech will fall off at this point.  If it does not, repeat the fumigation several times.  The patient must endure thirst and eat salty foods and garlic, but not drink any water.  The leech must come out with this treatment.  This is the image of an instrument for pulling out a leech from the throat if it is spotted.
It resembles tongs, as you see, but it has this bend which enters the throat. Its ends resemble the beak of a bird, with the roughness of a file; if it grasps something, it will not let go.
Another instrument is used to press down the tongue. It is a wide iron ring, large enough to encompass the mouth over the tongue. It is inserted into the mouth standing on its side, keeping the mouth open against the patient's will. The patient cannot remove it at all until it is removed.
Description of the instrument for opening the mouth in this exact manner.
Chapter Forty.
It contains discussion on the incision and lancing of tumors.
Tumors are numerous and varied, as will be described one by one in this book.  They differ in their incision and lancing in two ways: one, according to the nature of the tumor itself and the humors it contains.
And the second way is according to the locations in the body where they occur, because a tumor in the head is different from a tumor in the buttocks, and a tumor in a fleshy area is different from a tumor in a joint; each has its own treatment.
Some tumors should not be incised until the pus has matured and is fully formed, while others should be incised when they are still immature, such as tumors near joints.
Because if a tumor near a joint grows and infects the surrounding tissues, it may damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing the limb to be useless, or the tumor might be close to a major organ.
Because if you delay incision until it matures, you will harm the major organ, or if it is near the buttocks, you should incise it while still immature.  You should incise a tumor that is immature and near the buttocks, lest it ulcerate deeply and penetrate into the anus, causing a fistula.
And it becomes incurable to a degree.  You should know the time to incise tumors that have fully matured: this is when the tumor pain subsides, the fever disappears, redness and pulsation decrease, the tumor head softens, and other signs improve.
The incision should be made at the lowest point of the tumor, if possible, to allow easier drainage downwards, or at the thinnest, most prominent part of the tumor.  If the tumors are on the hands or feet, the incision should be along the length of the limb.
The incision should be made in the muscle, tendon, nerve, and artery areas; in general, in all flat, unyielding areas.  However, for areas that bend, the incision should follow the contour of the area.  If the tumor is in a fleshy area, it is best to leave it alone.
Until its maturation is complete, as we have said. If you incise it before then, the pus will flow excessively, causing much harm and filth, and the edges of the wound might become hard and deep. Some tumors may be incised across the body in an emergency, or according to the needs of the limb.
Small tumors should be incised once; large tumors, with a wide incision or many incisions, in proportion to the size of the tumor. Some tumors may need the skin to be peeled or cut if it has become like a tattered cloth.
Some have become somewhat dead, like what occurs in many boils and abscesses. Some are incised with a three-angled cut, and some are cut off in the shape of a rue leaf, like a tumor of the groin.
Some require a round or crescent-shaped incision, or similar cuts. Tumors without a head, such as flat, smooth tumors, should only be incised simply. If the tumor is large, has accumulated for a long time, and you have incised it, do not rush to drain all the pus at once.
Instead, drain some of it, then compress the tumor for another day, and then drain some more pus. Repeat this gradually until all is discharged.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a young child, or an old person, the animal spirit is often weakened by the sudden discharge of pus; the patient might die without you realizing it. Guard against this consequence with the utmost care.
After incising these tumors, you should cleanse the wound and examine it.  If the tumor wound is small or the incision single and simple, use a bandage of linen or old cotton.  If the tumor is large and the incisions numerous,
then you should insert a thread in each incision, so that some threads meet each other.  If the tumor has peeled away or eroded the skin, you should pack it with old cotton or linen strips, without moisture, and keep it compressed for three days.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until healed. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar, after which you have soaked a linen cloth in them and apply it to the bleeding area several times.
If the bleeding persists, you should use the remedies and treatments and cauterizations that we have described in many places in this book and in the classifications. If it is winter and the tumor site is heavily innervated, you should soak the dressings in a warm drink and oil.
and place them on the area. If it is summer and the areas are fleshy, you should apply the dressings soaked in water and oil, or in a cold drink and oil.  And when the third day arrives, as we said, you should dissolve the tumor and cleanse it.
And use similar treatments until healed. This is what you need to know about treating tumors in general.  As for the detailed methods, each tumor's treatment is described in a summarized, categorized manner.
Chapter Forty-one.
Concerning the incision on tumors appearing on the scalp.
Small tumors appear on the scalp, which are a type of swelling within sheaths. They have appearances like a hen's bladder, and their types are numerous.  Some are fatty, and others contain a moisture like mud.
Some contain a moisture like broth or soup, and others are stony, hard.  All of these pose no danger in their incision and removal, unless an artery is encountered during the incision.
The procedure for incision is to first probe it with the instrument whose image will follow, called the *muds*, so that you know what it contains. If it contains moisture, then cut it lengthwise with a simple incision like this:
Begin the incision from line ~b~ to line ~c~.  If the moisture is released, peel off the sac containing the moisture and cut it entirely, leaving nothing at all.  Often, it will return if any remains.
Then dip a cotton swab into the astringent ointment, if available, or into salt water, and fill the wound with it. Leave it until the next day, as it will consume any remaining sac material.  Then reapply the cotton swab with the astringent a second and third time if necessary.
Until you are certain that no part of the sac remains.  Then treat the wound with ointments until it heals. If the swelling contains fatty material, cut it in this manner:
Cut it with a sharp incision, insert forceps into the wound edges, and peel it off from all sides.  Exert all your effort in removing the peritoneum that contains it. If an artery intervenes, do what I have previously described, and do the same for the swelling if it is hardened from the incision.
The treatment is the same as previously mentioned.  The incision on a hardened swelling is easier because it has little blood and moisture. Some of these swellings in the head in some people have no moisture at all.  This is because I incised a swelling in...
the head of an old woman and found the swelling like hard, rough, white stone.  It could not be broken, even if someone threw it, it would only shatter.  The other swellings that occur in the heads of children are not like this.
At the roots of the ears, make a simple incision in all of them, and make the opening at the bottom so that the material flows downwards. Then treat them with the appropriate treatment.
Chapter Forty-two.
Concerning incisions on swine-like tumors that often appear on the neck, under the armpits, and in the groin.  They may appear on other parts of the body.  Those on the neck may be single or numerous, and some may develop from others.
Each such tumor is contained within its own sac, as is the case with tumors of the head and swellings of the merchandise, as we have described.  These swine-like tumors are numerous, some hard, some containing moisture, and some malignant, unresponsive to treatment.
Of those I observed, those favorable to touch, having a surface close to the color of the skin, movable in every direction, and not adhered to the neck's nerves, veins, or arteries, and not sunken. It is necessary to make a simple incision from top to bottom of the body, along the line from ~J~ to ~B~.
Peel it away from all sides and stretch the edges of the skin with a hook or two, or several hooks if necessary, as we mentioned regarding tumors of the head.  Remove it little by little, being careful not to cut a vein or nerve.  The scalpel should not be excessively sharp.
Lest your hand increase the cutting or distress the patient, and you cut what you do not need to cut. If you cut a vein or artery and are hindered from your work, place powdered styptic or some astringents that stop bleeding into the wound.
Close the wound and leave it until the inflammation subsides, the wound relaxes, and it is close to festering. The bleeding will stop at that point. Resume your work until you are finished. Then, examine with your index finger if there are any remaining, then small, further pieces of flesh, cut and remove them.
If there is a large vein at the base of the piece of flesh, you should not cut that piece of flesh from its base, but you should tie it tightly with a doubled thread and leave it until it falls away on its own without harm.  Then fill the wound with used cotton that has been dipped in Egyptian ointment and then treat it.
If you cut the entire piece of flesh, you should bring together the edges of the wound and stitch it immediately, after ensuring that there are no remaining fragments. If you see that a fragment of the piece of flesh's base remains, you must take this into account.
