You should aim for the base and root of the flesh when cutting, and use the stitching and what we have mentioned. For pieces of flesh containing moisture, apply a simple compression where the ripening location is evident, and make the compression from below as we have said.
Then, after the compression, apply ointment, such as Egyptian ointment, to consume any remaining corruption.  Once you see the wound is clean, treat it with flesh-growing ointments until it heals.
Chapter Forty-three.
On the incision of the throat for a tumor occurring within the throat.
The ancients have described this incision of the throat, but I have not seen anyone in our country perform it. This is in accordance with their words.  However, those with sore throats should avoid incision of the throat, as it will not benefit them.
In order that all the tumors and the lung might be diseased, and for those with a hot tumor in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, if there is no ailment in the trachea, then incision of the throat is necessary to escape the harm of suffocation.
Therefore, if we wish to do so, we should incise the throat below three rings of the trachea, or make four small transverse incisions between two rings, to the extent of an incision in the pleura, not in the cartilage. This location is suitable for the incision.
Because it is without flesh and its blood vessels are distant, if the surgeon is timid, then he should stretch the throat's skin with a hook, and then incise the skin until, when he reaches the trachea, he should avoid the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should incise the pleura which we have described, and he should find evidence of incising the trachea from the phlegm that comes out of it along with whatever is protruding, and from the cessation of voice.  He should leave the wound open for a time, and when the period of fear of suffocation has passed, he should bring together the edges of the wound from the skin.
And he should dissect it alone, without the cartilage.  Then the medicines that promote flesh growth are used until healing occurs.  The author of this book says, "The meaning of this explanation that we have described is that if they see the patient whose throat has been blocked by one of these tumors that they have mentioned.
When the patient was near death, and he was about to die, they went to incising the throat so that the patient might breathe through the wound, and be saved from death.  However, they were instructed to leave the wound open until the illness is completely resolved, and the duration of the illness is about three days, or similar.
Then they ordered the wound to be stitched and treated until it healed.  What I personally witnessed was a servant who took a knife and inflicted a cut on her throat, severing a portion of the lung's trachea.  I was called to treat her, and I found her weakening as if she was about to die.  I examined the wound.
I found that the blood coming from the wound was minimal, so I concluded that she had not severed any vein or artery.  Air was escaping from the wound, so I immediately sutured and treated the wound until she healed.  The servant experienced no further problem, except for a slight hoarseness in her voice.
And after a few days, she returned to her best state.  Therefore, we say that there is no danger in incising the throat.
Chapter Forty-Four.
Concerning the incision on a tumor that appears on the larynx from the outside, and is called a laryngeal tumor.
This tumor, called a laryngeal tumor, is a large tumor the color of the body, common in women, and comes in two types.
Either it is natural or accidental; the natural one is incurable, while the accidental one is of two types: one resembling fatty tumors, and the other resembling tumors that arise from arterial complications, and there is danger in incising it. Therefore, one should not use the knife on it at all, unless it is small. If you probe and examine it with a probe and find that it resembles a fatty tumor and is not connected to any blood vessels, then incise it as you would a tumor. Remove it with its contained sac if it is in a sac; otherwise, remove all of it, and then treat the area with the appropriate treatment.
Chapter Forty-five.
On incision of various tumors.
Tumors are numerous in type, and all types are mentioned in the classification.  It is important here to explain the difference between a tumor and an abscess, as it is problematic.  I say that an abscess presents with heat, fever, and fearful pains until the swelling subsides. As the pus matures, the fever and severity subside.  A tumor presents with neither heat, fever, nor pain, and it is contained within a peritoneal sac, a specific enclosure.  Its color is the same as the surrounding tissue; its beginning is like a boil, and it grows into a larger or smaller cyst.
They are of two types: either fatty or containing moisture.  The colors of the moisture are many, as mentioned in the classification.  Therefore, when treating a tumor, one should first probe and examine it with an instrument called a probe, as its image is found in the next chapter.
Assess the size of the tumor, then withdraw the probe and observe what follows. If a flowing moisture of any color is expelled, make a simple incision, as described for other tumors. If no moisture is expelled after probing, know that it is fatty.
Make a firm incision on it in this manner, as I have taught you, and suspend it by means of hooks. Carefully separate the skin from all sides, and if possible, remove the sac containing the tumor intact.
If the sac breaks during the procedure and you cannot remove it intact, which often happens, remove it piece by piece until nothing remains, for if any part remains, large or small, the tumor will likely return.
If you are unsuccessful and only a small portion remains, cauterize the wound with sharp cauterizing materials after completion of the procedure.  Apply compression to the wound and apply something to soothe any inflammation, and treat it with the usual treatment until healed. If the tumor is large, suture the edges and treat it with what will cause healing.
If you encounter a bleeding vessel, whether pulsating or not, immediately apply powdered alum to the affected area. Leave it compressed for two or three days until the wound has suppurated and the blood flow subsides, then proceed with the removal of any remaining tumor tissue.

Chapter Forty-seven.
In the treatment of the male breast.
The breasts of some people, resembling those of women, may swell during puberty, becoming unsightly and inflamed.  If someone dislikes this, the breast should be incised with a crescent-shaped cut, like this.
From line ~B~ to line ~C~. Then, remove all the fat.  Then, fill the wound with the medicated ointment.  Then, close the edges of the wound with stitches and treat it until healed. If the breast sags and relaxes due to bone weakness, as is common in women.
Then, incisions should be made on its upper sides, two incisions shaped like crescents, each touching the other at their ends, so that the larger line surrounds the smaller one, in this manner. From line ~B~ to line ~C~, then the skin between the incisions is peeled away, the fat removed, and the previously mentioned sutures, cauterization, and other treatments are applied until healed.
If you are unable to excise what you should excise, due to the patient's distress or excessive bleeding, then you should pack the wound with cotton soaked in caustic ointment and leave it until it consumes any remaining fat.  Then, treat it until it heals.
Chapter Forty-eight.
On the treatment of tumors appearing under the armpit.
These tumors that appear under the armpit are of the pig-like type, hard and encapsulated.  Some contain moisture.  Those containing moisture should be incised with a crescent-shaped cut, like this.
From line ~B~ to line ~C~.  If all the matter in the tumor has been discharged, pack it with used cotton and leave it until the next day, then remove the cotton.
And treat it with the appropriate ointment until healed. If the tumor is of the knot-like pig type, incise it as we described for pig tumors. If the wound extends to pus formation, use cauterization as previously mentioned.
Chapter forty-nine.
On the incision of a tumor arising from an artery or vein, known as an aneurysm.
If an artery is wounded and the skin above it adheres, a tumor often arises from it.  Similarly, a vein may also develop a swelling and tumor.
The signs by which one recognizes whether a tumor and swelling originate from an artery or a vein are as follows: if from an artery, the tumor is oblong, clustered deep within the body, and when you press it with your finger, you feel a crackling sensation.
And that which is from a vein has a round tumor on the surface of the body. Incisions on these tumors are dangerous, especially those in the armpit, groin, neck, and many other parts of the body, and if very large, one should avoid treating them with iron.
Also, those in the limbs or head should be avoided.  For those that are swellings of the artery's mouth, make a longitudinal incision in the skin, then open the incision with hooks.  Then, dissect and free the artery from the membranes.
Until it is exposed.  Then insert a needle beneath it, piercing to the other side and tying the artery with a doubled thread at two places, as I explained to you in the section on the arteries in the temples. Then, puncture with a lancet the area between the ligaments until all the blood within is released.
The swelling will then subside, and then use the treatment that causes suppuration until the ligaments fall away. Then treat with the appropriate ointments until healed.
If the swelling is from a vein rupture, you should grasp as much of the swelling with the skin as possible. Then, insert a needle below the area you have grasped, piercing through it, with a doubled thread attached, bringing it out from the other side. Then tie the swelling securely, as I explained to you in tying the hernia on both sides with the ends of the thread.  If you fear the threads will slip, insert another needle with a separate thread under the entire swelling, where the first needle intersects.
Tie your threads in four places, then cut the swelling in the middle.  Once what is within has come out, cut off the excess skin and leave what was tied.  Then place a poultice, dipped in a potion and oil, upon it. Then use the treatment of twisting and ointments until healed.
Chapter Fifty.
In the swelling that arises from nerve twisting.
As swelling occurs in arteries and veins, so too does swelling occur in the nerve if a blow is sustained or from excessive exertion, or similar causes.  This is more common in the wrists and ankles.
And in every place where the joints move, it is a hard swelling, similar in color to the rest of the body.  It is often painless, and if pressed hard, the patient feels a sensation resembling numbness. The swelling is not deep within the body.
Rather, it is beneath the skin, moving in every direction, and not forward or backward.  Any swelling in the joints should not be treated with iron, as it may cause a wound.  Any found in the head or forehead...
Cut the skin with a scalpel. If the swelling is small, grasp it with forceps and cut it from the root. If it is large, suspend it with a hook, scrape it, then remove it. Close the wound with stitches and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-one.
On the removal of warts appearing on the abdomen.
Warts, called "fungal" because of their resemblance to fungi, often appear on the abdomens and other parts of the body of some people.  Their base is thin, and their head is thick; sometimes their lips are turned outward.  They can be small or very large.
I once observed a man with two warts on his abdomen, resembling fungi in every way.  Their thin, whitish base had turned outward and cracked, with moisture constantly dripping from them.  I removed them, one weighing approximately eighteen ounces and the other about six ounces.
When removing them, if the affected area is moist and the wart's color is a moist white, with a thin base, cut it with a broad scalpel.  Have a cautery iron ready, as considerable bleeding often occurs during removal.
If bleeding becomes excessive, cauterize the area. If the affected area is sensitive and resists cutting with a knife, take a tightly wound lead thread and bind the wart. Leave it for two days, then increase the tightness of the lead thread, tightening it continuously.
As you tighten it, eventually the wart will break and fall off by itself without further effort.  If the wart has a thick base, there is some risk and uncertainty in cutting it, particularly if it is located on the abdomen.  However, it may be possible to cut off half or a portion of it.
Then cauterize it to prevent recurrence, and treat the wound until it heals.  Beware of cutting a wart that is dark-colored, insensitive, and dull-looking, as this could be a cancerous tumor.  We will discuss cancer after this.
The Fifty-second Chapter.
On treating umbilical protrusion.
Umbilical protrusion arises from various causes: either from a rupture of the peritoneum lining the abdomen, allowing abdominal fluids or intestines to protrude, as occurs in other hernias; or from blood emanating from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned; or from trapped air.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum and the leakage of abdominal fluids, the tumor will be the same color as the body, soft and painless, and will exhibit varying positions. If it is due to the protrusion of the intestines, its position will be even more varied than described.  When pressed with your fingers, it will disappear and then reappear, possibly accompanied by gurgling. It will greatly enlarge during bathing and strenuous exertion.  If it is due to moisture, it will be soft.
If pressed with your hand, it does not disappear, and does not increase or decrease.  If it is due to blood, the tumor will exhibit these signs, but will also appear dark. If it is due to the growth of flesh, the tumor will be hard, firm, and fixed in place.
If it is due to air, its touch will be soft.  The procedure in this case is to observe.  If the umbilical protrusion is due to blood from an artery or vein, or air, treatment should be avoided, as there is danger and deception involved, as I explained in the chapter on tumors caused by arteries and veins.
If the umbilical protrusion is due to the intestines or abdominal fluid, instruct the patient to hold himself still and stand upright, stretched out.  Then, draw a circle of lead around the entire navel. Then, instruct him to lie on his back before you.  Then, with a wide scalpel, make a cut around the navel, at the location marked by the lead.  Then, with a large hook, lift the center of the tumor upwards.  Then, tie the incision site tightly with a strong thread or silk tendon.
The knot of the bandage should be a figure-eight.  Then, open the center of the pulled tumor above the bandage and insert your index finger.  Search for the intestines. If the bandage has caught the intestines, loosen the figure-eight knot and push the intestines back into the abdomen. If it is fluid, extend it with a hook.
Cut off any excess. If an artery or vein is encountered, tie it securely and return to your work. Take two needles; thread two strong threads through them and insert the needles into the incision you made around the tumor, placing them firmly into the incision.  Then pull the tumor in four places at the needles.
If you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the pulled flesh rots and falls off on its own, or cut it when it becomes thin and rotten.  Then treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If the umbilical protrusion is from flesh that has grown there.
Or if it is from moisture, you should cauterize the tumor, as we said, and remove the blood or moisture found there, then treat it with what will heal the area.
Chapter Fifty-three.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have previously described the types of cancer and how to treat it with medications, warning against using iron to treat it lest it ulcerate. We also described cancer originating in the uterus, and warned against treating it.
The ancients said that if the cancer is in a place where it can be completely removed, such as cancer in the breast or thigh, and similar organs suitable for complete removal, especially if it is small and newly formed, then it should be completely removed.  However, if it is advanced and large, it should not be approached.
I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me achieve this.  If the cancer is localized, as we mentioned, proceed to draw blood from the patient, repeating this several times, then cauterize it if there is obvious fullness in the veins.
Then, apply a compress to the affected area, allowing the treatment to take effect.  Next, insert appropriate hooks into the cancer and scrape it from all sides along with the skin, completely removing all traces of its roots.  Allow the blood to flow, and do not cut it quickly.
Instead, press on the affected areas and remove all the thick blood with your hand or any available tools. If your work causes a large blood loss due to a severed artery or vein, cauterize the affected vein until the bleeding stops. Then, treat it with further treatment until it heals.
Chapter Forty-five.
On the treatment of tumors.
We have previously informed you on the classifications of dropsy, how water accumulates, the signs of each type, and its treatment with medicines.  Only the *zaqqi* type of dropsy is treated with iron; the *tabbi* type and the fleshy type are never treated with iron.
If you have treated this *zaqqi* type of tumor with medicines and your treatment has been ineffective, examine whether the patient has become extremely weak or has another illness besides the tumor, such as...
If the patient has a cough, diarrhea, or similar ailments, then avoid treating it with iron, for it is dangerous.  If you see a strong patient, with no illness besides the tumor, who is neither a child nor an elder, then proceed as follows: position the patient standing before you. And a servant behind him should squeeze his abdomen with their hands, pushing the water downward towards the pubic area. Then, take a pointed scalpel in this manner:
Its ends are narrow, elongated, and pointed like a scalpel, but with a slight curvature to avoid entering the intestines during use and causing harm. Then, observe whether the tumor originated from the intestines.
If the tumor originated from the intestines, the incision should be three fingers below the navel, next to the pubic bone. If the tumor originated from liver disease, the incision should be three fingers to the left of the navel. If it originated from spleen disease, the incision should be three fingers to the right of the navel.
Also, the incision should not be on the side the patient intends to lie on, to prevent fluids from accumulating in that weakened area.  Then, pierce the entire skin with the instrument, and insert the instrument into the incision, then raise your hand.
Using the scalpel, separate the skin from the peritoneum as if you were peeling it, to a depth roughly the size of a fingernail. Then, pierce the peritoneum until the scalpel reaches an empty space, the location of the fluid. Remove the fluid, and insert the instrument, which looks like this...
It is like a thin tube made of silver, copper, or polished leather.  At its base is a small hole, and on its sides are three smaller holes; two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
A pointed probe, resembling a pen's tip, may be placed on the end, like this.
At its upper end is a ring.  If the instrument reaches the fluid, it will drain immediately onto the instrument.  Drain a moderate amount of fluid, because draining more than necessary in a short time may cause the patient's death due to the disintegration of the vital spirit.
Or cause a near-death faintness.  However, drain only what his strength allows, guided by the patient's pulse and complexion. Then, remove the instrument and seal the fluid, as it will retain itself immediately.
Because of the skin covering the opening in the peritoneum, which you were instructed to apply in that manner. Then, repeat the procedure on another day, if you deem the patient suitable for this.
Also withdraw a small amount of fluid.  Do this for several days, while maintaining vigilance to avoid mistakes so that only a small amount of fluid remains.  If you fear for the patient's health and leave a large amount of fluid, treat with hot sand burial.
Excessive sweating in the bath and sun, enduring thirst, and treatment with dried medicines until recovery.  Cauterize the stomach, liver, and spleen after removing the fluid as described above.

Chapter Fifty-six.
Concerning the sores that appear on the foreskin, urethra, and dark discoloration, decay, and adhesion of the foreskin to the urethra.
This sore often appears on the urethra, as a dark, fleshy growth.  It can be malignant or benign.  A benign growth should be treated with a gentle snare.
Cut it completely until it is cleansed, then apply a cotton swab dipped in Egyptian ointment.  Afterwards, treat it with palm ointment until healed. However, if the sore is malignant, dark-colored, then cauterization must be used after cutting and removing the affected area.
If the sore is on the foreskin of an uncircumcised man, and some of the sore is inside the foreskin and some outside, then the sore inside must be removed first. Once it has healed, then treat the external sore.
Because if you treat both simultaneously, you risk perforating the foreskin.  This can also occur in women, and blackening and decay can appear in the foreskin.  Therefore, you must cauterize any blackened areas that may decay or have already decayed.
Then apply honey, crushed and sifted pomegranate rind, and carson to the affected area.  Afterwards, treat it with the usual remedies until healed.  If bleeding occurs, use a crescent-shaped cauterizing iron in this manner:
Cauterization is beneficial for both conditions, namely, bleeding and decay of the wound. If the foreskin is completely destroyed at some point, a lead tube should be inserted into the urethra to allow urination. Regarding the foreskin adhering to the shaft...
This adhesion occurs in those whose foreskin is healthy but who have not been circumcised, and it may also occur from a wound or tumor.  The foreskin should be separated with a sharp scalpel until the bond is broken and the shaft is freed on all sides.
If it is difficult to distinguish them upon examination, a small portion of both the shaft and foreskin should be separated.  The foreskin is thin and may easily tear.  Separate the foreskin from the shaft with a thin, damp linen cloth to prevent further adhesion.
Then treat with a astringent drink until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-seven.
On the purification of boys and the treatment of errors affecting them.
Circumcision is nothing more than separating a connection, like other surgical procedures, but since it is a procedure performed by us with intent, and is particularly used in boys, it is necessary to design the best possible course of action for it.
The easiest path to safety, I say, is that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books because it was not used in their laws, but rather it is something we have adopted.
Through experience, I have found that the majority of practitioners, surgeons, and barbers, use purification with knives and scissors, using the cautery, ligatures, and incisions with the fingernail. I have tested all these methods.
Thus, I found nothing better than purification with scissors and ligation with thread, because purification with a knife often results in damage to the skin.  The skin of the foreskin has two layers; thus, it is possible that the upper layer of skin is cut, while the lower layer remains.
One is then forced to make another incision and suffer further pain.  Cautery does not provide security against cutting the urethral tip, because it might have entered its opening.  As for purification by fingernail, the skin might tear, and your work spoiled, or the boy's skin might be naturally thin.
Thus, many are born without needing purification, as I have seen.  As for purification by scissors and ligation with thread, experience has revealed its superiority to me, because the scissors have a balanced cut, since the blade above is like the blade below.
When you squeeze your hand with the blades balanced, the cut is uniform and made at the same time.  The thread thus becomes like a wall for the urethral skin on all sides, and no mistake occurs.  First, the procedure involves calming the child.
Especially if he is somewhat understanding, explain that you are only tying the thread to his urethra, leaving it for another day. Then, cheer and delight him in every way you can, and whatever he can comprehend.  Then, have him stand before you, erect.
Do not have him sit. Hide the scissors in your sleeve or under your foot.  Do not let the boy's eyes see them or any instruments.  Then, insert your hand into his urethra and inflate the skin, lifting it upwards until the urethral head emerges.
Then clean it of any accumulated dirt.  Then tie a doubled thread at the marked spot.  Then, tie a second thread slightly below it.  Then, using your thumb and index finger, grip the lower ligation firmly and cut between the ligatures. Then quickly lift the skin upwards and extract the urethral head.  Allow the blood to flow a little; this is better and lessens urethral swelling. Then dry it with a damp cloth, and dust it with powdered, burned dry gourd ashes; this is the best I have tried, or finely ground carob flour; it is also excellent.
Then quickly lift the skin upwards and extract the urethral head.  Allow the blood to flow a little; this is better and lessens urethral swelling. Then dry it with a damp cloth, and dust it with powdered, burned dry gourd ashes; this is the best I have tried, or finely ground carob flour; it is also excellent. Then place him on his back, using a cloth.  Place a cooked egg in rosewater, beaten with fine, fragrant rose oil, and leave it on him for another day.  Then treat him with further remedies until he is healed.
This is an image of the appropriate scissors for cleaning.
The scissors should be sharp and straight, not bent, with the head of a nail.  The blades should be the same length as the handles.  A common mistake in cleaning is that the skin that is inside may be entirely or partially turned over during the cut, so it is necessary to stretch it with your fingernail at the same time, before the affected area swells.
Cut it evenly. If you cannot hold it with your fingernail, pull it with a hook and cut it. If three days have passed and the area beneath the urethral head remains swollen, let the hot swelling subside and gently peel it off.
Cut it according to your judgment, and be careful around the urethral head. If some of the urethral head is cut, it will not be harmful. Treat it with the cauterants described in the article on cauterants.
If more skin is cut than needed and it shrinks upwards, this will not cause much harm either. Treat it as previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-eight.
On the treatment of obstructed urine in the bladder.
Obstructed urine in the bladder occurs due to blockage by a stone, coagulated blood, pus, or growing tissue, and the like.  If you treat it with the various treatments described in the classification, then [the condition] will be resolved.
If the urine has not passed and you suspect the obstruction is due to a stone damaging the neck of the bladder, the patient should be placed on their knees, then one leg should be placed on their back, and their abdomen should be pressed against their back. The patient should hold themselves in place as much as possible.
This will force the neck of the bladder to push the stone backward, allowing the urine to flow. If the urine does not flow with this method and the patient's condition worsens, then the extraction of the stone using an instrument called catheters, of which this is an image [of]:  should be employed.
Made of silver, it is thin, smooth, hollow, like a bird's feather tube in its thinness.  It is about a foot and a half long, with a gentle funnel at its head.  The method of drawing the urine with it is to take a folded thread and tie a piece of wool or cotton to one end securely.
You insert the end of the thread into the lower end of the catheter and cut off any excess wool using pliers so that it enters the tube, closing it like a button.  Then, the catheter should be lubricated with oil, butter, or egg white, and the patient sits on a chair.
His bladder and urethra are softened with moist ointments or warm water and oil.  Then, the catheter is gently inserted into the urethra until it reaches the base of the urethra.  Then, the urethra is pulled upward towards the navel, and the catheter is pushed into it.
Once the urethra is pulled, the catheter is pushed inside until it is near the anus.  The penis is then tilted downward while the catheter is inside, and pushed until it reaches the bladder, where the patient feels that it has reached an empty space.
This is done in this manner because the channel through which the urine flows is curved.  If the thread with the wool is pulled gently, the urine will follow the wool.  Then, it is withdrawn and the urine is poured out, and the catheter is reinserted.
Continue doing this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.
Chapter Fifty-nine.
How to inject the bladder with a syringe.
Illustrations of suitable instruments for this purpose are made of silver or of hollowed ivory, with a long, thin tube.  To instill water and medications, a device called a syringe is used.  Here is its image/illustration:.
Made of silver or hollowed ivory, with a long, slender tube, hollowed throughout except for the tip, which is solid and has three holes: two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
The hollowed portion where the nozzle is, should be just large enough to accommodate it without excess.  Thus, when pulling something moist, it will be pulled, and when pushing, it will be pushed outward, just as in the case of the oil-thrower used in naval warfare.
If you wish to remove moisture from the bladder, insert the tip of the syringe into the moisture and pull upwards on the nozzle.  The moisture will be drawn into the syringe.  Then, insert the tip of the syringe into the urethra, as described for catheters, and push the moisture out with the nozzle.
This moisture will reach the bladder and the patient will feel it.  This is also a picture of a delicate enema syringe, used to irrigate the bladder.
Made of silver or lead, with a head resembling a small funnel at its top and a groove below to hold the cord. Then, take a camel's bladder and place in it the moisture you wish to irrigate the bladder with. Then tie it securely between the two parts with a doubled thread.
Warm the moisture slightly over the fire. Then insert the tip of the irrigator into the urethra. Then firmly press on the bladder containing the moisture until the patient feels that the moisture has reached the bladder.
If you do not have a bladder, take a piece of thin material, make a circle from it, and pierce it all around. Then insert a strong thread through those holes as you go around. Then tie it to the instrument and use it as you did with the bladder, pressing with your hand until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter Sixty.
On removing stones.
We have previously discussed the types of stones, their treatment with medication, and the differences between stones formed in the kidneys and those formed in the bladder, along with their symptoms.  We have also described stones requiring manual removal and incision.
This refers specifically to stones formed in the bladder, and those that develop in the urethra.  I will describe this clearly and concisely.
I say that bladder stones are most often found in children.  A sign of this is that the urine exiting the bladder is thin like water.
Sand appears in it, and he rubs and plays with his penis, often hanging down.  The anus also frequently prolapses in many of them.  Children recover from it easily until they are fourteen, whereas it is difficult in the elderly; in young adults it is intermediate between the two.
Those with larger stones have easier treatment, while the opposite is true for smaller stones.  If we turn to treatment, first, the patient should be given an enema to remove all the impurities from his intestines, as this can prevent the stone from being seen during examination.
Then the patient is taken by their feet and shaken downwards to cause the stone to descend into the neck of the bladder, or he is made to jump from a high place several times.  Then, he is seated upright before you, with his hands beneath his thighs, so that the entire bladder is tilted downwards, and then examined.
Feel it from the outside. If you sense the stone in the space, immediately make an incision on it.  If it is not at all palpable, you should smear the index finger with grease from the left hand if the patient is a child, or the middle finger.
If it is a fully grown boy, insert it into his anus, and search for the stone.  Once you find it under your finger, move it little by little towards the neck of the bladder. Then press on it with your finger and push it out towards the place where you intend to make the incision.
You instruct a servant to squeeze the bladder with his hand, and another servant to stretch the vulva upwards with his right hand and the skin beneath the vulva towards the location of the incision with his left hand. Then, take the forceps, which have this form:
Make the incision between the anus and the vulva, not in the middle, but to the side of the left groin.  The incision should be directly on the stone, with your finger in the anus pressing it outwards in an oblique manner so that the incision is wide from the outside and narrow from the inside, to the extent possible, so that the stone can be removed.
Do not press too hard, for the finger in the anus might push the stone out when making the incision, which will be less difficult.  Know that some stones have angles and edges, which makes removal more difficult.
Some are smooth, like acorns, and round, making removal easier.  Those with angles and edges require a slightly wider incision. If it does not come out this way, then you must devise a strategy, either by grasping it with a firm forceps, the tip of which is like a rasp.
So that it grasps the stone firmly and does not slip.  Or, if possible, introduce a delicate, curved instrument underneath it. If you are unable to do that, widen the incision slightly. If blood starts to flow excessively, stop the bleeding with a cautery.
If there is more than one stone, remove the largest first into the bladder opening, then make an incision around it, and then remove the smaller ones afterward. Do the same if there are more than two. If the stone is very large, it is foolish to make a large incision, as this would expose the patient to one of two problems.
Either he will die, or he will experience constant dribbling of urine, because the area will never heal properly. But try to push it out.  Or, devise a strategy to break it with forceps until it comes out in pieces.
Once your work is done, dress the wound with kandar, sabr, and sheyan, applying pressure and placing a bandage soaked in oil, a drink, or rose ointment and cold water over it to soothe the inflamed area.  Then the patient should lie on their back, and do not remove the bandage for three days.
When the swelling subsides, wash the area thoroughly with water and oil, then treat it with the date palm ointment and the basilisk ointment until healed. If excessive inflammation, swelling, or other problems, such as blood clotting in the bladder that prevents urination (which will be recognized by the presence of blood in the urine), arise in the wound.
Insert your finger into the wound and remove that blood, for if it remains, it will lead to the corruption and putrefaction of the bladder. Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat each type of condition with a similar remedy until it heals.
Throughout the treatment, it is necessary to bind the thighs together to hold the medications applied to the affected area in place.  If the stone is small and lodged in the urethra, causing inflammation and blocking urine flow, treat it with the methods I've described before it progresses to a rupture.
Often, this treatment obviates the need for incision.  I have tested this method using a lancet of tempered steel, of this form:
A triangular, pointed blade is fitted to a handle.  Then, a thread is used to tie the penis below the stone, to prevent it from returning to the bladder.  Then, the lancet is gently inserted into the urethra until it reaches the stone itself.
Rotate the lancet a little at a time against the stone, aiming to pierce it through to the other side.  Urine will flow immediately.  Then, press with your hand on the remaining portion of the stone outside the penis.  It will break apart and pass with the urine, and the patient will recover.
If this treatment is not possible due to an obstacle, tie a thread below the stone and another above the stone.  Then, make an incision on the stone within the penis between the threads and remove it.
Then, release the threads and clean the clotted blood in the wound.  The thread was tied below the stone to prevent it from returning to the bladder, and the other thread was tied above it so that when the threads are released after the stone is removed, the skin will return to its place and cover the wound.
Therefore, when tying the upper thread, you should lift the skin upwards so that it returns and covers the wound, as we have said.
Chapter Sixty-one.
On removing stones in women.
Stones rarely develop in women. If one of them presents with a stone, treatment is difficult and impossible for many reasons. One reason is that the woman might be a virgin, and another that you will not find a woman who allows herself to be examined by a doctor.
If she is chaste or from among those with restrictions, a third reason is that you do not find a woman who is skilled in this craft, especially manual work. Fourth, the location of the incision for a stone in women is distant from the stone's location, requiring a deep incision.
And there is danger in that. If necessity demands it, a skilled female physician should be sought; however, such a person is rarely found. If not found, seek a trustworthy and virtuous male physician, or bring in a skilled female midwife or a woman with some knowledge of this craft.
She should be brought in and instructed to perform whatever examination is necessary for the stone.  First, if the woman is a virgin, the finger should be inserted into her vagina to search for the stone. If found, the stone should be pressed against the finger.
If she is not a virgin but a married woman, instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the woman's vagina and search for the stone, after first placing her left hand on the bladder and pressing it firmly.
If she finds it, she should guide it from the mouth of the bladder downward to the extent of her ability until it reaches the root of the thigh, and then make an incision on the corresponding side of the vagina, at the root of the thigh, from whichever direction she finds the stone in that area.
And her finger must remain on the stone, pressing it. The incision should initially be small.  Then, the probe should be inserted into the small incision.  If she feels the stone, enlarge the incision as much as necessary to allow the stone to exit.  Know that there are many types of stones.
Among them are small and large, smooth and rough, long and round, and those with protrusions.  Understand their varieties so that you can ascertain what you need. If blood flows excessively, sprinkle powdered alum on the area and hold it for a while until the bleeding stops, then return to your work.
Until the stone is removed. Prepare the instruments mentioned for removing stones in men, to aid in your work. If excessive bleeding occurs and you know the bleeding is from a severed artery, apply styptics to the area.
Bind it firmly with dressings, and leave it. Do not return to it. Leave the stone; do not remove it. Perhaps the patient will die.  Then treat the wound. Once the bleeding subsides after a few days and the area is infected, return to your work until the stone is expelled.
Chapter Sixty-two.
In the fissure of the watery fistula.
A watery fistula is the accumulation of moisture in the white peritoneum beneath the scrotum's skin surrounding the testicle.  It may also be in a special membrane, naturally formed on one side of the testicle.
It may be mistaken for another testicle and lie between the scrotal skin and the white peritoneum, as we mentioned.  This only happens rarely.  This fistula arises from weakness in females, leading to the accumulation of this material.  It may also result from a blow to the scrotum.
This moisture comes in various colors, either yellow, reddish-bloody, dark-dusky, or whitish-watery, the latter being the most common.  The signs by which it is recognized are...
Where the water collects, if it's in the white peritoneum we mentioned, the swelling is somewhat elongated and round, like an egg, and the testicle isn't visible because the moisture surrounds it completely.
If the moisture is in a separate membrane, the swelling is round on one side of the testicle.
For this reason, one might imagine it as another testicle.  If the moisture lies between the scrotal skin and the white peritoneum, it is palpable. To determine the color of the moisture, probe the swelling with the square probe we previously described.  Judge the color by the appearance of the probe's impression.
If we turn to treatment with leeches, the patient should be bled if possible and if their body is full.  Then, the patient should lie on their back on a slightly elevated surface, with several cloths underneath, and you should sit to their left.
Instruct a servant to sit on the patient's right, extending the patient's penis towards one side of the scrotum and towards the lower abdominal region. Then, take a wide scalpel and make a lengthwise incision through the scrotum's skin, extending almost to the pubic area.
The incision should be made in a straight line parallel to the line dividing the scrotum in half, until it reaches the white peritoneum.  Peel this back, taking care not to cut it.  Make your peel from the side that is closest to the testicle and continue to peel as far as possible.
Then, thoroughly empty the water-filled peritoneum.  Separate the edges of the incision with forceps.  Lift the peritoneum upward, avoiding contact with the scrotal skin. Cut the peritoneum, either in one piece or in sections, as you can.
Especially the thinner portion, if not completely cut, there is a risk of the water returning. If the testicle protrudes outside its skin during your work, once you have finished cutting the peritoneum, return it to its place and then stitch the edges of the scrotal skin together.
Then treat it as you would any other surgical wound until healed.  If the testicle has been damaged by another condition, it is necessary to tie off the vessels in the sac to prevent bleeding.  Then, detach the testicle from the sac, remove the testicle, and treat it as previously described.
If the collected water is in both areas, know that it is a double hydrocele.  Incise the other side in the same way you did the first, or if you prefer, perform a single operation, then remove the blood.
Insert wool soaked in oil or rose oil into the incisions.  Place another layer of wool soaked in liquid and oil on top, covering the testicles and the abdominal area.  Then place a folded cloth, the *rafayd*, on top. Tie it with the six-pointed bandage in this manner.
Then take two cloths, stitch them together, and fill them with fluffy wool in this fashion.  Make the ties from damp cotton or wool and tighten them with a belt around the trousers.  One end of the ties is secured on the buttocks, leading towards the back.
The other end goes towards the pubic area, and the two ends are placed on the lower thighs. The remaining two ends are placed on the hips.  All the ties are fastened with a belt around the trousers.  This hydrocele can also be treated by cauterization instead of incision with an iron.
This involves taking a gentle, heated scalpel to cut the scrotal skin, exposing the white peritoneum containing the water. Then take another cauterizing instrument in this manner:
It resembles a Greek eye; then cauterize that peritoneum while it is hot until all the moisture is expelled.  Then stretch the peritoneum with forceps and, using the cauterizing instrument, scrape off and cut the surrounding layer as much as possible to remove all of it, taking care not to burn the testicle.
Know that this method is safer and less likely to cause bleeding and is better than using incision. If, during the incision or cauterization process, a hot tumor, bleeding, or anything else arises, you should treat whatever occurs with the appropriate treatment as previously described.
If the patient is cowardly and cannot tolerate this procedure due to its unpleasantness, then use the cauterization method as described, if, based on the previous signs, it is appropriate.
If the discharge is watery, the patient should be seated on a high chair.  Then cauterize the swelling at the base with a broad probe.  Position the cauterization along the body, making the opening wide enough for all the water to drain.  Then place cotton on the area and bandage it.
Allow the remaining water to drain, then treat the wound until it heals. If the water does not drain during the cauterization, this is likely because the white membrane is obstructing the opening of the wound, preventing the water from escaping.
In that case, you should insert a goose feather, or the tool used to extract the water from the beloved (a previously described instrument), into the wound, or widen the wound opening slightly.
Know that the water may return and collect after six months or so. If it does collect, cauterize it in the same manner, and the patient should endure the days.
Chapter Sixty-three.
On the incision of fleshy sores and their treatment.
Know that incision on these sores is fraught with danger, leading to death in most cases. Therefore, we recommend leaving them alone and avoiding it.  I will describe the treatments for them and the various types of tumors.
I say that many tumors can occur in the bodies composed of two parts. This occurs from various causes, either from a sharp discharge settling into the body parts or from injury. The tumor's color will be the same as the body's color, and it will not have any water.
The tumor will be hard, and it may be stony, dark in color, and without sensation.  It may also arise from the complication and swelling of arteries, as previously mentioned, or from the swelling of veins.  If from arterial swelling, it is recognized by the spreading of the tumor.
If you press it with your fingers, it should not be incised at all.  As for that which arises from swelling of the veins, nothing spreads when pressed with fingers.  This type may be incised, like other flesh.
One should incise the scrotum's skin, then pull the testicle upward, and remove it from the white peritoneum.  Free the suspended part from the vessels, tie off the vessels, and cut the suspended part after freeing it from every side of the testicle.  If the testicle has adhered to the growing flesh.
Then the testicle should be removed and cut. If the adhesion is between parts of the peritoneum or between vessels, then all of that adhesion should be freed and cut in a circular fashion.
If the flesh growth is in the place of the adhesion, which is behind, then all of it should be cut and the testicle removed, as we said.  When your work is finished, dress the wound with moistened wool in rose-oil and a drink, and then treat it with other remedies until it heals.
Sixty-fourth Chapter.
On treating the dropsy which is with the dilated [organ].
Dilatation is a twisted swelling, resembling a bunch of grapes, along with relaxation of the female [parts].  It makes it difficult for the afflicted person to move, exercise, and walk.  In treating this ailment, the approach is similar to what was mentioned previously.
However, it is necessary to mention the procedure as performed by the ancients.  I say that the afflicted person should sit on a raised chair, then pull the [female] appendages downwards, and then grasp the scrotum skin with your fingers along with the vessels close to the penis.
A servant of yours holds it and stretches it strongly.  Then, with a wide, sharp scalpel, make a slanting incision alongside the vessels until the vessels are exposed.  Then, peel it off from all sides, as I described to you in the removal of the arteries in the temples.  Then insert a needle with a doubled thread into it.
Tie it at the initial points where the dilatation occurred, and also tie it at its end.  Then, make a vertical incision down the length of the body through the middle, and remove the accumulated turbid, corrupt humors.
Then treat the wound as with other surgical wounds that require time to heal so the vessels nourishing one of the [female] parts will fall off.  There is no problem with that.  However, if the dilatation affects all vessels, then it is necessary to remove one of the [female] parts along with the vessels, so that the egg does not lack nourishment. Before the vessels are cut, it withers and is of no use.
Chapter Fifty-Six.
In the treatment of intestinal discharge.
This discharge occurs from a fissure that appears in the peritoneum extending across the abdomen, near the groin area.  The intestine then protrudes from this hernia into one of the [female] parts.
This hernia may be either from a fissure in the peritoneum or from its extension. These two types occur from many causes, such as a blow, a jump, a cry, or lifting a heavy object, and so on.  A sign of the hernia arising from the extension of the peritoneum is that it occurs gradually over a long period of time.
It does not occur suddenly.  The swelling is gradual to the depth because the peritoneum compresses the intestine.  A sign of the hernia being from a fissure in the peritoneum is that it initially causes severe pain all at once, and the swelling is distinct and visible under the skin nearby.
This is due to the intestine exiting from the peritoneum, and sometimes pus may also exit with the intestine, in which case the discharge is called intestinal and purulent.  It may also have air, and sometimes intestinal waste may become trapped there, leading to the patient's demise.
Because it causes severe pain and rumbling, especially if compressed.  Treatment of these types of illnesses with iron is dangerous, so one must avoid it.  The method of treatment is to instruct the patient to push the intestine back into its place with his hand if it can be returned.
Then, the patient lies on their back before you, and you raise their legs.  Then, pull up the skin around the groin, and make a lengthwise incision through the entire scrotum.  Then, insert hooks into the edges of the cut to the extent needed to open the incision further.
The incision should be as large as possible to allow the testicle to exit.  Then, the peritoneum under the scrotum skin is peeled back until the white, hard peritoneum is exposed on all sides.  Then, insert your index finger behind the testicle.
Between the white peritoneum underneath the testicle's skin and the second peritoneum, release the adhesions behind the testicle.  Then, with your right hand, pull inwards on the scrotum skin.  At the same time, pull the white peritoneum upwards with your left hand, lifting the testicle with the peritoneum towards the incision.
You instruct the attendant to pull the testicle upwards, and you completely release the adhesions behind it.  Examine with your finger to ensure that no twisted intestines are within the hard white peritoneum.  If you find any, push them back into the abdomen below.
Then, take a needle with thick thread, twisted from ten strands, and insert it at the edge of the peritoneum under the scrotum skin near the incision. Then, cut the ends of the thread so there are four strands left. Then, interweave some of the strands together in a solid manner and tie them tightly around the peritoneum, which we mentioned is under the scrotal skin, on both sides. Then also wrap the ends of the threads and tie them tightly so that nothing from the vessels supplying it can reach it. So that no inflammatory swelling is formed.  Also, create a second ligature outside the first, at least two finger widths away.  After these two ligatures, leave a space of about the size of a fingertip of the peritoneum under the scrotal skin.
So that no inflammatory swelling is formed.  Also, create a second ligature outside the first, at least two finger widths away.  After these two ligatures, leave a space of about the size of a fingertip of the peritoneum under the scrotal skin. Cut the rest completely around and remove the testicle. Then, make an incision under the scrotal skin that bleeds and oozes, as we described previously.  Then use wool soaked in oil, placing it in the wound.  Apply the ligature we described.
The white peritoneum, after cutting, may need cauterization to prevent bleeding, as this frequently happens.  Leave the ligature until it falls off naturally.  If it is slow to fall off, apply hot water and apply something to cause sweating to help it come off.
Then treat the wound with the usual treatment for surgical wounds until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-six.
In this type of flatulence, I have seen no one dare to treat it with iron.  The ancients mentioned making it as described in the flatulence with a 'dalia', which involves tying off the vessels gently after making an incision below.
Then, make an incision in the middle and treat the swelling to open it so that the vessels fall out. Treat the wound as previously described until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-six.
In the hernia which occurs in the groin.
The hernia in the groin may appear, as we said, but nothing of the intestines descends into the groin. If anything does descend, it is slight and returns at all times.
However, if it persists for a long time, the tear in the peritoneum increases, causing the intestines and testicles to descend into the scrotum. This occurs due to the extension of the peritoneum in the groin, as we have said. This is because the peritoneum extends and then relaxes, causing the area to bulge.  Treatment is by cauterization, as its description was previously given.
And it can be treated with iron in this way: the patient lies on their back before you, then you cut the bulging, protruding hernia area across, to a width of three fingers, and then you separate the layers of peritoneum beneath the skin.
Until the white peritoneum beneath is exposed. Then you take a thread, place it on the bulging area of the peritoneum, and press it into the depth of the abdomen.  Then stitch the two bulging areas, one to the other, on the end of the thread of the peritoneum, joining them together with stitches.
Then pull the thread and do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor touch the testicle or anything else, as I have instructed you in the treatment of intestinal obstruction. Then treat it with the treatments for surgical cases.
When the stitches are severed, cleanse and close the wound until it heals, then the peritoneum contracts and the herniation does not increase.
And this is the best method in this situation, because it is closest to safety.
Chapter Sixty-eight.
On Relaxation of the Scrotum's Skin.
The skin of the scrotum often relaxes in some people without the internal tissues relaxing, and this spoils its appearance.  Therefore, whoever desires to treat this, should lay the patient on their back.
And cut all the relaxed skin from the thick skin, then bring the edges together. If you prefer, first stitch the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches and secure them, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it with the treatment of other surgical conditions until it heals and the stitches fall out.
The Sixty-ninth Chapter.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
For this reason, I should not have mentioned it in this book, but I have done so for two reasons: one, so that a physician will know it if asked about it and know the treatment for those affected; and the other, because we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
Such as sheep, oxen, and cats, and similar animals. I say that castration is of two types: either by pressing or by incision and cutting.  The method of pressing is that we place the animal in hot water until its testicles relax and soften and hang down.
Then you press it with your hand until it dissolves and is no longer discernible when touched. As for castration by incision and cutting, it is necessary to hold the animal and squeeze the skin of its testicles with your left hand, then tie the cords and make a single incision on each testicle.
Until the testicles emerge, then cut them off after peeling them and leaving no peritoneum on them except the thin peritoneum that is on the vessels. This method of castration is better than that done by pressing.
Because pressing might leave some of the testicles, and the animal will desire mating, then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
On treating hermaphrodites.
The hermaphrodite in men comes in two types. One appears in the area below the pubic bone or on the scrotal skin between the testicles as if it were a woman's vulva with hair, and urine may flow from what is in the scrotal skin.
In women, there is one type, appearing above the vulva on the pubic bone, like small, protruding male testicles. One of them appears as a man's penis, and both resemble female genitalia.  Treatment for all three types.
The two types of men and the one type of women, the excess flesh should be cut until the effect subsides, then treated with other surgical treatments until healed.  As for the second type of men from whom urine flows from the scrotal skin, no action is taken, and it will not heal.
Chapter Seventy-one.
In the excision of the clitoris and excess flesh from the vulva of women.
The clitoris may increase beyond the natural size, becoming unsightly and discoloured.  In some women, it may enlarge to the extent of resembling that of men, affecting sexual intercourse.
One should grasp the excess clitoris with a hand or forceps and cut it off. Do not cut too deeply, especially into the root, to avoid excessive bleeding.
Then treat it with surgical remedies until healed.  As for the excess flesh, it is flesh that grows in the vaginal opening until it fills it.
And it may extend outwards, resembling a tail. Therefore, some early practitioners called it the "tail" disease.  One should cut it off, just as one cuts the clitoris, and treat it until healed.
Chapter Seventy-two.
On the treatment of ruptures.
Rupture is when a woman's vulva is not perforated, or the perforation is small, and it may be either congenital or acquired.  The acquired type is due to a preceding cause, and may arise from excess overgrown flesh.
or from thin or thick peritoneum, and it may be in the depth of the uterus, or on its sides, or above or below it.  It prevents intercourse, procreation, and childbirth, and perhaps menstruation.  Knowledge of this condition is obtained by examination by the midwife.
If the obstruction is evident and close at hand, then if it is not apparent, she should examine it with her fingers or a probe. If the obstruction is from thin peritoneum and is close to the labia, then proceed to pierce it; that is to say, place something like a splint on the labia, then place both of your thumbs.
And the woman lies on her back with her legs spread, then forcefully separates the labia until the thin peritoneum is pierced and the obstruction opens.  Then take wool and soak it in oil, and place it on the area, and have intercourse with the woman daily, so that the area does not adhere again.
If the peritoneum is thick and dense, it should be cut with a broad scalpel resembling a thin, flat knife. If the obstruction is from overgrown flesh, grasp it with forceps and cut it off.  Have with you remedies to stop bleeding without causing a wound, such as pomegranate, alum, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then use a wide lead tube so that the wound does not quickly close up. Hold it in place for several days, and use a wick made of dry linen, then treat it with the usual remedies for an abscess until it heals.  There may be further growths of flesh in the uterus, which should be cut in this manner.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor, the cancerous tumor that occurs in the uterus should not be treated with iron at all.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red pustules that appear in women's vulvas.
Hemorrhoids are the swelling of the mouths of the veins, causing copious, continuous bleeding.  If hemorrhoids progress, they become warts.  We have already mentioned their types and signs in the classification; we describe them here.
Of these, some are treatable, some not.  I say that hemorrhoids and warts, if deep within the uterus and not apparent to touch, are not treatable with iron.  Those on the mouth of the uterus, which can be felt, are those that are treated.
The woman should enter a cool room, then use a scalpel or rough cloth to remove the warts from their roots.  Then, if blood flows from one of the blood-stopping tools, stop it without pricking.
For example, with caquia, shiyan, and frankincense, and similar things.  Then raise her legs to the wall for an hour, then lower them into cool water.  If the bleeding persists, have her sit in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate peels, and astringents, and similar things.  Then take barley flour, mix it with honey and vinegar.
Apply this to her back.  If the bleeding stops, then stop. Otherwise, apply leeches to the chest and breastbone, without any restrictions. Then take wool, dip it in the juice of the thyme or the juice of the asparagus or the juice of the willow branches, with astringent drink, and apply it to the area.
If the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until it heals.  As for a red sore, resembling the heads of sores, rough in appearance, it should be cut away as described for warts, and treated until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
Concerning the treatment of abscesses that appear in the uterus.
Many types of tumors can appear in the uterus, such as cancer, hardened tumors, ulcers, canker sores, malignant sores, polyps, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and inflamed tumors.  All these diseases and their types and signs have been described in the classification.
Therefore, in this section we must discuss the inflamed tumor that appears in the uterus, if it is a tumor that has gathered for a time, how to treat it with iron.  One must observe if the tumor's pain in its initial stages is severe, accompanied by throbbing, inflammation, and fever.
And if you see it red, if touched, do not rush to treat it. Treat it with what aids ripening until these symptoms subside.  Then, the woman should sit on a chair with two seats and lean back.
And she should bring her legs together towards the lower abdomen, with her thighs spread apart, and place her arms under her knees, binding them with a suitable bandage.
Then, the midwife should sit on the right side and use the instrument to open the cervix, the image of which will be shown later. If you intend to use this instrument, you must estimate the depth of the woman's uterus so that what enters the instrument does not exceed the depth of the uterus.
Otherwise, the patient will be harmed. If the instrument is longer than the depth, then pads should be placed on the lip of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the uterine depth.  The spiral that runs inside the instrument should be positioned from the upper side.
And while you hold the instrument, the attendant turns the spiral until the cervix is opened. If a boil appears and is felt with the hand to be soft and thin, it should be incised at its head with a wide scalpel. When all the matter has been discharged.
A soft wick dipped in rose oil or green oil with some astringent should be placed in the wound. The wick should be protruding from the incision in the cervix.  Outside, on the uterus and pubis, place pure wool dipped in boiled water for baking.
Then, treat with the appropriate ointment for two days until healed.
The uterus and wound may be washed by injecting water and honey, then water boiled with root of the sos or long zeraound.  Then, return to the ointment, if the abscess is hidden inside the uterus.
Avoid treating it with iron; rather, treat it as described in the division.
Chapter seventy-five.
Teaching midwives how to treat living fetuses if they emerge in an unnatural form.
The midwife should first know the natural form of childbirth.  Among its signs, if you see the woman pressing downwards, longing to breathe the air, and feeling relief from her postpartum state.
And if the delivery of the fetus is swift, know that this birth is natural, and the fetus emerges headfirst with the placenta attached or hanging from the umbilical cord.  If you see these signs, then you should massage her abdomen to expedite the delivery of the fetus.
If it emerges headfirst, the placenta descends with it, and is cleaned thoroughly of any abnormalities.  Any birth other than this is unnatural and reprehensible, as the fetus may emerge feet first, with its hands or feet coming out before its head.
And if one hand or one foot emerges, or if its head and hand, or if it emerges folded, or perhaps if it turns on its back, and similar reprehensible forms, the midwife should be skilled and gentle with all these things, and wary of mistakes and errors.
And I am explaining each of these forms and how to manage them, so that you can recognize them. If the fetus emerges headfirst, the natural way, and the labor is difficult and the woman's strength weakens, then have her sit on a chair and order women to restrain her.
And bind her uterus with cooked linseed and moist ointments.  Then the midwife should insert a small scalpel between her fingers, and cut the placenta, or cut it with her fingernail, until the moisture within it drains. Then massage the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then the woman should be injected with linseed saliva mixed with sheikh oil.  Then, after the injection, she should be made to cough and sneeze with a knick-knacks and hold her mouth and nose, for the fetus will emerge then. If the child's hands emerge first, they should be gently pushed back, little by little.
If entry is not possible, place the woman on a platform and lift her legs up. Then shake the platform on the ground, and the woman holds on so she does not fall during the shaking. If the hands do not enter and the fetus dies, cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them moderately, and it will emerge. If the fetus emerges on its feet, then the feet should be pushed upward, then the fetus should be turned slightly, little by little, until it is straightened, then one of its legs should be gently pulled.
If they [the legs] emerge to the thighs, instruct her to cough and sneeze her with a knick-knacks, and it will emerge. If it does not emerge as described, then gently turn the fetus back to its natural position. Then it will emerge smoothly.  If it resists all these methods.
Take some saliva of the *khatmi*, saliva of linseed, and sheikh oil, and dissolved gum. Grind all of them well in a mortar. Then apply it to the woman's vulva and lower abdomen. Then, make her sit in lukewarm water until the shurasif.
If you see that it has softened below, make her a pessary of myrrh and have her endure it. If she holds the pessary for an hour, have her sit on a chair, then sneeze her, close her mouth and nose, and gently press her lower abdomen. Then the fetus will emerge immediately. The fetus will emerge on its knees and hands.
If it emerges in this manner, gently insert your hands, little by little. Then, the woman should lie on her back, her hips lowered, lying on a bed, and holding her hands. Then, gently press on top of her abdomen. Then remove the cushions from under her legs so that they are raised. If the fetus does not emerge, take both her legs and move them vigorously. Then, gently press above the shurasif, little by little, until the fetus rises. Then the midwife should insert her hand and gently adjust the fetus.
And instruct the woman to strain until the fetus emerges. The fetus will emerge sideways, with one arm hanging down.  Gently try to return the arm. If unable to, have the woman stand and walk. If she cannot walk, have her lie down.
Shake her legs vigorously. Then use the ointment, which I described, made from saliva. If it is dislodged, have her sit on a chair and work to reposition the arm and straighten the fetus to its normal form. Have the woman sneeze and instruct her to strain until it emerges.
The fetus emerges on its back, arms outstretched, and face towards the mother's back. The midwife should grasp the fetus's arm and gently turn and straighten it. Once straightened, apply the fats and saliva we previously described.
Then instruct her to move slowly.  If she does so, have her sit on a chair, and instruct her to lean to the right side.  Then have her strain, and use a compress to make her sneeze.  The fetus will emerge, upright on its side.
If it descends into the cervix in this manner with the placenta, then slit the placenta with your fingernail to remove excess moisture.  If it is wrapped around the fetus's face and neck, cut it from the umbilical cord to prevent suffocation and death.  Then push the fetus inward, so that it is in proper position.
Tell the woman to strain repeatedly, and it will emerge easily.  Regarding the birth of twins or multiple fetuses, know that twins are common; three or four may also be born and survive, but this is rare.  As for five, it is unnatural and they will never survive.
And if there are many, they are surrounded by a single placenta.  They are separated by a partition-like membrane connecting each to its own umbilical cord.  They may emerge like a single fetus, naturally and easily, or they may emerge with difficulty, as described previously.
If some of them are difficult to deliver and take on the abnormal forms we mentioned, treat them as described.  Let the midwife be gentle, and perform the actions with care and patience, using any method that leads to safety.
The number of fetuses that can be conceived in the womb is variable: one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten.  I have evidence that one woman miscarried seven, and another fifteen, all simultaneously conceived.
By the estimation of the All-Knowing and All-Powerful, this knowledge must be part of a midwife's understanding.  Perhaps such a case might occur, and she must be prepared to address it accordingly.
Chapter Sixty-Seven.
On the delivery of a dead fetus.
If, after treating the dead fetus with the methods described in the classification, it does not emerge and you are forced to manually intervene, then you must consider the woman's condition. If she is strong and has no symptoms that suggest potential harm, the woman should lie on her back on a bed.
Her head should be tilted downward, and her legs raised. A servant should hold her from both sides or secure her to the bed to prevent her body from shifting when the fetus is delivered.
Then, moisten the mouth of her uterus with moist ointments, along with the saliva of the *khatmi* (a plant), fenugreek, and linseed. The midwife then anoints her hand with these ointments and saliva, gently inserting it into the uterus.  Then, she seeks a place to implant hooks into the fetus, and the appropriate locations for this.
Observe; if the fetus is positioned to descend headfirst, implant the hooks in its eyes, back of the head, mouth, palate, under the chin, collarbone, near the ribs, or beneath the clavicles.
And if it is descending feet-first, then into the pubic area and the middle ribs.  The hook should be held in the right hand, its curve positioned between the fingers of the left hand. The hand should be inserted gently and a hook implanted in one of the aforementioned locations.
Until it reaches an empty space. Then, another or a third hook is inserted opposite the first, if needed, to ensure the pull is even and not biased to one side. The pull should be steady and not just straight, but also moved in every direction.
To facilitate its exit, as is done with a tooth during extraction.  During this, the pull should be relaxed if any part is obstructed. If a part is obstructed, the midwife should anoint some fingers with the ointment and insert them to turn the obstruction. If some part of the fetus emerges, the hooks should be moved to other locations.
She [the midwife] should slightly raise it [the fetus] to do this until the entire fetus is expelled. If a hand emerges before other parts and cannot be pushed back due to compression, it should be wrapped in a cloth and pulled out until it is all expelled, then cut off.
Either at the wrist joint or at the elbow. This should be done for the other hand and feet as well.  If the fetus's head is large and encountering pressure during delivery, or if there is accumulated fluid in the head, a pointed/sharp scalpel should be inserted between the fingers.
With it, the head is cracked open to allow the water to exit, or it is pressed with an instrument called a *mashdakh*, whose image will appear with the images of other instruments in the following chapter.
Likewise, if the fetus has a large head, it should be cracked or pressed with the *mashdakh*, as we said, then the bones are extracted with forceps.  If the head comes out and is compressed at the collarbone, it should be cracked open to allow the moisture in the chest to drain.
Then the chest will contract. If the chest does not contract and cannot be expelled, cut it as best as possible.  If the lower abdomen is swollen or has lumps, it should be incised so that the moisture may drain from it.
If the fetus is on its feet, pulling it and positioning it toward the cervix is easy. If it is compressed against the abdomen or chest, it should be pulled with a cloth wrapped around your hand, and the abdomen or chest should be incised to allow the contents to drain.
If all the other organs have been removed and the head remains inside, the midwife should insert her left hand. If the cervix is open, she should insert her hand into the cervix, locate the head, and pull it with her fingers toward the cervix. Then, she should insert a hook or two hooks and pull it out with them.
If the cervix has contracted due to a hot, inflamed tumor, it should not be forcibly treated, but rather moist, fatty substances should be applied, and bandages used. She should sit in relaxing, moist waters.
If the fetus is on its side, if it can be positioned, the same methods described for a living fetus should be used. If that is not possible, it should be cut open and removed.  Absolutely nothing of the placenta should be left inside.  Once you have finished your treatment, apply the treatments for hot tumors that appear in the uterus.
If bleeding occurs, she should sit in constricting waters and use other treatments.  I have observed a woman who conceived, then had the fetus die within her, and subsequently conceived again.
Then the other fetus also died. After a long time, a tumor appeared in her womb and swelled until it opened, discharging pus. I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time, but the wound did not heal.  I applied some strong, astringent ointments to it.
Then a bone came out of the area, and after a few days, another bone came out. I was surprised by this, as the abdomen does not contain bones.  I concluded that they were bones from the dead fetus.  I probed the wound and removed many bones from the fetal skull.
I continued this until I extracted many bones. The woman was in good condition and lived for a considerable time, with a small amount of pus continuing to discharge from the area.  I have presented this unusual case here.
Because it contains knowledge and assistance for what a skilled physician attempts with his hands in treatment.
Chapter Seventy-Seven.
It contains images of the instruments needed for removing the fetus.
Image of a spiral for opening the cervix.
This binder with which books are bound has two spirals at the ends of the planks, but this spiral should be finer than the binder and made of either ebony or boxwood.  The width of each plank should be two finger-widths.
Their edges are about a finger's width, and their length is one and a half spans.  In the middle of the planks, there are two rods of the same wood, securely fastened to them.  Their length is half a span or a little more, and their width is about two fingers or a little more.  These two rods...
They are the ones that enter the cervix to open it when you turn the spirals.  Image of another similar instrument, finer and lighter.
Made of ebony or boxwood, shaped like tongs, but with two rods at the end, as you see.  The length of each rod is about a span, and their width is two fingers.  If you wish to open the cervix with it...
Have the woman sit on a bed, her legs spread apart.  Then insert these two rods, closed, into the cervix.  You hold the end of the instrument below between her legs, then open your hand with the instrument, just as you do with tongs, to the extent you want to open the cervix. So that the midwife does what she wants; an image of another spiral, mentioned by the ancients.
Image of the instrument used to expel the fetus.
Image of the instrument used to break the head of the fetus.
It resembles scissors, with teeth on the end, as you see.  It can also be made rectangular, like tongs, in this form, as you see, with teeth like saw teeth for cutting and separating.
Image of a device also.
Image of a hook.
This hook should be somewhat thick so as not to break when pulling the fetus with it.  Image of a two-pronged hook.
Image of two wide scalpels for cutting the fetus.
All these tools, the more varied their types and the more prepared they are by the craftsman, the faster his work and the higher his reputation among people.  Do not undervalue any tool, for you may need it.
Chapter seventy-eight.
In the delivery of the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, one should instruct the woman to hold herself, then make her sneeze with the instrument, and hold your hand over her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out this way, then do so; otherwise, take another measure.
