Pierce a hole in the cover and place in it open herbs for the cervix, such as foeniculum, seseli, dill, chamomile, shih, silybum, and quassia, any or all of these.  Soak them in water and place on heat. Then place a silver tube over the hole in the cover, and the other end into the cervix.
Hold it until the vapor reaches the bottom of the uterus, then have her sneeze as we said. Then the placenta will be expelled quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to dip her left hand in the grease of a she-camel or the saliva of a viper.
Then she inserts it into the vagina and searches for the placenta. If she finds it, she grasps it and pulls it out slowly, little by little. If it is stuck deep in the uterus, insert the hand as described until finding the placenta, and then gently pull it out little by little.
So as not to dislocate the uterus with forceful pulling, one should gently move it to the sides, right and left.  Then increase the pulling force.  It will then respond and detach from the adhesion. If the cervix is closed, then we have described the treatment with sneezing, and the treatment with the pot and herbs.
If it does not come out with all the methods described, avoid force when pulling again.  Instead, tie what has come out to the woman's thigh near the cervix, then inject her with the four-part ointment.  It will cause it to fester after a few days.
It will dissolve and come out. However, if it festers, a foul odor will ascend from it to the stomach and head, harming the patient.  Therefore, the appropriate fumigant should be used.
Therefore, some of the ancients tried the smoke of frankincense and dried figs.  A depiction of the apparatus used to fumigate the woman when menstruation and the placenta are retained, and the like.
It is made of lead, shaped like a funnel, or made of copper. The narrow end is placed at the vulva, and the wide end is placed over a fire.  Incense is placed on embers; it is held until it is gone, and then more is put in its place.
Chapter Seventy-nine.
In the treatment of the non-perforated anus.
Many boys are born with an unopened anus, which may be blocked by a thin membrane.  The midwife should pierce this membrane with her finger, or, if not, with a sharp scalpel, taking care not to touch the muscle.
Then apply wool soaked in wine and oil, and treat it with ointments until healed.  If you fear that it will close up again, insert a lead tube into the opening for several days, and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
The anus may also be blocked by a scar or a tumor.  It is necessary to incise the scar tissue, then treat it with the remedies mentioned, including cauterization.
Chapter Eighty.
On treating warts that occur on the lower part of the body.
Warts that occur on the lower part of the body are a thickening and hardening that occurs near the anus, either externally or within the surrounding space, and may be present as a single or multiple lesion.
If this thickening becomes chronic, it may open and discharge a whitish watery fluid or thin pus.  Some of these warts may penetrate into the rectum and intestines; others may not.  Penetrating warts can be identified by what comes out with stool and flatulence when the patient defecates.
Perhaps worms may emerge from it.  Some warts, if located in the surrounding space, may penetrate the bladder and urethra.  Others may penetrate the hip joint and the tailbone.  A wart that penetrates the anus is also recognized as penetrating, regardless of its other characteristics.
Insert your index finger into the anus and introduce a thin probe, made of brass or iron, into the wart, if there is no deviation.  If there is a deviation, insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair.
Until you feel the probe or hair in your finger.  If you feel nothing at all, and no stool, flatulence, or worms emerge from the opening, as we have mentioned, know that it is not penetrating, and proceed with treatment.
If the wart penetrates the bladder or urethra, the sign is the discharge of urine from it, and the inability of the affected area to heal with medication.  If it penetrates the hip joint or tailbone, the sign is the probe reaching that location.
If there is no deviation to the bone, and pain persists in the vicinity, with constant discharge of pus, and if treatment and ointment application are ineffective in healing, these penetrating warts will never heal.
Treatment is futile for those who try to deal with them using the methods of ignorant physicians.  However, for those that are not penetrating and not chronic, healing is expected with the iron, as I describe, and as I have experienced.  The patient should lie on their back before you.
Raise his legs and tilt his thighs towards his abdomen. Then, insert a copper or lead probe if the wart has a deviation, so you can find where the probe ends. If the patient feels it near the anus, then you should insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel the probe has penetrated your finger completely, without feeling any membrane or flesh between your finger and the probe, then know for certain that it is penetrating.  Do not waste effort on it, as it will not heal, as we have said.
Some say it heals in some people rarely.  A useful treatment is to heat a thin cautery according to the size of the wart, as previously mentioned, and introduce it into the wart until it reaches the vicinity of the anus.  Then, repeat this two or three times.
So that you know that all the excess, hardened flesh, resembling a bird's feather tube, has been burned. Then treat it with a thread soaked in oil until that burned flesh comes out. Then treat it with healing ointments, and if it heals.
Otherwise, it will never heal with any other treatment.  If you insert the probe but it does not reach your finger in the anus, and there is a thick layer of flesh or membrane between it and the probe, and you see the wart just below the surface of the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the wart. While passing the incision with the probe in the wart, continue until the incision reaches where the tip of the probe ends. Remove and discard the probe. Then clean away that hardened flesh, resembling a bird's feather tube, leaving nothing behind.
Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If bleeding overwhelms you and prevents you from performing the cutting of the flesh, the best and quickest remedy is to cauterize it with fire or a strong medicine.  Cauterizing with fire combines two beneficial effects: burning the excess flesh.
Cut the blood and dry the moisture. Then treat it with a thread soaked in oil or powdered sulfur mixed with oil until the area becomes purulent and the burned flesh comes out in the pus.  Then treat the wound with healing ointments that promote the growth of firm flesh.
These are the medicines that are astringent and drying.  If it heals, it is healed, and there is no need to return to the procedure. If the wart extends deep into the anus and is far from the surface of the body, insert your finger into the anus and search with it.
If you feel the probe and between you and it there is a barrier of peritoneum or flesh and it is close to breaking through, then the only action is with hope and expectation.  Either cauterize it, as we said.
Or, cut it until you reach its bottom so you can insert the thread and treatment from close proximity.  Do not extend the cut too far, lest you sever the muscle surrounding the anus, causing the patient to involuntarily pass stool. Then treat it as we described.  Perhaps it will heal as we said.
Or, penetrate that barrier and pierce it after you have cut it close to the anus, as we said, either with the probe or another sharp-edged instrument. Then clean away all of the congealed flesh in the cut as far as you can reach.
Then suture the entire wound, including the cut and the upper opening, until it is closed.  The wart may remain open within the anus, which will be less burdensome for the patient.  The wart may heal in this manner.
If, when you insert the probe into the wart and it is on one side of the anus, near the body surface, with the skin and the ring of the anus, then take a probe with a pierced tip, like a cobbler's awl, in this way.
And insert a thread, made of five threads or so, into it. Then insert the probe with the thread into the wart until you reach its bottom. If it is protruding from the edge of the anus from the inside, nearby.
Then pull the thread out of that hole by inserting your finger into the anus and pulling out the end of the thread. Tie the two ends together and leave it for a day or two. Whenever the thread cuts into the flesh, pull it firmly until the flesh between the ends of the threads is cut and falls out.  Then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the opening of the wart is not protruding, make it protrude as best you can, but do not do this if it is deep, to avoid cutting the muscle. Then do as described until it heals.
The image of the pointed lancet, with which you cut the warts, should have a very sharp curvature on one side and a blunt side on the other, so as not to cut what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter Eighty-one.
Concerning hemorrhoids that bleed, their excision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids come in two types: either within the anus, resembling red swellings or like grapes, and they may be small or large, and blood continually flows from them.
They may be single or multiple, and some are external to the anus, near its edges. However, those external to the anus are, for the most part, less moist. Yellowish water, or a little blood, drips from them constantly.  They are the color of the skin.
For those internal to the anus, instruct the patient to defecate and strain until the anus is stretched.  The polyps will then appear, and you should quickly seize them with forceps or your fingernail, and cut them at their base, if the forceps do not remain held firmly on them due to their moistness and laxity.
Then grasp it with a rough cloth or a piece of sharpened linen, or pull it with your fingers, and cut it, then sprinkle some sharp powders on it after cutting, in order to act as cautery, or cauterize it as previously described in the chapter on cautery.
Then treat it with other remedies until it heals. If the anus does not yield to stretching, administer an enema containing a little stinging substance to wash what is inside the anus and to quickly cause it to yield when the patient strains. As for the polyps external to the anus, their treatment is easy and simple.
Take it with your fingernail or forceps, cut it, and then treat it as previously described until it heals. If one dislikes cutting with iron, one should use a needle and thread.
Then pull the polyp upwards and pierce it with the needle at its base from the opposite side, wrap the ends of the thread around the needle, which is positioned across the polyp, and pull the polyp firmly.  Tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this with all the polyps and leave one unpierced.
To cause the blood to flow, then apply a cloth soaked in rose or cypress oil to the anus, have the patient rest, and leave it until the polyps fall off.  Once they have fallen off, treat them with ointments and other remedies until they are healed.
As for treating fissures, they often arise from dryness and retention of waste.  If prolonged and remedies are ineffective, it is necessary to scrape them with a scalpel or your fingernail until they become moist and the top layer that prevents healing is removed.
Then treat it until it heals properly. If it does not heal, scrape it more vigorously than before, until it bleeds and is scraped clean, then treat it.  It will heal.
Chapter Eighty-two.
On the treatment of ingrown nails, both ingrown and non-ingrown, dry warts, and the pimple.
The pimple is a rounded swelling the color of the skin, resembling the head of a nail, found throughout the body, particularly on the soles of the feet and fingers, and causes pain when walking.
It is necessary to dissect around the pimple, grasping it with a lancet or a forceps, and to pull it out from its root. Then treat it. If you wish, cauterize it, as described in the relevant section.  As for dry warts, each one is a small, rough, hardened, rounded protuberance on the surface of the body.
It often appears on children's hands. Its treatment is to stretch the wart, cut it, or pierce it with a silk thread or hair until it falls off.  If you wish, cauterize it as previously described with fire or a strong medicine.
Ingrown and non-ingrown warts may be removed with the instrument I have mentioned in the treatment of the pimple.  The pimple is also a small, hardened, thick protuberance on the surface of the body, going quite deep.  If it is affected by cold, one feels a sensation similar to the sting of an ant.
It occurs throughout the body, mostly on the hands.  Treatment involves taking a tube made of strong goose or eagle feathers and placing it around the pimple so that the tube encircles it. Then, rotate your hand with the tube to break the skin surrounding the pimple.
And if you wish, you can do this with a brass or iron tube, in this manner,  placing it deep in the flesh and pulling the pimple from its root.
The top of the tube should be firm, twisted, and easy for the fingers to grasp and twist.  If you wish, cauterize the pimple as previously described for cauterizing warts in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
On the forms of instruments used for injections in diseases of the rectum, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe may be made of silver, porcelain, or hollowed or hammered brass.  These instruments may be made in various sizes to suit those who use them. Those used for treating young children or those with narrow or painful rectums should be very slender.  Image of a large syringe:.
Its upper funnel, where the reservoir is attached, should be wide, with a partition to hold the reservoir as shown.  The lower tip, which enters the rectum, must be very smooth and perfectly rounded, with two holes on one side and a single hole on the other.
The hole should be the thickness of a reed, or slightly thicker.  The reservoir, in which the medicines are placed, is made of animal bladder or sheepskin, and should be small and approximately one span in diameter.
It is pierced with many holes, spiraling outward, with the distance between each hole about the thickness of a finger.  A strong thread of doubled yarn (approximately 10 strands), is then inserted into these holes to tie and gather the reservoir like a small mat. Once the medicine is placed inside, tighten the head of the syringe between the stones firmly, and then inject. This is an image of the reservoir and its holes.
The method of injection is to fill the bladder or skin pouch with the medication, warmed to the patient's tolerance, avoiding extreme heat or cold.  Then, the patient lies on their back with their legs raised.
A mat or blanket is placed beneath them.  The tip of the syringe is then lubricated with ointment, egg white, or licorice juice, or similar.  Gently, it is inserted into the rectum.  The attendant holds the reservoir with both hands, squeezing it firmly until all the contents of the syringe are emptied into the bowels.
Then, the syringe is removed, and the patient lowers their legs. If the medicine is prompting expulsion, the patient should hold it as long as possible. If the patient sleeps with it in all night, it is more effective for expulsion.  If it is used for diarrhea and the expulsion is rushed, repeat the enema until recovery.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the rectum, the syringe should be very gentle, smoother than the syringes used for children.  This is an image of a gentle syringe with its skin drawn tight.
Chapter Eighty-Four.
In the treatment of surgical wounds.
I have previously mentioned in the classification the treatment of conditions similar to burns. Here, I describe their treatment with the hand and with medicines in full detail.  I say that surgical wounds differ according to the cause of the wound and the location of the injury.
The causes of wounds are numerous, such as striking with a stone, cutting with a sword or knife, stabbing with a spear, stick, or arrow, and similar things.
And the wounds, according to their location on the body, are like those on the head, neck, chest, abdomen, liver, and other organs.
And I describe the treatment of some wounds to make them a model and a principle for all other wounds.  I begin with the simpler wounds of the head, especially since the compound wounds will be mentioned in the first chapter of the third book of this book.
I say that if a simple wound occurs on the head, and it is not a fracture of the bone, then, if it is from a blow with a stone or similar, and only the skin is damaged, and the wound is large, and you fear the occurrence of a hot swelling, then quickly incise the wound with a lancet on the spot. Do not delay this, and release blood according to its strength, if nothing prevents the incision.  Especially if the blood coming from the wound is slight.  Let the incision be from the opposite side of the injured area.
Warn the patient against fullness, drinks, and thick foods. If something prevents the incision, then let him reduce his food intake, avoid meat and drinks, and if a hot swelling occurs on the wound, apply a cotton pad soaked in rose oil, alone or with a drink that has a binding effect.
And if you fear a hot swelling, apply to the wound, if it is fresh with its blood and the air has not altered it—the air I will describe to you shortly—; but if the air has altered it, apply some ointments to it.
Until the pus is discharged, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large, and caused by a sword cut or similar, and its edges do not come together by themselves, bring them together by stitching, as I will describe in the stitching of abdominal wounds.
If the skin has exposed the bone and is hanging, and its attachment is only in a small projection, cut it, then treat the wound with medicines to form strong flesh in place of the skin.  Tell the patient to eat foods that provide strength, such as heads of animals and similar.
If a wound in the skin has become putrid and is not attached to the bone, cut away all the putrid flesh and cauterize it, then treat it. If in these wounds there is an artery or vein bleeding, and the bleeding does not stop with medicines, search for the artery.
If you strike it and it does not stop, cut it with a lancet and tie it off. If the previously mentioned treatments are not effective, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and superficial, their treatment is easy; it is sufficient to sprinkle this powder on them, while they are still bleeding.
Before the air changes it, the recipe for the powder is: one part frankincense, two parts asafoetida, and three parts quicklime or un-quicklime.  Grind all together, sift, and fill the wound with it, applying firm pressure to ensure good adherence.
It will adhere to the blood, then compress it from above with bandages. Leave it undisturbed as long as it does not swell. The medicine will not be removed until it is healed.  Quicklime alone may achieve this effect if the frankincense and asafoetida are unavailable.
This action can also be performed on many larger wounds if properly applied and bandaged, and if the wounds are fresh and still bleeding, so long as the air has not altered them.  However, if the air has already caused some alteration, especially during the summer.
Therefore, using this powder is not the only effective treatment.  Apply some ripening ointment or a poultice of barley flour mixed with water and honey to encourage pus formation, then treat it with other methods until healed.
If, however, the wound involves a small fracture of the bone, pull it out with a cloth.  Treatment of head injuries is discussed in the section on neck injuries. If the wound is in the neck, there is no difference in treatment compared to a simple head wound.
If a nerve or artery in the neck has been severed, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treatments that cause strong constriction, such as verdigris, alum, and similar substances, because they injure and spasm the nerve.
Never apply anything cold to the wound, because the essence of the nerve is cold, and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest of organs.  Use gentle remedies such as washed rose petals, repeatedly moistened with fresh water or oil, or rose and zinc ointments.
If also washed with fresh water and other mineral stones in this manner, and with moist ointments.  If the wound is large, use sutures or bring the edges together with splints, and then treat until healed.
If the wound has a cavity and pus has collected at the bottom, drain it from the lowest point.  If an artery has been severed in the wound and you see blood flowing, tie it off, or cauterize it if necessary.
If part or all of the hyoid cartilage has been cut, but the vessels are intact, bring the edges of the skin together with sutures on the hyoid cartilage.  Do not touch the hyoid cartilage, but smooth and restore it to its natural shape, then tie it securely, and leave it for a number of days.
Then treat it with astringent and drying ointments until healed. If the wound has affected the bone, examine it. If there are bone fragments, remove as many as possible. If not all can be removed, leave it until the wound becomes infected and heals, making removal easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle it with the powders we described. If bone in the wound becomes infected and forms a fistula, treat it as described in the chapter on chest and shoulder surgery.
If the wound is from a spear or knife and you see a cavity, observe. If air comes out when the patient breathes, know that it is a fatal wound.  If there is no cavity and the wound is fresh with blood, do not initially use powders or tighten it, lest the blood be trapped in the cavity.
For it will recede to the heart and kill the patient. Instead, apply a drawing ointment twice daily. If no ointment is available, place a soft cotton in the wound to absorb the moisture.  Have the patient sleep with the wound uppermost.
To drain what gathers there. If three days or more have passed, and the patient shows no spasms, bad palpitations, or shortness of breath, and their overall condition is good, know that the wound is healthy. Treat it with bandages and other remedies until healed.
If the wound cannot be seen, and the discharge persists, know that it has become a fistula. Treat it according to that section.  The patient should lie on the wound to drain what gathers there. If the wound is on the chest or back, treat it according to the preceding description of suturing.
If it is large, or if small, if it has affected the bone and broken off fragments, explore the wound and remove the fragments if they are loose. If they are not loose, leave them be.
Allow the wound to fester, as this will make removal easier.  The treatment of other wounds occurring in other parts of the body is governed by the principles we have discussed.
Chapter Eighty-Five.
On wounds of the abdomen, and the expulsion and suturing of the intestines.
A wound to the abdomen may be large, small, or medium.  A large wound may expose intestines, or several intestines.  Returning and suturing them will be more difficult and arduous.
A small wound, too, may present another difficulty, making the return of the intestines more difficult. If the intestines are not promptly returned to their place, they will swell and thicken, making their return more difficult.  Therefore, the most favorable wound is the medium-sized one, because the return of the intestines is not as difficult as it is with these other two types.
Know that there are four methods for suturing the abdomen. Two are general, suitable for suturing the abdomen and other bodily wounds, and the other two are specific, applying to all wounds but especially suited for suturing the abdomen.
And I will describe them to you one by one with explanation.  If the wound is small and something of the intestines has come out, and its return is difficult, it is either due to the smallness of the wound, as we said, or because the intestines have been inflated by the cold air.
If this is the case, you should warm it by dipping a sponge or damp cloth in lukewarm water, or water in which izhar, sa'di, and senbel have been boiled, and apply it to the intestines until the swelling subsides.
A drink with a constricting property may also achieve this, and is superior to plain water in resolving the swelling. If you see that the swelling has subsided, moisten the intestines with water in which khutmmi or khabazi have been boiled, as this will facilitate their return with ease.
If, after this treatment, its return is still impossible, make a small incision in the wound using the tool with which you would incise nostrils. The initial treatment, if successful, is preferable to incision, and incision is only resorted to when absolutely necessary and all other means fail. This is a picture of the tool.
Its one curved side is limited, and the other side is unlimited. The thin end is not as thin as a scalpel, but slightly blunt. It is a tool resembling a small saw, as you see. If the wound is wide and the intestines have entered, their return should be to their natural shape.
Its specific location, if possible, is preferable. However, if the wound is wide and in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on their back with their legs elevated higher than their head. If it is in the upper abdomen, the head and chest should be higher than the lower body.
Likewise, if the wound is on one side of the abdomen, always aim to keep the side with the surgical incision higher than the other side. This approach is also necessary for major and intermediate surgeries.
For smaller surgeries, place it as best as possible. Then, have a helpful assistant hold the entire wound in their hands, bringing the edges together, and then gradually revealing sections to the person performing the suturing.
This is a description of basic single-layer suturing. Take a needle or needles depending on the size of the wound. Leave a space on the edges of the wound equal to the thickness of a little finger. Insert one needle without thread into the edges of the skin. Using the edges of the peritoneum beneath the skin, from the inside, so that it penetrates on that side. The edges of the skin and peritoneum have been brought together, forming four layers. Then, using a doubled thread around the needle, draw it repeatedly from both sides until the edges of the wound are firmly united.
Then leave a space equal to the thickness of a finger and insert another needle.  Tie it with the thread as you did with the first needle. Continue this process with as many needles as necessary to completely close the wound. Use needles of medium thickness.
Because very thin needles quickly cut the flesh, and thick needles are also difficult to insert into the skin, therefore the needles should be of medium thickness.  The needle should not be inserted too close to the skin edge.
To prevent the flesh from tearing quickly and the wound from opening before healing.  Also, do not space the stitches too widely, lest the wound fail to heal. Then, cut the ends of the needles so that they do not injure the patient when sleeping, and provide a protective flap of linen fabric on each side to hold the needle ends.
Leave it until you know the wound has healed.  This method of stitching with needles is best suited for small surgical wounds, as they may be closed with just one or two needles, or thereabouts.
As for the method of stitching for the common type of wound, gather all four edges – the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum – in a single operation.  Use a needle with a thread of moderate thickness and then, after penetrating all four edges, bring the needle back out from the original entry point.
So that the thread is interwoven over the wound, producing a stitch like that used in securing bags.  Leave a space between each stitch equal to the width of a little finger. This is a stitch understood by everyone.
With this stitch, a surgical procedure was performed on a man whose abdomen was pierced by a knife.  The surgical opening was over a span of more than a span, and approximately two spans of the small intestine had protruded.  The wound was located in the middle of the abdomen.
He repositioned the intestines after they had been outside the wound for twenty-four hours.  The wound healed in approximately fifteen days, and he was treated until he recovered.  He subsequently lived many years and functioned normally in all aspects of his life.  Physicians had previously concluded that he would never recover.
It is remarkable that I did not use any ointment, as I was in a location where there were no medicines available.  I simply applied used cotton to the wound twice a day and treated it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the specific types of stitching, I will cite Galen's text: Begin the stitching from the skin.  Insert the needle from the outside to the inside.  Once the needle pierces the skin and the muscle running straight along the length of the abdomen.
Leave the peritoneum's edge on the side where you inserted the needle.  Pierce the other edge of the peritoneum from inside to outside.  Then pierce that same edge of the peritoneum again, this time from outside to inside.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side. Pierce its other edge from inside to outside.  While piercing the peritoneum, also pierce the edge of the mesentery on its side until you have pierced through entirely.
Then, starting from the same side, trace the edge of the peritoneum on the opposite side. Bring the needle out of the skin near it, then return it to that skin and trace the edge of the peritoneum on the opposite side, along with the edge of the mesentery.
Bring it out of the skin on its side. Do this again and again until you have stitched the entire wound in this way.  In general, this stitching is like the stitching of a furrier for furs.
By stitching the peritoneum once from one side with the skin's edge, and leaving it once from the other side until empty.  As for the second special type of stitching, which Galen also mentioned, this is his statement verbatim.
He means that you should stitch it in the way some healers stitch, by bringing each part to its corresponding counterpart, naturally.  That is, joining the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge and the edge of the mesentery to its other edge, in the manner I describe to you.
The needle should be inserted into the mesentery's edge nearest you, from the outside, and passed inside it alone, leaving the edges of the peritoneum. Then, return the needle and pass it from outside to inside in both edges of the peritoneum, and then return it again.
And pass it from inside to outside in the other edge of the mesentery on the opposite side. This type of stitching is better than the common, easy stitching previously mentioned. This is Galen's statement verbatim. Some have said that a two-needle method can also be used to close the abdomen. This means to insert a single thread into both, beginning by inserting the needle from your side and passing it to the other side; then insert the other needle from the opposite side, passing it back to your side, similar to the stitching of menders.
Know that if the tear is in the middle of the abdomen, its repair is more difficult than in other parts of the abdomen.  As for treating the wound, its treatment is like that of other wounds.  If you encounter the wound fresh with its blood, before the air changes it, and you have returned the intestines to their place and stitched and secured it, then apply the medicinal poultices.
If the wound has been affected by air, apply some available ointments until it suppurates, the sutures come loose, and the peritoneum and mesentery heal; then treat it like other wounds until it heals.
If you lack remedies, apply a poultice of worn cotton from the beginning of suppuration and change it twice daily, as I taught you, until it heals.  You do not need other treatment in most cases of simple wounds, unless you fear that the wound might cause the major organs to experience pain.
Therefore, it is necessary to dip soft wool in moderately warm oil or rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and armpits. If he feels pain or putrefaction in his bowels, which often occurs, then irrigate them with a lukewarm, astringent black drink.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the bowels and has become a penetrating wound into them. Know that the thick parts of the intestines heal more easily, while the thin parts are more difficult to heal.
As for the intestines known as the jejunum, it will not heal from any injury that occurs in it at all, due to the abundance of its large veins, the thinness of its tissue, and its proximity to the nervous system.  But if the extruded part of the wound is a small amount and you catch it fresh, then return it according to how you would return any other part of the intestines.
If it has been some time and it has turned green or black, you should tie it with a thread above the blackened area to prevent bleeding, because there are veins and arteries in the protruding part. Then cut what is below the ligature.
And make the ends of the thread hanging from beneath the wound, outside of it, so that you can easily pull it and remove it when the protruding part falls off and the wound suppurates.  Describing the wound that occurs in the intestines, but if a small perforation occurs in the intestines...
For in some cases it may be possible to repair it, as I have seen a man who had a spear wound in his abdomen, to the right of the stomach.  The wound became chronic and a fistula developed, from which feces and wind were discharged.  I treated it, however, without expecting it to heal. I continued treating him until he healed and the area closed up.  When I saw the area had healed, I feared that the patient might suffer further harm in his abdomen. However, absolutely no such harm occurred, and he remained in the best possible condition. Indeed, he ate, drank, had sexual relations, bathed, and engaged in physical exertion without difficulty.  Some experienced practitioners have stated that if a small wound occurs in the intestines, it should be treated in this way.
This is to take large ants by the heads, then bring together the edges of the wound.  An ant, with its mouth open, is placed on the edges of the wound. When it bites down and closes its mouth, its head is cut off; this adheres and does not separate. Then another ant is placed near the first.
Continue doing this with several ants, according to the size of the wound.  Then, stitch the wound closed.  These heads remain adhering to the intestines until they heal, and no harm occurs to the patient.  It is also possible to suture the intestines with fine thread.
The method for extracting the adhering animal tissue from the Egyptian animal, after it has entered a needle, is to take the end of this thread from the animal tissue, and carefully pull it out.  Then, a thin, spun linen thread is tied to the end, and that thread is then inserted into the needle. The thread of the animal tissue is used to suture the intestines, then returned to the cavity.  This method of suturing with ants and animal tissue is only based on hope and expectation.
However, if the tear is large and extensive, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, there is no remedy and no cure at all.
Chapter Eighty-Six.
On the treatment of the common cold and nasal polyps.
Know that any wound or tumor, if it becomes chronic, old, and ulcerated, and if it continuously discharges pus without stopping, is generally called a nasal polyp (nasor) in any organ, and we call it a cold (zakaam) and nasal polyp.
It is, in fact, a hard, dense, white mass with no pain, having a cavity like the hollow of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some have called it a feather, and it is sometimes moist, constantly discharging pus.  Sometimes the moisture may cease, and this moisture may be copious, or scant, thick, or thin.
I have previously stated in the classification of surgical cases that any ulcer that does not heal and in which flesh does not grow is due to one of nine causes; one is either a deficiency of blood in the body, or its poor quality, or because of hard flesh inside and on its edges preventing the growth of good flesh. Or it is due to the ulcer being excessively harmful and unclean, or that the ulcer itself is putrid, and the substance discharging from it is of poor quality; or that the treatment is inappropriate; or that a plague-like sickness has spread in the region; or, lastly, it is due to specific local factors.
Or it is due to the ulcer being excessively harmful and unclean, or that the ulcer itself is putrid, and the substance discharging from it is of poor quality; or that the treatment is inappropriate; or that a plague-like sickness has spread in the region; or, lastly, it is due to specific local factors. As observed in the city of Saragossa, where the maturation of diseases and the healing of tumors are slow and difficult, or due to one or several bones being present; I have detailed all these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medication in the classification.
Here, I must inform you about the treatment of fistulas and nasal discharge, which are treated manually. Know that fistulas may occur in all parts of the body; some lead to large veins, arteries, or nerves.
Or to the peritoneum, or to the intestines, or to the bladder, or to a rib, or to a vertebra of the spine, or to a joint of the numerous articulations, such as the hand and foot; and some lead close to a major organ or similar structure.
Those fistulas that follow this pattern are difficult to cure, and their treatment is arduous and fraught with ignorance.  If there are fistulas that do not reach these mentioned locations, I hope and anticipate success in their treatment.
To ascertain this, take a probe of copper or iron. If the fistula runs straight, probe it with this. If the fistula is winding, probe it with a thin lead probe, as lead is malleable.
If it enters and curves toward the winding path, if the fistula has numerous openings that the probe cannot locate, inject one of its openings. The moisture you inject will flow towards the other openings and drain.
Then, investigate in any way possible to determine if there is bone, nerve, or if the fistula's bottom is deep or shallow, or if it is a single fistula with multiple openings.  Ascertain all of this to the best of your ability through careful exploration and the patient's pain when you touch the area.
And similarly with the signs and causes of the swelling.  If you have established all of this accurately, then proceed to treatment with confidence.  Consider, if...
If the fistula is visible, close to the surface, or in a healthy location distant from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or any of the locations I have previously described, then surgically incise the fistula as described previously, and remove any hardened material, putrid flesh, and excess tissue within.
And treat any flesh or warts that grow in its opening, until it heals.  If the fistula's opening is deep and in a straight line, you should incise it deeply as possible, and then clean it of all its putrid flesh.
Then, use a finely woven applicator to insert strong medicines into the fistula's depths, which you can discern with an instrument. Do this repeatedly until the strong medication consumes all remaining corruption within the fistula's depth.
Then apply ointments that promote the growth of healthy tissue until it heals. If it does not heal with this, cauterize it as previously described. If the cause of the fistula is bone, and you are certain of this, probe and incise it as I have told you, unless something prevents you from doing so.
If it is a vein, nerve, or vital organ, as we have said, and the bone is exposed and has some corruption and blackness, remove the corruption until it disappears.  Then treat it with something that promotes healing until it heals; if it does not heal and the pus persists as it was before.
Know that you have not completely eradicated all corruption.  Expose everything affected again, thoroughly remove and cleanse it with all your effort, then apply a treatment.  If it heals, well and good.  If not, know that the corruption lies in a very deep recess, so expose it all again.
Thoroughly remove and cleanse it with all your effort. If the corrupt bone is small and you can pull it out, remove it with the delicate forceps suitable for that purpose.
If there are many bones, remove them all completely; do not leave anything behind.  If any ligaments obstruct them, and cutting them presents no risk, cut those ligaments and flesh if necessary, and expose the bones.
If you cannot remove the bones in your time, apply something to the wound to make it fester. Leave it for days until the flesh and ligaments surrounding the bones fester. Protect the wound, so that the opening you made in your work does not close up.
But if you fear that, apply a piece of cotton soaked in powdered sulfur with oil, or a piece of cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments.  When those ligaments fester and the bones become loose, they will be easy to remove and pull out.
If it is a single large bone, such as a tibia or femur, and only the surface has become corrupted, thoroughly remove the corruption until the blackness and decay are gone, then close the wound.  If a large part of it has corrupted and the corruption has reached the bone marrow, it must be scraped.
Cut it all the way to where the corruption ends. Then treat it until it joins together. I am telling you about a case of a man with a diseased leg to use as an example and help in your treatment. This man was young.
Around thirty years ago, a man experienced pain in his leg due to a condition originating within his body, until the material reached the leg and caused a large swelling.  There was no external cause.  Then, time passed and the doctors made mistakes until the swelling opened.
A large amount of material drained from it, and the treatment was poor, so the leg became diseased with many open sores, all emitting pus and bodily fluids.  A group of physicians treated it for about two years, but none of them was skilled in the art of surgery.
Until they came to me.  I saw his leg, with a great flow of material coming from those open sores.  The man's body was wasted and his complexion yellow. I inserted a probe into one of those sores, and it reached the bone. Then I investigated all the sores.
I found that some of them connected to each other from all sides of the leg. So I cut open one of those sores until I exposed some of the bone, which I found to be corrupted, decayed, blackened, putrefied, and riddled with holes, penetrating to the marrow. I then reported what I had discovered.
I removed the diseased bone, and I believe that there was no other corruption in the bone other than what I had cut away and reported. I then used healing medicines to close the wound for a long time, but it did not heal.  Then I again exposed the bone, further than the first exposure.
I found the corruption connected to the bone, and I reported what I saw of that corruption as well. Then I tried to close it, but it did not heal or join.  Then I exposed it again, and I kept cutting the bone piece by piece and trying to close it, but it would not heal, until I had cut away about a span of bone.
I extracted it with its marrow, then closed it with medicines, and it healed quickly and recovered.  This repetition of work and incision was necessary because of the patient's weakness, their low tolerance, and my fear for their life.  Because they were constantly experiencing severe fainting spells due to excessive vomiting.  They recovered completely.
Strong flesh grew in the place of the bone, and their condition in their body improved. Their strength returned, and they managed their affairs.  They experienced no injury in their walking that harmed them at all. If a bone that grew in a place in the body had broken.
It is necessary to expose it in this manner: take a bandage and tie it to the end of the protruding bone. Have someone pull it upwards. Use a second, thicker wool bandage.
Then tie it to the flesh under the bone, pulling the end to draw the flesh downwards.  Expose the flesh in the area you want to expose, so that the saw does not injure the flesh. Place a plank or board under the bone from below, securely.
Because if you do this, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the diseased bone.  The incision should be slightly above the area of disease, so that there is no disease within the bone cavity that does not appear on the surface, and you will not have to make the incision again.
If the bone is diseased but not protruding, and its parts are joined together, and the disease is in its center or in a part of it, expose the flesh from all sides. Then place the plank from below, then make the incision from the side where the disease is.
When finished with the incision, make another incision on the other side. Let the incision be a short distance from the disease, as we said. If the disease is in a joint, cut the diseased joint itself and make an incision in the bone where it connects on the other side.
If the disease is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy other than removing it. If the disease is in the hand or foot metacarpals, treatment is very difficult. However, you should scrape away the disease as it appears to you and remove and cleanse it in any way you can.
By any means that works for you, as long as you do not encounter a vein or nerve.  Know that the instruments, saws, and blades for cutting these bones vary greatly, depending on the bones' placement, position, thickness, thinness, size, smallness, hardness, and fragility. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare a tool suitable for each type of work.
Know that the work itself can indicate the type of tool needed, if you have extensive experience and knowledge of the craft.  A master craftsman, having witnessed various illnesses, understands this. He may invent tools for each illness, and I illustrate various tools at the end of this chapter.
Make them examples to follow and use as a guide for others, God willing.  Image of a saw:.

Chapter Eighty-seven.
On amputating limbs and spreading bones.
Limbs may become putrid, either from an external cause or from an internal one.  If you treat this corruption with medicine and the treatment is ineffective, and you see the decay spreading through the limb, unchecked by anything.
Then it is necessary to amputate the limb to the extent of the corruption, so that the patient may be saved from death or a great affliction greater than losing the limb.  A sign of this affliction is that the limb will become black, as if fire had burned it.
Or, after turning black, it will fester and spread to the adjacent limb, affecting the whole body.  Proceed with amputation immediately, also if the cause of the decay is the sting of some venomous creatures, such as the sea scorpion, snake, or centipede, and the like.
If the decay or sting is in the fingertip, amputate the finger; do not delay until the decay spreads to the hand. Likewise, if the decay has reached the hand, amputate it from the base of the forearm; do not delay until the decay spreads to the arm.
Likewise, if the decay has reached the arm, amputate the arm at the elbow joint.  If the decay has spread to the shoulder, do not amputate the shoulder, for that will cause the patient's death. Employ other treatments as your ability allows.
Likewise, do the same for the leg. If the decay reaches the toe, amputate the toe at one of the phalanges. If the decay reaches the foot, amputate the entire leg. If it reaches the knee, amputate the leg at the knee joint.
If the decay has reached above the knee, there is no remedy except to leave it and submit the patient to death.  To amputate or dissect the limb, tie a bandage below the place where you intend to cut, and tie another bandage above the place.
One attendant pulls the lower bandage downward, another the upper bandage upward. You, with a broad scalpel, separate the flesh between the bandages until all the flesh is exposed. Then cut or dissect; be sure to place linen cloths on all sides.
So that the saw does not touch the correct spot, causing the patient excessive pain and a hot swelling.  If bleeding occurs during your work, cauterize the spot quickly or apply some hemostatic agents, then return to your procedure until you are finished.
Then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. I will give you an example of a similar affliction in a man's leg.  This is the very affliction I am describing; namely, there was blackness in his leg accompanied by a burning sensation resembling fire.
This decay first appeared in his toe, and it spread throughout his entire foot. The man felt compelled to remove himself from the affliction when he saw the decay progressing through the limb, along with the intense pain and burning. He amputated it at the joint, and he recovered.
After a long time, the same decay appeared in his index finger. He sought me out, and I applied all the remedies I could to the hand.  After cleaning his body, the decay did not stop, and it continued to progress to the other finger.
Until the decay spread to his hand. He asked me to amputate his hand, but I refused, hoping to contain the decay.  I was also afraid that amputation would be fatal, as his strength was failing.  When he lost hope in me, he returned to his town.
I was later told that he ultimately amputated his entire hand and recovered.  I have related this story so that it might be a help in understanding and treating illnesses of this kind, and as a guide to be followed.
Chapter Eighty-eight.
On the treatment of abscesses and how to inject medicines into them.
If a tumor develops in a fleshy organ and persists for a considerable time, then bursts or opens, discharging all the matter within, leaving the area empty like a vessel.
The skin over it, like a torn cloth, may become thin, but it has not advanced to a point of affecting bone, nerve, or ligament.  Therefore, it deserves to be called an abscess, not a fistula, unless its duration extends to the point of causing damage to one of these structures.
In such a case, it is called a fistula or ulcer, and its treatment is to cut away all the skin, as it surrounds the area, particularly if it is thin and like torn cloth, and you are certain that it does not adhere to the affected area due to the damage.
If you anticipate the skin might adhere, and the damage has not reached that extent, and there is thickness of flesh in the skin, treat it with injections.  Observe the abscess; if it is large and the pus it produces has a foul odor, inject it with Egyptian medicine.
Which is vinegar, oil, honey, and verdigris, in equal parts mixed in a vessel and cooked over fire until the medicine thickens and reaches the consistency of honey, then take what you need of it, dilute it with water and honey, and inject it into the abscess.  Tighten the opening and leave the medicine within for about two hours.
Then, remove it by squeezing. Do this for several days until the abscess is cleansed and the foul odor is gone.  If this medicine is unavailable, it can be injected with ash water.  This involves taking ash from olive or oak wood, and pouring water over it.
Then filter it and inject it until you are certain that the abscess has been cleansed.  If the patient cannot tolerate Egyptian medicine or ash water, inject it with water and honey mixed with some powdered verdigris.
Or inject it with honey and drink mixed together, because honey cleanses and washes, and the drink adheres to the abscess, especially if the drink has a property of tightening and drying.  If you do this several times and are certain that the abscess's corruption has gone, then inject it with something that will promote the growth of flesh within.
For example, take some Palestinian ointment and dissolve it in rose oil and a astringent drink, or inject it with some other remedies and ointments whose properties we have described in the chapter on ointments.  If the opening of the abscess is narrow and does not allow the injection tube to fit.
If it is narrow, widen it slightly with an instrument, or place a thread soaked in Egyptian ointment or Syrian ointment within it until it widens. Also, if the opening is wide, bring its edges together with stitches, leaving only enough space for the injection tube to pass, without excess.
Also, if the opening from which pus is flowing is elevated, make an incision at the lowest point so that the pus flows downwards, because if pus is injected into the depths of the abscess, it prevents the flesh from growing there.
If you are unable to cut the abscess downwards as you desire, then apply pressure to the limb so that the pus flows out easily, as best you can, and make sure that no pus remains trapped within it.
Regarding the joining remedies that are applied to the abscess from the outside, and how to apply them: take one of the joining ointments similar to what you desire, and spread it onto a linen cloth; let the cloth cover the entire abscess. Then, with scissors, cut an opening slightly wider than the abscess's opening, and spread some soft ointment onto another piece of cloth, sized to fit the opening. Place it on top, positioned a little away from the abscess's opening to allow pus to easily flow out. Do not remove the larger cloth until later.
For several days, the one applied over the abscess opening is the one you remove at all times, to allow the collected pus to exit, and to see if the fluid draining is abundant or scant, mature or immature.
Also, examine the abscess site itself; does the patient feel pain, or not? Is there swelling, or not?  If your treatment continues in this way, and you observe that the material draining from the abscess opening is minimal,
Know that the abscess has adhered, or is about to adhere. In that case, treat it again tomorrow, or on the third day. When you treat the wound, replace the cloths and renew the ointment. If, after a long time, thin pus comes out of the abscess, do not despair of its adherence.
For his healing is near, because some people's wounds heal slowly, and others' heal quickly. If, after many days, unripe pus comes out of the abscess, know that it has not adhered.  If the abscess's adherence is delayed,
Then increase the drying of the medications, and let the medications be moist in consistency and dry in strength, like the palm ointment, if more galbanum is added to it. The best treatment is to use palm ointment.
It is treated by applying rose oil, then sprinkling moderate-strength old wine over it and mixing it with it. You can do the same if palm ointment is unavailable.  Honey, after being cooked until thick, can also be used.  Or, you can take myrrh, licorice, and kender, grind them, and sprinkle them over honey while it is on the fire. Then, apply it to a cloth and wrap it around the abscess, or apply the thickened cooked honey, sprinkle the medicines on it, place cloths over it, and bind it.
Long-stalked zeraun, iris root, asmanjounian, and kresna flour, and rue, used individually or mixed together, ground, sifted, and sprinkled over the described honey can be used.  If the abscess persists and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption within it.
If it has affected the bones, or other hard nerves or ligaments, treat it like a fistula as described above.
Eighty-ninth Chapter.
In the treatment of the inflamed and injured finger, the cutting of the superfluous finger, and the separation of the fused fingers.
Dahis is a profuse growth of flesh that arises beneath the toenail or fingernail, sometimes on other fingers. If left untreated, it grows into a swollen, inflamed tumor.
Or it may become corrupted and foul, even eating away the base of the nail, possibly destroying it entirely, and the corruption may reach the bone, causing a putrid odor and widening the end of the finger, turning it a dark color.  If treated as described in the section and your treatment is unsuccessful,
Then it is necessary to cut away all the excess flesh growing from the nail with a knife, and then cauterize the wound.  Cauterization in this case is very beneficial. If the bone and nail are sound,
If the corner of the nail has pressed the flesh inward, irritating and hurting it, a thin strip of material should be placed under the corner of the nail that is irritating the flesh, lifting it upwards, and the flesh should be gently cut away.
Apply the burning, corrosive medicines to the remaining flesh until it is all gone, then treat it with ointments until it heals. If the corruption has affected the bone, it must be cut away and removed, for it will never heal as long as there is corrupted bone.
If you see decay and corruption spreading in the finger, cut it off at one of the phalanges as previously described, then treat it until it heals. If the nail is injured or bruised and severe pain develops, it is necessary to first apply a bloodletting treatment.
Then split the nail with a sharp lancet in a slanted incision from top to bottom, taking care not to cut into the flesh beneath the nail, as this would cause severe pain to the affected area and may lead to the growth of excessive flesh in that spot.  Then treat the area to relieve pain.
As for the extra finger that sometimes grows on some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, or partly bony, or may have a nail, and some of it may grow from the base joint of some of the fingers.
Or it may grow on some of the finger bones, and those that grow on the finger bones themselves do not move, and those that grow at the finger joint may move.  Any that are purely fleshy can be easily removed; cut them at their base with a broad lancet.
However, those that grow at the base of the joint are difficult to treat, so avoid cutting them.  As for those that grow on the finger at one of the phalanges, it is necessary first to cut away the flesh in a circular manner around the bone, then to scrape away the bone with one of the appropriate chisels.
Then treat it until it heals.  As for the fusion that sometimes occurs between fingers, this often happens at birth, or during the healing of a wound, burn, or similar injury.  In such cases, it is necessary to cut through the fusion.
So that the fingers return to their natural position.  Then place between them a cloth or bandage soaked in rose oil, so that they do not fuse quickly, and separate them, or place a thin sheet of lead between them, until the area heals properly.
Likewise, if fusion occurs between a finger and the palm, cut through the fusion in a way that best restores the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
In the cutting of sinews.
Sinews are twisted, thick veins filled with black, dark matter, occurring in most body parts, most commonly in the legs, especially in the legs of porters, labourers, and carriers.  First, it is necessary to use vigorous rubbing of the body to remove the dark matter.
Then, bleed the sufferer at the basilic vein.  Treatment with iron is of two types: one, to cut and remove the dark blood; the other, to excise the sinew and remove it entirely.  As for cutting, the leg is first soaked in hot water.
Until the thick, turbid blood dissolves.  Then, the leg of the afflicted person is bound with a bandage from above the thigh to below the knee.  Then, the sinew is cut in one, two, or three wide cuts.
Then, remove the dark blood with your hand from the lower leg upwards and from above downwards until you remove the amount of blood you deem sufficient, or as much as the patient's strength allows.  Then, bandage it and instruct him to avoid foods that produce dark matter, and repeat vomiting and bloodletting.
When the veins are filled and that harms the patient, and as for its excision, it is done in this way: The patient's leg is shaved if it has much hair, then placed in a bath, and the leg is soaked in hot water until it becomes red and sweats, or vigorous exercise is performed.
If a bath is not available to heat the limb, then the skin is cut lengthwise opposite the sinew, either at the end near the knee or at the bottom near the heel. Then the skin is opened with hooks and the sinew is scraped from every side until it is palpable.
When it appears, you will see it red and firm.  Once it is free from the skin, it will appear like a tendon.  Then, insert a probe underneath it until it rises and emerges from the skin.  Fasten it with a smooth, blunt hook.  Then, cut another incision near that one, about the width of three fingers.
Then, scrape the skin off the sinew until it is visible.  Then, lift it up with the probe as you did before and fasten it with another hook, as you did at first.  Then, cut another incision, or several incisions if necessary.
Then, remove and cut it at the end of the incision, near the heel.  Then, pull it and remove it until it comes out of the second incision.  Then, pull it towards the incision above it.  Do this until you pull it out from the third incision, the highest of all the incisions. Once all of it is out, cut it.
If pulling and scraping are not successful, insert a needle with a strong, doubled thread, tie it, and pull.  Insert the probe underneath it and twist your hand in every direction until it comes out, taking care not to break it. If it breaks, it will be very difficult to remove.
Removing it all will cause harm to the patient.  Once removed completely, apply wool soaked in a potion and rose-oil or oil to the wound, and treat it until it heals.  If the abscess is tense, with twists and turns in various directions.
If it is not straight, as we said, it is necessary to make an incision on each side of its twists and turns.  Then, hang it with hooks until you remove it completely.  Take care, when making incisions, not to cut or injure the sinew, as this will make its removal difficult, and take care with all your effort.
Image of the incision used for the abscess.
Image of the blind hook.
It should not have a hook like other hooks, nor should its end be sharp so as not to injure the sinew.  It should be thick and smooth in its bend, because if it is thin, its thinness would cut the sinew.  Rather, it should have an advantage of thickness, as we said.
Chapter Ninety-one.
In the treatment of the civilian tendon.
This tendon arises in the legs in hot countries like al-Hijaz and the Arab countries, and in hot, sparsely populated countries, and it may arise in other parts of the body besides the legs, originating from a festering that occurs beneath the skin.
As it occurs within the bodies of snakes and worms, and the festering of the gourd, and the worm that develops between the skin and flesh, and a sign of the beginning of this tendon's occurrence is that a severe inflammation occurs in the leg, then the area becomes swollen, then the tendon begins to emerge from the location of that swelling.
As if the root of a plant or animal, and when its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing from one to two dirhams, and tied, and the lead left hanging from the leg.
Whenever anything emerges from it to the outside, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it extends a great deal, cut some off and wrap the rest, but do not cut it from its origin before it is all out, for if you cut it, it will contract and enter the flesh, causing a tumor, festering, and an undesirable ulcer at the site.
Therefore, it is necessary to treat it gently and pull it out little by little until the last part emerges and nothing remains of it in the body.  Sometimes, this tendon can be as long as five or ten cubits in some people.
I have heard that it has emerged from a man as long as twenty cubits. If it breaks during your treatment, insert a reed into its hole and press it firmly against the body for a long time, until all the material within it is discharged. Then, try to treat the affected area with medicines for several days.
Then treat it with the treatment for tumors. This tendon may have many branches, especially if it appears in a joint of the leg or the leg itself, producing many openings from which branches emerge. Treat it as we described in the division and as previously mentioned.
Chapter Ninety-Two.
On the incision for the worm that develops beneath the stomach, called the "cow's ailment.
This illness is called "cow's ailment" in some of our countries, because it frequently affects cattle. It is a small, single worm that develops between the skin and flesh, crawling throughout the body, both upward and downward.
It becomes apparent to the touch as it crawls from one limb to another, until it pierces the skin in a certain location and emerges. Its formation comes from the corruption of some humors, like worms, snakes, and the growth of gourds in the abdomen.
The danger from it is that if it crawls within the body and rises to the head, reaching the eye, it might rupture it and cause its loss. This often occurs.  Therefore, if you wish to treat it and remove it, it should be done when it is crawling and visible to the touch.
Therefore, one should bind tightly above and below it with a strong bandage, then make an incision over it and remove it. If it has burrowed into the flesh and you cannot find it, apply a cauterization with fire to the area until it is burned out.  The greatest danger is its potential to damage the eye, as we have stated.
If you see it has reached the head near the eye, bind tightly under it on the forehead, then incise over it and remove it.  The patient should maintain a regimen of purifying his body with laxatives for corrupt, foul humors, and be cautious about foods that produce putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-three.
On the incision of the disease known as the "nafar".
This disease, called "nafar" in our country, is a pain that appears in some organs and then spreads from one organ to another.  I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was summoned to a woman ill in a certain countryside.  She uncovered her arm, and I saw a slight swelling in the vein of her arm. After an hour, I saw that swelling crawl up her forearm, like a worm, ascending to her shoulder as quickly as mercury.
If it travels from one place to another, the pain disappears from that location and settles in the shoulder.  Then, after an hour, it spread throughout the rest of the body, eventually reaching the other arm.  She then told me that it was circling her entire body, as I observed.
I was astonished by the speed of its transfer from one organ to another.  I had not previously witnessed this disease in this form with my own eyes, although I had seen others where the pain moved from one organ to another. But I had never seen it in a woman as I saw it in this woman.
I attributed this only to the fact that the woman was from the countryside, with a dry body and exposed veins.  Therefore, this moving sensation was apparent to the touch.  It should not manifest in this way among those of comfort, whose bodies are moist and whose veins are hidden.
If you wish to treat it, and the sufferer feels pain, if it appears visibly as we described, then firmly compress above and below it with speed, and cut open the area until that confined wind is expelled.
If you do not see it, treat it by shaking the body and dispersing the winds, such as rotten seed and  skabinj seed, and similar medicines.
Chapter Ninety-Four.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and according to the places on the body where they are lodged.  Regarding their differences by type, some are large, some small; some are hollow glass, some solid glass; some have three angles, some four.
Some have tongues, some have shards.  As for those that differ according to the organs they lodge in, there are two types:  Either the organs are major hollow organs like the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like. If an arrow strikes one of these organs, and you see the signs of death that I will describe to you later, then you should avoid extracting the arrow, for death will usually befall the patient. And if these bad signs do not appear, and the arrow is not lodged deep within the organ, then extract it and treat the wound.  And of the signs of the brain, if an arrow strikes it and pierces the bone and injures the membrane covering the brain, then severe headache, dizziness, and fainting may result.
And if these bad signs do not appear, and the arrow is not lodged deep within the organ, then extract it and treat the wound.  And of the signs of the brain, if an arrow strikes it and pierces the bone and injures the membrane covering the brain, then severe headache, dizziness, and fainting may result. Redness in the eyes and tongue, spasms, confusion, vomiting, and perhaps bleeding from the nostrils or ears.  Perhaps speech is lost, and the voice gone, and a white, porridge-like moisture may emerge from the wound, and liquid like meat juice flows from it.
If these signs appear, then refrain from treating the patient and extracting the arrow if it has not already been removed.  As for the signs of an arrow striking the heart, if it is close to the left breast and you feel it as though it has lodged in something solid rather than empty.
And perhaps the arrow has a movement like a pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, followed by coldness in the extremities, cold sweat, and fainting.  Know then that death is inevitable.  And the sign of an arrow wounding the lung is the discharge of frothy blood from the wound.
The vessels adjacent to the neck swell, and the patient's complexion changes, breathing becomes labored, and he craves inhaled cold air. If the arrow strikes the diaphragm in the chest, it will be near the small ribs, and breathing will be heavy with intense pain.
And he sighs and all the muscles of the shoulders move.  If the arrow strikes the liver, intense pain follows, and blood resembling the liver in its redness comes from the wound. If the arrow strikes the stomach, undigested food may emerge from the wound.
And his condition is obvious. If the arrow strikes the abdomen, becomes lodged, and something of the stool, or of the intestines or bowels, is discharged from the wound, there is no hope for treatment nor for extracting the arrow. If the arrow is lodged in the bladder and urine is discharged.
If something protrudes outwards and the patient's pain intensifies, know that he is doomed.  As for other organs such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, spine, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar organs, they are mostly spared.
Unless the arrow strikes an artery or nerve, and unless the arrow is poisoned, I will tell you something I have witnessed concerning these arrows so you might draw upon it for your treatment.  An arrow struck a man in the root of his nose, at the bridge of his eye.
I extracted it from the other side, under the earlobe, and he recovered without harm to his eye.  I extracted another arrow for a Jew that had struck his earlobe, under the lower eyelid; the arrow had disappeared, and only its small tip, which attaches to the shaft, remained.
It was a large arrow from a composite bow, with square, smooth iron, and no ears. The Jew recovered without harm to his eye.  I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian, and the arrow was Arab.
It had ears, and I split it open between the windpipes; it had pierced his throat. I gently worked at it until I extracted it. The Christian was spared and recovered.  I extracted another arrow from a man's abdomen, and we thought he would die from it.
After about thirty days, or thereabouts, without any change in his condition, I split the arrow and worked at it, extracting it. He recovered and suffered no further harm. I also saw a man with an arrow in his back, and the wound had closed over it.
Then, after seven years, the arrow emerged from the base of his thigh.  I saw a woman with an arrow in her abdomen; the wound had closed over it, and the arrow remained.  Her condition did not change, and she experienced no problems with her normal activities.
I saw another man with an arrow in his face; the wound had closed, and he experienced little pain.  Many such cases exist. I extracted an arrow from a Sultan's officer who had been struck in the middle of his nose, slightly deviated to the right side.
The arrowhead was completely gone.  I was called to treat him three days after the arrow struck.  I found the arrow wound very narrow.  I probed it with a thin probe and felt nothing.  He was experiencing a prickling and pain under his ear on the right side.
I hoped that the prickling was from the arrowhead, so I dressed the area with a poultice that promoted drawing out and maturation, hoping the area would swell and show a sign of the arrowhead so I could open it.  But nothing happened to indicate the arrowhead had reached that spot.
I continued applying the poultice for many days, but nothing happened.  The wound eventually healed, and the patient despaired of removing the arrow.  One day, he felt the arrow inside his nose, so he informed me.  I then applied strong, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it, feeling the thin arrowhead, which was stuck to the wood.  Then, I increased the application of the strong medicine to open the wound further until the arrowhead was visible.  I was with him for about four months.
Then, when the wound widened and I could insert forceps, I pulled and moved it, but it would not come out.  I continued to gently manipulate it with various instruments until I grasped it firmly with precise forceps, as their depiction is found at the end of this chapter.
The physicians had judged that his nasal cartilage could not be repaired, but I repaired it.  The wound healed, and the patient recovered completely, with no further problems.  I am telling you how to remove some arrows so that you can use this as a model and guide for what I have not mentioned.
Because the parts of this craft and its details cannot be grasped by description or encompassed by a book.  Instead, the skillful artisan measures the little by the much, and what is present by what is absent, and devises a new method and a new instrument for unusual cases.
If I descend from this craft, I say that arrows are extracted from the members they entered in two ways: either by pulling from the point of entry, or from the opposite side, which is the way out of where they entered.
Either the arrow is protruding into fleshy tissue, and it is pulled out.  If it does not come out at its time of impact, you should leave it for a few days until the surrounding flesh rots and its removal is easier.  The same applies if it has lodged in bone.
If it does not come out, leave it for several days and try pulling and moving it daily. It will come out. If after several days it still does not come out, you should pierce around the arrow in the same bone from all sides with a fine awl until you widen the space for the arrow and then pull it out.
If the arrow has lodged in the skull bone and has penetrated one of the brain ventricles, and some of the symptoms I mentioned have appeared, then refrain from pulling the arrow out and leave it until its condition improves after a few days.
Then proceed with pulling out the arrow. If it is deeply lodged and does not come out easily, use the awls around the arrow as described. Then treat the area until it heals.  However, if the arrow has concealed itself in some part of the body, and it is hidden from sight, search for it with a probe.  If you feel it, pull it out using suitable instruments. If you are unable to remove it due to the narrow wound and the deep penetration of the arrow, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way.
then widen the wound until you can grasp the arrow and remove it.  If it has two prongs, grasp them and remove the flesh that has become entangled in them, from all sides, using every possible technique.  If you are unable to remove the flesh, break the prongs and twist them until you release them.
When attempting to remove the arrow from any location, use twisting motions with forceps in all directions to extract it. Be extremely gentle, so the arrow does not break, making it harder to remove. If you are unable to remove it after some time.
Leave it for a few days until the surrounding flesh around it becomes infected, then try again; it will then be easier to remove. If bleeding occurs, use the treatments mentioned in the relevant section.  Take great care to avoid cutting any veins, nerves, or tendons, and employ all possible techniques.
Use every means possible to remove the arrow, with gentleness, patience, and firmness, as described.  When pulling the arrow out, it is best to position the afflicted area in the same posture it was in when the arrow was lodged.
If that is not possible, use whatever method you can.  As for an arrow that emerges from the opposite side, either something of it will protrude outward, or you will feel the arrowhead near the surface of the skin, protruding.
Make an incision over it, large enough for the forceps to grip, then pull it out; it will be easier to remove. If it is lodged in bone, twist your hand in a circular motion to impact the arrow and enlarge the space for it to emerge, then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for several days and try again until it comes out.  If the arrow's shaft remains, push it out with it. If the shaft has broken off and you wish to use a pushing instrument, insert a hollow instrument into the arrowhead's shaft, and then push it out using that.
If the arrow is hollow, push it out using an instrument that fits into the hollow.  The arrow will thus be easier to remove. If the arrow is poisoned, you should cauterize all the flesh affected by the poison if possible, then treat it appropriately.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, abdomen, bladder, or flank, and is close enough to be felt with a probe, and you can make an incision, then incise, being careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then suture the wound if necessary, and treat it until it heals.  Image of forceps used to extract arrows:
Its tips are like the beak of a bird, crafted like a file, so that if it grasps an arrow or anything, it will not let go.  Different sizes, large, small, and medium, can be made, depending on the size of the arrow, the size of the wound, and its width.
Image of the hollow cannon.
Image of the solid cannon.
This is solid, like a rod, so that it can easily enter a hollow arrow and push it out.
Chapter Ninety-five.
On the opening of veins.
The veins commonly opened in the body are thirty. Sixteen of them are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the *ḥassisīn*; the two arteries in the temples, visible; and the two veins at the corners of the eyes, known as the *nāẓirīn*.
