The vein that stands up in the middle of the forehead, the vein at the tip of the nose, the two veins in the neck, the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth, the two veins in the lower lip, and these veins known as the *jayārak*, and the two veins under the tongue.
The veins opened in the arm and hand are five. One is the *qayfāl*, which is on the outer side; the common people call it the vein of the head. The *akḥal*, which is the middle vein, is composed of a branch of the *bāsaliq* and a branch of the *qayfāl*; the common people call it the vein of the body.
The *bāsaliq*, which is located on the inner side, is also called the *ibṭī*; the common people call it the vein of the abdomen and the cord of the arm. It is located on the *zanad* (the forearm), where it attaches.  It is the one that clearly emerges above the thumb, and the *ʾasīlīm*.
It is the vein between the little finger and the ring finger, having two branches. In the leg and foot, there are three veins. One is located below the bend of the knee, on the outer side; the second is the *ṣāfin*, located near the heel on the inner side; and the vein of the *nisā*, located near the heel on the outer side.
In the other leg, there are three veins like them.  As for the two veins behind the ears, their opening is beneficial for chronic colds, migraines, scabies, and chronic, stubborn head sores; and their opening will be as I describe.
To do this, shave the patient's head and rub its back in the area of the two veins with a rough cloth, rubbing well.  Then, the patient's neck is bound with a turban until the veins appear behind the ears in the low parts of the head.  Feel for them with your finger, and where you feel a pulse under your finger, mark the spot with ink.
Then take a scalpel, which is known as a lancet, and insert it under the vein in the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone. Then lift the vein and skin upwards and cut the vein with the skin cleanly, making the cut approximately the length of two clenched fingers.
Or as much blood as desired is drawn.  Then, the vessels are tied with ligatures and left until healed.  In the beginning of the book, the cutting and burning of these vessels was discussed.  As for the arteries in the temples, their opening is beneficial for chronic migraines, severe headaches, chronic eye inflammation, and sudden, severe eye discharge.
Their opening, as I describe to you, involves binding the patient's neck with a turban until the veins become clearly visible and their pulse is felt under your finger.  At that point, mark the spot with ink.  Then lift the skin above the vein using your index finger.
Insert the lancet from below, lift the vein upwards, and sever it as you did with the other veins.  Draw off the amount of blood needed.  Then, loosen the patient's neck binding and place your finger on the vein for an hour.
Then place a pledget and a ligature on it, and tie tightly from above.  Leave it until it heals.  Their mention, cutting and draining was discussed previously in the beginning of the book.  As for the opening of the forehead vein, its benefit comes after the opening of the temporal veins for chronic facial ailments such as sores, ulcers, and chronic redness.
Its opening, as I describe to you, involves binding the patient's neck with a turban until the vein appears.  Then, take the instrument called the lancet.  This is its image:
Place the pointed end of the lancet on the vein, and strike from above with a mallet or similar object. Allow the blood to flow as desired. Then, release the patient's neck binding and apply a bandage until it heals.
It can be opened with a broad lancet, but it should not be as sharp as other lancets, rather, it should have a slightly broad tip.  Open it by twisting, because the bone is close.  A narrow lancet may break there.
As for the veins near the eye sockets, they are beneficial for eye ailments such as scabies, redness, and other facial diseases.  As for their opening, the patient should bind their neck with a turban.  Then, open them while standing at the patient's head. The incision should be slightly oblique, lengthwise, with a narrow, slightly broad lancet.
If the area has little flesh, a narrow lancet may break. Then, draw the required blood, place a bandage on them, and bind them for one night.  Then, remove the bandage.  As for opening the nasal vein, it is beneficial for severe fevers.
Severe headaches, and facial diseases such as the red swelling that appears on the nose, especially if chronic, are also treated by opening the veins.  To open the vein, bind the patient's neck, hold the patient's nose with your left hand, and take a long, narrow lancet.
Insert it into the middle of the nostril itself, between the nasal septum, in a straight line, because the vein is not apparent there to the touch.  The blood will immediately appear.  You should press the lancet in slightly, draw the needed blood, then bandage it for one night. It will quickly clot.
As for the jugular veins, opening them is beneficial for shortness of breath, the onset of leprosy, and black humoral diseases that appear on the skin's surface, such as black vitiligo, scabies, and bad sores and ulcers.  Their opening is performed by binding the patient's neck beneath them with a bandage, and the surgeon stands at the patient's head.
The patient sits on a chair, then the vein is opened lengthwise, with a somewhat wide incision. Then, draw a moderate amount of blood, or as much as needed.  Then do the same with the other vein, and then untie the bandage.
Pull the veins with a moderate pressure so the patient does not suffocate, and leave it until the next day. Then the wound will heal.  As for the veins of the cheeks, opening them after opening the cephalic vein is beneficial for sores in the mouth, gum disease, bad sores, chapped lips, and bad sores.
(Those) which are in the nose and around it.  Their opening is done by having the patient sit in front of you, tying their neck with a turban, then turning their lips and looking for the veins; one is seen to the right of the lip, the other to the left.  Note their darkness.
Also, there are thin, dark veins around them.  Cut them cleanly off. If it is difficult and you are unsure what they are, aim for cutting the largest and most obvious ones.  Likewise, do the same with the veins on the lower lip.
As for the two veins under the tongue, opening them after opening the cephalic vein is beneficial for the constrictions that occur in the throat from diseases of the palate and diseases of the mouth.  How to open them: have the patient sit before you facing the sun, lift their tongue, and look under the tongue on one side for a vein, and on the other side for a vein. Their color will be dark.
Open them, but be careful not to cut them deeply, for there are arteries beneath them, and excessive bleeding might result from those arteries.  As for the three veins that are opened at the elbow, these are the ones customarily opened among people. Opening them can be done in two ways.
Either by piercing with a wide, comfortable lancet, or with a lancet of a knife type (the incision),  [and this is its image]. Image of the wide, comfortable lancet:
It should be wide, as you see, to be suitable for opening hollow, full, prominent, visible, thick veins containing thick, dark blood.  And this is the image of the olive-shaped lancet:
This lancet is narrower and has a thinner tip, suitable for opening thin veins containing thin, yellowish blood.  And this is the image of the incision lancet:
This incision lancet, suitable for cutting, comes in various widths and thicknesses, depending on the width or narrowness of the veins.  One can infer other things from this, and it is well-known among artisans.
As for the jugular vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of its incision is that it draws blood from the ailments that are under the throat and neck, from the chest and abdomen.  The phlebotomist must be cautious when performing the incision, and be careful of the underlying artery, as if he errs and deepens the incision, he will sever that artery.
This will cause bleeding, therefore it is necessary that its incision not be made with a lancet for piercing, but rather an incision with the incision lancet.  If the jugular vein is not clearly visible, one must avoid it and turn to another vein or seek out a branch of it.
Or you may incise the arm's vein instead, for it is clear, and cut it with the incision lancet, as we have said. If you wish to incise it directly, it is necessary, before tightening the arm, to palpate the area in order to identify the pulse point, and then mark it with ink.
Then tie the arm and incise the vein with an oblique incision with the incision lancet, as we said, and take care that the blow be distant from the artery. When you see, upon tightening the bandage, a puff in the area you marked with ink, that puff is the swelling of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see the blood during the phlebotomy surging like the urine of a child, and the blood is thin and red, know that it is arterial blood. In that case, immediately place your finger upon it for a long time, then remove your finger. If the bleeding stops, it will often stop, then tighten the arm.
Leave it be, and warn the patient against neglecting it.  Let it be on a bandage and do not move it for days until it heals. If the bleeding does not stop, and you are overwhelmed, and no remedy is available to you at the time, sever the artery if it is visible to you, for its ends will contract and the blood will cease.
Or take a pistachio shell, split it, take one half, and tie it firmly over the vein with bandages and splints until the next day. If the blood stops, then do so. Otherwise, treat it with the previously mentioned application of sharp cauterizing devices for the bleeding. Stopping the blood flow is not difficult in most cases.
Because of the smallness of the wound and the firmness of the bandage on the arm, understand this.  As for the dark vein, the benefit of its incision is to draw blood from the top of the head and lower part of the body, because it is composed of a branch from the basilic vein and a branch from the cephalic vein, as we have said.
The phlebotomist should be careful with his incision, for there are nerves beneath.  If he increases the depth of the lancet's incision and strikes a nerve, numbness will result, which is difficult to cure, and perhaps it will not heal at all. This nerve is often palpable.
If in some people it is hidden and thin and cannot be seen, then your incision should be a shallow cut with a lancet, and avoid the nerve as best you can.  If the vein is between two nerves, make a lengthwise incision of the vein. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of its incision is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for eye diseases, and if you wish to incise this vein, it should be done with incisions using the olive-shaped lancet or the broad rue-shaped lancet, as it is the safest of all veins because it has neither artery nor nerve beneath it.
However, when making the incision, you should avoid striking the head of the muscle with the lancet, and seek the soft spot.  If it is not struck on the first attempt, it will not be harmed if incised again multiple times. However, it may swell in some people if not incised on the first try.
But that swelling will not harm it.  As for how to perform the incision, its symptoms, and what should be done to repair it, first you should know that phlebotomy is used either for maintaining health alone.
Or, it is used for diseases. The one used for maintaining health and preventing diseases should be done on one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the cephalic, the acubal, and the basilic.  The incision should be performed at the beginning of spring, when signs of fullness appear.
The incision should be made on a Sunday or Tuesday after three hours of daylight have passed.  As for the incision used for diseases, there is no fixed time, but whenever needed and necessary, whether it be night or day.
At any hour and time.  Boys should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age, nor should elderly people who have passed sixty years.  If someone intends to perform phlebotomy for any reason...
He should cleanse his intestines before the incision with a gentle enema if there is a lot of retained phlegm, so that foul impurities from the intestines are not drawn into the veins during the incision, harming the main organs.  A feverish person, a drunkard, and a person under the influence of alcohol should not be bled until these conditions have passed.
Further, phlebotomy should be avoided following dysentery, vomiting, fever, excessive sexual activity, exertion, exercise, late nights, fasting, and everything that weakens the body or mind.  Then, if the blood is thick, one should consider thinning the humors beforehand.
By means of foods, drinks, and medicines, if possible. Then he should take a bath, if there is no impediment, or engage in some exercise, so that the blood is thinned.  He should perform the incision in the middle of the day, as we stated, and aim to clear his chest that day of all bad psychological symptoms.
Such as worries, anger, and fear, and from all bodily ailments such as excessive fatigue and exhaustion, sexual intercourse, and the like.  He should prepare his surroundings with items people typically use, such as fragrant herbs, perfumes, and amusements, to the extent possible for each individual.
Then the phlebotomist should sit on a higher cushion than the patient, then extend the arm and rub it with his hand two or three times, then tighten the bandage with the ligature and twist it two or three times. The tightening should be moderate.
Because if the tightening is not moderate, either excessive tightening will prevent the flow of blood, or if it is too loose, it will also prevent the flow of blood.  Then, after the tightening, the patient should rub his hands together.
Until the veins swell and are clearly visible to the touch.  Then the phlebotomist should wipe the lancet with a small amount of old oil, specifically. Then he should place his left index finger on the same vein slightly below the spot where he wants to make the incision, so as not to injure the vein. Because some veins are like tendons; they retract during phlebotomy. Others are filled with air, so when the lancet is placed on them, they recede beneath the lancet and deceive the phlebotomist, and the lancet does not open the vein.  If it does open the vein, it will only be a narrow opening.
Therefore, the phlebotomist must be sure and careful in all of these matters. Then, he should lower the lancet; if the vein opens on that first attempt, then good.  Otherwise, he should try again slightly below or above that spot, quickly if the area hasn't swelled up.
If the patient swells or complains, leave him for a day or two, and do not tighten the bandage, as it may cause a hot swelling.  He should not enter a bathhouse.  Then, if he wishes, he can have the phlebotomy repeated.  If the lancet penetrated and the opening was small, and the flow of blood was thin.
And fearing that not the desired amount of blood will be released, reposition the lancet in the same puncture, gently and straight, and enlarge the opening slightly.  Do this quickly before the area swells, as in many people, the area may swell with a small opening.
If you see it has swollen, do not repeat the procedure on it at all, for it will not help you.  Apply some clarified oil to it.  It facilitates the flow of blood and is better in this area than oil itself and other ointments as well.
Use clarified oil in all your phlebotomies when blood flow is difficult.  The specific remedy (and the other one) may also do this if applied to the spot; the blood will become thinner and dissolve if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the phlebotomy site, which often happens, especially in someone who has not been bled before or whose vein opening was small, quickly apply a sponge soaked in warm salt water to the swelling and bandage it for an hour.  It will dissolve.
This should be done after the blood has completely exited the vein itself, or another vein.  If any discoloration, black or green, remains in the area after a few days, it does not harm.  If you wish, apply some poultices of gum arabic and aloe mixed.
Or something from the juice of the fūdinj and the like.  A swelling and protuberance often occur when phlebotomizing the basilic vein.  Place your hand on it. If you find it pulsating when you press on it, that is a bad protuberance, so beware of applying any of the things we mentioned.
For it might bleed arterial blood.  However, bandage it with something constricting to solidify the area, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.  One should extract blood from each person according to their strength and the prevailing color of their blood.
If the blood is dark, let it flow until it turns red.  Similarly, if you see it thick, let it flow until it thins.  Likewise, if it is sharp, let it flow until its sharpness subsides.  It is appropriate for those who are strong and full and require a single, immediate bloodletting.
To widen the vein's incision, and make the lancet wide.  Conversely, for the weak, narrow it, and make the incision in stages.  The opening should be narrow. The best instrument for venesection is one that is angled, slanted, or slit, not pointed.
This type of venesection is free from bleeding and cutting nerves. It is better and safer than venesection across or lengthwise.  If someone experiences fainting during venesection, it is necessary to give them something to eat before the procedure, such as bread soaked in pomegranate juice or vinegar.
If he is feverish and blood is drawn three or four times, if he has a cold temperament, then let him eat bread soaked in pomegranate juice, or honeyed drink, or perfumed with spices, or a fragrant herb drink before the venesection.
If fainting occurs during venesection and is due to excessive blood loss, then he should be given meat broth and a thin herbal drink.  Perfume with rosewater should be used, and the chest should be rinsed with it.  Use all the methods mentioned in the section on fainting.
If the venesection is for expectoration, and if someone wishes to relieve their arm and release their blood a second time, then for someone whose venesection was for copious expectoration and whose strength is weak, the blood should be released little by little, according to their strength, over several days.
And if someone wishes to relieve their arm and release their blood a second time, and their body is strong, then let them do so after seven or nine hours from the first venesection.  And if someone wants to draw blood from their body to the opposite side from where it has accumulated, then let them be relieved on the second or third day.
And if a person has a lot of blood in their body, which has become hot and intense, causing fever, then blood should be drawn in one single, large quantity, and the opening should be widened until fainting occurs, after having carefully considered all the conditions for venesection. And to place your hand on his pulse while the blood flows, so that death does not occur in place of fainting, as this often happens if the surgeon is ignorant and carelessness occurs.  And this should not be done if you intend to release the arm and draw the blood again.
If the opening of the vein has closed and the blood is difficult to draw out, then vigorously probing it or twisting it forcefully is very bad.  Rather, either leave it until it can be opened again, or use the scalpel blade to remove the clotted blood from the opening of the vein.
Or apply something of salt dissolved in water, or something of the antidote *al-fāriq* or *al-shaxznāyā*, and gently probe it until the blood flows.  If the vein has become swollen, leave it.
Do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If necessity requires drawing blood again, then either draw blood above that spot, or draw blood in the other arm or from another vein.
As for drawing blood from the arm's vein, it is used as a substitute for the *akhl* and *bāsiliq* veins if these are unavailable or hidden, because it is composed of both of them.  The method for drawing blood from it is that the patient should immerse his hand in hot water until the forearm turns red and the vein becomes clearly visible. Then tie it with a bandage a little tightly, with moderate pressure.
Then draw blood from the vein a little off center, neither across nor lengthwise, and make the opening wide.  Make the incision slightly above the wrist joint.  If the blood is difficult to draw, re-immerse the hand in the hot water basin and allow the blood to flow into the water.
Until you have obtained your desired amount.  If it is summertime, you may dispense with re-immersion in the hot water.  Most often, drawing blood into hot water is done in winter.  Drawing blood from this vein is safer than from any other vein. Because there is no vital vein or nerve beneath it.  As for drawing blood from the *asīlim* vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments. The method for drawing blood from it is to tie the wrist with a bandage or your hand after immersing it in hot water until the vein swells.
Because there is no vital vein or nerve beneath it.  As for drawing blood from the *asīlim* vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments. The method for drawing blood from it is to tie the wrist with a bandage or your hand after immersing it in hot water until the vein swells. And it becomes very apparent to the touch.  Then draw blood from it, a little off-center.  Even if you sever it completely, it will not harm him.  Be cautious; do not use the lancet too forcefully.  Below it lies the nerve of the fingers, and the area is bare of flesh. Then return the hand to the hot water.
And allow the blood to flow. If you do not return it to the hot water, the blood will clot in the opening of the vein and will cease to flow.  When you have removed the desired amount of blood, apply grease and salt to the vein to prevent rapid clotting.
And likewise, this must be done with every narrow branch.  As for the benefit of drawing blood from the left hand, it is beneficial for spleen ailments.  You should perform this procedure in the same way as you did for the second vein.  As for drawing blood from the saphenous vein, its benefit is for diseases in the lower body, such as ailments of the uterus.
And amenorrhea, kidney ailments, chronic sores of the thighs and legs, and similar ailments.  The method for drawing blood from it is to immerse the leg in hot water and apply rubbing until the veins become dilated. Then tie above the joint of the leg with a bandage. The vein is located near the heel, visible near the big toe.
From it, many branches extend throughout the leg.  Draw blood from the widest branch, or near the heel where they join, for this is the best and safest approach. If you draw blood on the front of the leg, be cautious of the nerves located beneath the skin on the front of the leg.
Make your incision with a twisting motion as if you were about to sever it, and the lancet should be sharp. If blood does not flow readily, return his leg to the hot water and allow the blood to flow until it is emptied. If the surgeon misses the vein on the first attempt.
Then reposition the incision slightly higher, for the location is safe, and no harm need be feared if the nerve is avoided, as we stated.  Do likewise with the saphenous vein on the other leg. As for the femoral vein, its location is, as we said, near the heel, on the outer side.
Its benefit is for hip pain, if it is due to hot blood. The method for drawing blood is to have the patient enter a bath and accelerate the process.  Tie the leg from the hip to above the heel with a thin, long bandage, with four fingers width of the bandage.  Only then will it become apparent.
If it appears, draw blood in any case, though twisting is preferable. Alternatively, sever it by cutting or slitting; its location is safe.  In most people, it is very hidden, if you cannot find it.
If it does not appear at all to the touch, draw blood from a branch of it, which appears on the back of the foot near the little and ring fingers.  Avoid the nerves and draw off the amount of blood you desire.
Then loosen the bandage, place a piece of cotton on the incision site, and bandage the area tightly.  It will quickly heal.
Chapter Ninety-six.
On cupping and its use.
Cupping vessels can be made of horn, wood, copper, or glass.  Cupping can be done in two ways: one with a mark/incision to draw blood, and the other without a mark.
This cupping without a mark is also twofold.
Cupping of the sides of the neck, both sides, and the chin cupping, below the lower jaw of the mouth, and shoulder cuppings, and sacrum cupping on the tailbone, and elbow cuppings, which are the middle of the arms, and leg cuppings, and heel cuppings.
Cupping draws blood from the thin, dispersed veins within the flesh.  Therefore, it does not deplete strength like venesection.  Cupping, whether with or without fire, should not be used in any disease of fullness until the entire body is emptied.
If cupping is needed, whether for a disease or habitually, we use it at the beginning, end, and middle of the month, and at any time.
For some people, when blood increases to the point that cupping is needed, they experience heaviness and headaches in their head.  Some have fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck.  Some have itching in their face, temples, and darkness and heaviness in their eyes. Some scratch the area where they were cupped.
Some laugh excessively. Some find the taste of blood in their mouths, and their gums swell and they spit blood. Some sleep excessively. Some see blood, redness, slain men, and wounds, and similar things in their dreams. Whenever we see any of this, ...
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we then order cupping after two or three hours have passed of the day.  The benefit of the “quick cupping” is for heaviness in the head and water that flows down to the eyes.
However, this should be after the body has emptied itself, and this cupping can be a substitute for venesection of the temples.  Caution should be taken that those who are cold-headed or have a cold should not use it, as it would greatly harm them.
Therefore, the elderly and those with cold diseases in their head should not use it, and those who habitually use it will be afflicted with forgetfulness.
Therefore, the cupper should lower the cupping instrument slightly to avoid the onset of forgetfulness.  As for cupping the shoulder, it is a substitute for venesection of the temples and the veins of the eye.
Therefore, it is helpful for asthma, shortness of breath, tearing of the respiratory system, cough, and fullness.  The cupping of the shoulder should be slightly elevated, because if it is lowered, it will generate weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the temples, it is helpful for pains in the head, ophthalmia, migraine, constriction, and pain at the roots of the teeth. It is a substitute for venesection of the veins of the eye. The cupper should be instructed not to insert the instrument deeply to avoid cutting an artery and causing bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it is helpful for sores in the mouth, gum disease, and similar diseases in the mouth.  It may take the place of venesection of the veins in the lips.  As for cupping the shoulders, it is helpful for palpitations resulting from fullness and heat.
As for cupping the forearm, it is helpful for what venesection of the three veins—the veins of the eye, the temples, and the veins of the throat—is helpful, because it draws blood from all those delicate veins in the flesh and draws those delicate veins from other, thicker veins until the drawing reaches the three thick veins.
The cupper should be instructed not to press too hard with the instrument, because the area lacks flesh and has nerves and arteries beneath.  As for a single cupping on the coccyx, it is helpful for hemorrhoids and sores of the lower parts.
The cupper should be instructed to use a large, brass cupping vessel, because the area needs strong suction, and glass cupping vessels may break.  A considerable amount of pressure should be applied.  As for cupping the legs, it clearly reduces fullness.
Because it draws blood from the entire body and is helpful for chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder; it causes menstruation and is helpful for boils and abscesses; it acts as a substitute for venesection of the groin and foot. However, it greatly exhausts the body, and fainting occurs in most people.  Cupping of the foot is similar in its benefits to cupping of the legs.
The method of applying cupping is to first place an empty cupping vessel and apply moderate suction.  Do not prolong the application of the cupping vessels, but rather apply and remove them quickly to draw the humors to the area evenly.  Repeat this process until the area turns red and opens.
When the redness of blood appears, then apply pressure and resume suction gradually.  Then, observe the condition of the body.  For those with thin, porous flesh, a single application of pressure is sufficient to prevent the area from becoming ulcerated.
The cupper should widen the pressure, deepen it slightly, and adjust the suction with gentle, controlled movement. If the blood is thick, then pressure should be applied twice; the first time to open a path for the thin and watery blood, and the second to remove the thick blood.
If the blood is very thick, then apply pressure a third time to achieve the desired result.  Generally, if one wishes to remove a small amount of blood, a single application of pressure is sufficient.  If a large amount of blood is desired, more pressure should be applied, especially if the blood is thick.
The pressure should be deep. The moderate limit for pressure is the depth of the skin only. What ointments should be used when applying cupping, and what water, and what should the cupper beware of?  For those with thick, hard, dry skin and tight pores...
The areas for cupping should be anointed with opening, softening, and dissolving ointments.  In summer, examples include the ointment of Khārizmī, violet ointment, sweet almond ointment, or pumpkin seed ointment.
However, in winter, examples include narcissus ointment, lily ointment, chamomile ointment, or hyacinth ointment, and similar ones. If the excess is thick and cold, use the ointment of marjoram, nightshade, frankincense, or fennel, and similar ones.
If the cupper has open pores and soft flesh, then ointment should be avoided.  These individuals should wash their cupping marks after the procedure with rosewater, cold water, grape vine water, pumpkin water, or similar waters.  And those whose blood is excessively moist...
They should wash their cupping marks with vinegar, or with rue and sumac water, and similar things.  And if their excess is thick, they should wash their cupping marks with old wine, marjoram water, fennel broth, or chamomile broth, and similar things.  Cupping should be avoided during and after bathing.
Instead, it should be used an hour or two after leaving the bath.  One should not sleep after cupping.  This is what the cupper and leech-applicator should consider before and after cupping.
First, one should consider whether the cupper or leech-applicator is bilious, with their blood tending towards sharpness and inflammation.  Then they should take cooling foods like pomegranate, chicory, vinegar, endive, and squill, and similar things.  Their food should be poultry, lamb, and similar stews.
And if their temperament is cold, they should be given honey-water and miya-water or bizury-squill water, and they should consume moderate fragrant wine, between old and new.  They should be given little food, and their diet should consist of poultry, canaries, sparrows, and doves in stews.  On the day of cupping and leeching, the drink should be more plentiful than the food.
Sometimes, some people should be given Faruq's antidote or musk medicine or shiltha before cupping and before or after leeching, to strengthen the main organs and thin the blood.  However, feverish people should not be given it.
As for cupping without a specific condition, these are the cuppings placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, kidney area, and groin.  These organs do not tolerate a condition being placed on them.  Rather, they are intended to draw blood from one organ to another.
For example, placing a cupping on the breasts in cases of running nose, or we use it to dispel cold air that has settled in an organ, like placing a cupping on the abdomen and navel.  It loosens the organ, warms it, and relieves pain by resolving that air.
It may be placed on the kidneys if there is a blockage or stones there, as its strong drawing power might open the blockage or dislodge the stones from their place.  Likewise, it does this when placed on the liver and spleen, when there is trapped air within them.  These cuppings can also be used empty, by suction only.
It may be used with fire, or filled with lukewarm water in cases of abscesses.  This means filling a large cupping with hot water alone, or water in which some suitable herbs have been boiled.
Then it is placed full on the area, held, and removed, repeated as needed.  This is the image of the cupping used with fire.
Its mouth opening is two fingers wide, as depicted, and its depth is half a span.  On one side, near the middle, there is a small hole just large enough for a needle to enter, made of thick Chinese or brass.
Its surface is smooth, flat, and polished so as not to injure the organ when placed on it.  In its center is a cross-shaped protrusion of brass or iron where the candle is placed with fire.  This cupping can also be made larger or smaller depending on the illnesses.
The user must be skillful, as cupping for children and thin individuals is different from cupping for strong, healthy adults.  As for how to place this cupping with fire on the area, one takes a wick of strong linen or a small candle of pitch and places it on the center of the hardened rod in the center of the cupping.
So that the fire ascends upwards towards the bottom of the cupping, to avoid burning the patient's body.  Then, it is placed on the area, and the finger is placed in the hole we mentioned, so that when holding the cupping, we do not need to remove the finger.
Then, steam will emerge from that hole, and the cupping will be released on the spot.  The wick is then relit as needed.  However, a cupping used for water-filled sores does not have a hardened rod or a hole.
Instead, it is used by filling it with water and placing it on the area only.  This is its form.
The larger the cupping, the more water it holds, the better.
Chapter Ninety-Seven.
On the application of leeches.
Leeches are used most often on areas where cupping cannot be applied, either because they are small, like the lip, gums, and the like, or because the area is lacking flesh, such as the finger and nose, and similar parts.
How to use them: Choose leeches from clear, unpolluted freshwater, and leave them in fresh water for a day and a night so that they are hungry and have nothing left in their bodies. Then the body is first bled by venesection or cupping, and then the afflicted area is treated.
Until it turns red, then the leech is placed upon it. When it is full, it will fall off. If possible, drawing the affected area with a cupping vessel is more beneficial. Otherwise, wash the area with vinegar then with plenty of water, and massage and squeeze it. If bleeding continues after the leech falls off, and it is a oozing, then dampen a linen cloth in cold water.
Place it over the area until the oozing stops. If the bleeding is excessive, sprinkle powdered alum or a similar astringent on it until the bleeding stops, or place peeled halves of beans on the area.
Leave it until the beans adhere to the area, and the bleeding will stop.  If further leeching is needed, do not re-use those leeches if others are available. If the leeches refuse to attach, then the area should be wiped with fresh blood.
Or pierce the area with a needle until some blood is drawn, then place a leech. If it senses any blood, it will attach to the spot. When you want it to detach, sprinkle some powdered licorice, salt, or ash on it and it will fall off at that spot.
Chapter Three.
On Fractures.
This chapter also contains what is needed in the art of medicine, namely the setting of fractures and dislocations of the bones.
Know, my sons, that many ignorant physicians and laypeople may claim expertise in this chapter, those who have never examined the writings of the ancients on it, nor read a single letter from them.
For this reason, this branch of knowledge is lacking in our country, and I have never encountered a skilled practitioner in it. But I have acquired what I have of it by my extensive reading of the books of the ancients and my diligent effort to understand them, then by dedicating myself to experience and practice throughout my life.
I have presented to you in this chapter everything that my knowledge encompasses. My experience has been applied to it, after I have brought it closer to you, clarifying it of its lengthy aspects, shortening it to the utmost brevity, explaining it to the fullest extent, and depicting for you numerous images of the tools used in it.
This is further elucidation, as I have done in the previous two chapters.  There is no power except from God, the Most High, the Supreme.
Chapter One.
In this chapter are summarized and collected matters concerning the breaking of bones. Before we begin to describe broken and dislocated limbs one by one, it is appropriate to mention some general statements and sections at the beginning of this chapter. This will first compel you, and anyone else eager to learn this noble craft, to understand and grasp its true meaning.
Therefore, I say that whenever a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall occurs, one should first hasten to reduce the displacement or loosen the parts, or both. Unless prevented by factors such as weakness or the affected person is a child or very old person, or if the weather is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then, his diet should be restricted to cold legumes, poultry, and venison.  Drinks, fatty meats, and overeating, and any food that causes excessive blood flow to the veins, should be avoided until the swelling subsides and the possibility of further discharge to the affected area is minimal.
Then, the patient should return to his usual regimen.  If the fractured bone begins to knit, the patient should eat nourishing, dense, and viscous foods such as barley, rice, marrow, and beef tripe. Eggs, soft fish, and thick drinks, and similar foods, will hasten and improve the healing of the fracture.  Know that fractures in strong men and the elderly will not heal to their original state.
Due to the dryness and hardness of their bones,  fractures in bones that are extremely flexible, like those of young children, may still heal, but nature forms a glue-like substance around the fractured bone, thick and binding, holding the fragments together.
Holding them together until they reach their original strength and security, so that nothing hinders their function.
Therefore, the patient's diet should consist of strong, viscous, and thick foods, as mentioned.  Know that fractures vary by the affected body part, as a fracture in the leg is different from one in the head, and a fracture in the chest from one in the back.
So too are all other parts different from one another. We will discuss each type of fracture in detail in its own section, and the type of bone fracture may also vary within itself.
Because the fracture may be a simple cracking without splinters, or it may be lengthwise with splinters and detached or non-detached fragments.  The fracture may be accompanied by a wound and skin tear, or be a slight crack.
Each type has a specific method of treatment, which will be explained in detail in its place.  Signs of a fracture include its crookedness, swelling, visibility to the touch, and creaking when pressed by hand.
If there is no visible distortion, creaking, or discomfort when palpating the bone, and the patient experiences little pain, there is no fracture, but perhaps a contusion, a minor fracture, or a slight crack.  Therefore, the affected area should not be moved by pulling or probing at all.
Instead, apply suitable medications to be mentioned later, then gently bind the area.  Understand that when a bone is fractured in two without splintering, each part has separated from its counterpart.
You should promptly realign and straighten it before a hot swelling develops. If a hot swelling occurs, leave it for several days until it subsides, then realign it using any gentle method you can.
Know that realigning and straightening a bone is easier than one with splinters.  Then it is bound as will be described later. If the bone has splinters, the fractured limb (hand or foot) must be stretched from both sides.
Either by your hands if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with both ropes and hands. Position the limb on a level surface in its natural shape.  Once the fractured bone parts have been stretched, then gently and skillfully return those protrusions to their proper places.
Strive diligently to cause no pain or suffering to the patient through your actions.  Do your utmost to join the two bone fragments together in the best possible position.  At that time, you should touch and feel them with your hands. If you find anything out of place, correct and straighten it to the best of your ability.
Beware of excessive stretching and forceful prodding, as many ignorant people do, and often cause a hot swelling or inflammation in the limb with such actions.  I have witnessed this repeatedly from their actions.
After alignment, careful treatment, and binding, keep the limb still and quiet. Warn the patient not to move it during waking hours, sleep, or when turning over, getting up, or excreting; in short, during any movement.  Ensure the limb is in a position that prevents pain.
If the patient feels pain or discomfort in any position, he should change the position to one that does not cause pain.  Furthermore, the position should be straight and level.
So that if the limb is straightened, it will not become crooked.  Now, as for how to bind a fractured limb, as I describe to you, know that fractured limbs vary in size, shape, and condition.  Those that are small, such as the arm, finger, forearm, and similar parts, . . .
The bandages should be soft and gentle for smaller limbs, like the arm and fingers.  For larger limbs, such as the thigh, back, and chest, the bandages should be broad and firm.  A wide bandage is needed for a larger limb, to bind it evenly and firmly on all sides, without any gaps.
Then, after completing the alignment, apply the appropriate plaster. Starting with a soft, gentle traction on the fracture site, then bandage the fracture site itself three or four times, depending on the limb's needs.  Lightly tighten the bandage, then move it upwards from the fracture site.
Tighten the bandage less than the tightening of the fracture site, and then gradually move the bandage away from the fracture site, gradually loosening the tension, until the bone is in a good, correct position. Then take another bandage, and wrap it also around the fracture site.
Wrap it several times. Then move the wrapping down to the lower part of the fracture. Maintain the tension and looseness of the wrapping as described for the first wrap (the upper part). Insert soft padding or cloth between the wraps to correct any misalignment of the fracture.
If there is misalignment, do not insert anything. Otherwise, wrap another bandage around it, and then apply firm splints over these wraps, provided there is no swelling or inflammation of the limb.
If there is swelling or inflammation, apply something to relieve the swelling and reduce the inflammation. Leave it for a few days, then apply the splints firmly at that time. The splints should be made from wide, flattened reeds carefully prepared, or from ebony wood.
Made of pine, palm fronds, or similar readily available materials, such as papyrus.  Ensure each splint is the same size and shape.
However, the splint placed directly on the fracture should be slightly thicker and wider than the others.  The length of the splint should be appropriate for the size of the fractured limb.  Image of the splint:
Then, tighten the splints with another bandage, as you did initially.  Tie securely from above with strong threads, ensuring the tightness is especially focused on the fracture site. The tightness should decrease the further away from the fracture it is.
The threads should be of medium thickness, and preferably made of damp linen.  Threads made from twisted linen strips, as some ignorant practitioners do, are a serious mistake, as these are too thick.
Because the tightness is then uneven, and thin threads are also unsuitable, as they don't provide the desired tension.  The gap between splints should not be less than a finger's breadth.
If the patient is irritated by the edges of the splints after tightening in the correct places, place soft padding, such as soft wool, underneath to prevent any discomfort.  If the fracture is accompanied by a wound and the skin is perforated, we will discuss this separately.
Know that not every fractured limb needs to be splinted from day one.  If the limb is large, splints should not be applied until five, seven, or more days later, depending on your assessment of the risk of developing a fever-inducing swelling.
This is a description of the dressings used by the ancients to treat fractures, dislocations, and sprains. It is a concise, general dressing that sets bones and is suitable for most temperaments, particularly children and women, as it is neither excessively hot nor cold.
Take the flour dust, the fine particles that adhere to the millstones during grinding. Knead it as it is, without sifting it with egg white, forming a dough that is neither thick nor thin, and then bake it.
Another dressing for fractures, dislocations, and sprains:  Take ten dirhams each of mastic, frankincense, acacia, raisin, gum arabic, and gypsum. Take five dirhams each of myrrh and licorice, and twenty dirhams of acacia. Finally, take twenty dirhams of Armenian or Roman clay. Grind all the ingredients together and mix them with acacia water or egg white if the patient's temperament is hot.  This dressing, well-combined, will quickly set broken bones and is suitable for most people due to its balance.
Another dressing useful for fractures and sprains:  Take ten dirhams each of gum arabic, mastic, and white cumin. Take five dirhams each of myrrh and licorice, six dirhams of acacia, and twenty dirhams of Armenian clay. Grind all together finely, sift, and knead with water or egg white, then apply.
A dressing for dislocated joints and bones, relieving pain and for bone splitting and fractures:  Take prepared wool, dip it in vinegar and cooked oil, and apply to the affected area.  This dressing does not have the strength to set a fracture.
However, it is excellent for relieving inflammation and pain, particularly. Another dressing for setting a broken bone: Take unripe fig leaves and wild poppy leaves, grind both together, and apply as a poultice. A further, concise dressing to be used when a bone is broken.
To resolve the remaining swelling, take the root of cumin, chamomile, violet, and poppy seed—one part each. Grind all together and mix with a poultice if the limb is not inflamed; if inflamed, mix with coriander water or water and apply.
A stronger dressing for resolving a hard swelling after a fractured bone: Take the root of cumin, flax seeds, fenugreek, rosemary, marjoram, violet, and chamomile—one part each.
Grind all together and mix with dill water, fresh water, or a poultice, depending on the heat of the limb and the cessation of its heat.  As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that this should be applied to a fractured limb during setting.
Except for the cerate made of wax and oil, nothing else.  He described it as being of medium thickness.  As for Galen, he believed that things with dryness and some heat, such as myrrh, mastic, and frankincense, and the like, should be applied to the fractured limb during setting.
A dressing for weakness and pain: Take poppy seeds, chickpeas, shaved human hair or bird feathers, cumin, and salt—equal parts. Grind, sieve, mix, and apply as a dressing.  Regarding the amount needed to keep the ligament...
Then it should be removed; observe. If no pain, rubbing, or movement of the fractured bone from its place occurs, do not remove it for many days. However, if severe rubbing, troubling pain, or swelling occurs in the area,
Then promptly remove it immediately and do not delay.  Remove the dressing. Then take a soft cloth or damp sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the area until the itching and pain subside. Then allow the limb to rest for an hour.
Then apply softened wool soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil, and bandage it overnight to relieve the hot swelling, reduce limb swelling, and alleviate the pain.  Then, return to gentle stretching and a light bandage, do not apply the initial tightness.
And treat it gently until it heals. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and puffiness, and all symptoms have completely disappeared, and the bandage and stretching are needed, then redo it as you did at first, in the same manner. If nothing of what we have mentioned happens to the limb.
Then do not remove it until after three, four, five, or seven days.  Sometimes it can be left for twenty days. All this depending on the condition of the limb as we mentioned, until the fracture is firm and the flesh is healed.
In that case, increase the tightness of the bandage further than the initial tightness. Also increase the strengthening of the patient's diet, as previously mentioned. If you see that the fracture site has become too dry and wasted more than is necessary, know that the nourishment is not reaching it.
Then apply lukewarm water each time you remove the bandage, every three days.  Ease the tightness of the bandage slightly.  By doing this, nourishment will reach the limb and it will heal quickly.  As for what the ignorant among those who set fractures do, of breaking the limb again...
For if it is not properly set the first time, and is set crookedly, then it is a mistake made by them, and a great deception. Even if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and acted upon it. I have seen no trace of it in any of them. The correct approach is not to do it.
Chapter Two.
Concerning fractures of the head.
Fractures of the head are numerous, their forms diverse, and their causes varied.  Some fractures are caused by sword blows, and these may extend completely to the peritoneum beneath the bone. Like the way a carpenter works on wood.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called a carpenter's fracture.  Alternatively, the sword may have cut only part of the bone, leaving only one side fractured, and not penetrating to the end. This type of fracture is called a simple fracture, and the wound from these fractures may be large or small.
Some fractures are caused by contusions or abrasions, resulting from a blow with a stone, a fall on a stone, or similar.  These fractures may extend to the membrane beneath the bone or be superficial.
The wound from these fractures may also be large or small.  Some fractures are hidden within the bone, barely perceptible, a slight crack.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called a hairline fracture.  A fracture may also result from a fall or a blow from a stone or similar object, causing a portion of the bone to sink inward, creating a depression, much like a piece of copper that is struck and a portion sinks inward.
This is most common in the cranium of young children.  All these types of fractures can have detached or non-detached fragments. We will discuss the treatment of each in its place, and we will identify all these types of fractures through examination.
Inspecting them with probes and removing putrid flesh. The hairline fracture is identified by examining the bone, rubbing and staining it with ink, where the fracture will appear black.  Treatment of the fracture first considers the patient's symptoms.
If you see obvious symptoms that suggest danger, such as vomiting bile, prostration, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, bulging and redness of the eyes, and similar symptoms, do not approach or treat the patient, for death is inevitable in most cases with these symptoms.
If you see symptoms that do not alarm you and hope for the patient's recovery, then begin treatment.  If the injured person comes to you in the initial stages of the injury, especially in the winter.
You should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day, in any case. If it is during the summer, you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane beneath the bone deteriorates, causing the symptoms we have described.
If the bone fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain, and there is crushing and bruising, you should remove the crushed and bruised portion, as I am describing to you: shave the head of the injured patient.
Expose the bone in any way possible, according to the nature of the wound and the patient's condition. If, during the exposure of the bone, you see bleeding or a hot swelling, address these accordingly.
Fill the area with cloths soaked in rosewater and ointment. Dress the wound until the swelling subsides and bleeding ceases. Then, proceed to the repositioning and extraction of the bone, using one of two methods of procedure.
One method is to cut the bone with a thin, narrow blade, and this is its image/illustration:.
Then, use another cut, slightly wider than the first, and this is its image/illustration:.
Then use yet another cut, wider still than the second.  You should have a set of different blades, some wider than others, some shorter than others, and all with extremely sharp edges.  Let them be made of Indian steel or good quality steel.
Use gentleness in striking the blade so as not to dislodge the head and injure it. If the bone is strong and hard, you should first pierce around it with drills (called non-immersed drills).
Because they do not penetrate beyond the bone of the skull, as the drill has a rounded edge below its sharp head, like a ring or small circle, which prevents it from sinking in and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
You should have many such drills, each suitable for the thickness of the bone, so that for each skull you have a drill whose sharp tip is of the appropriate length and shortness for the thickness of that skull.  These are illustrations of three types. Of drills, large, medium, and small.
As for how to drill around the fractured bone, position the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you feel the bone has been penetrated.  Then move the drill to another location, leaving a space between each drill hole the thickness of a reed or similar.
Then cut between each pair of holes with the saws, using as much gentleness as possible, as we said, until you remove the bone, either by hand or with another instrument you have prepared for this purpose.
Such as spatulas and fine forceps.  Care must be taken that the drill or saw does not touch the peritoneum.  If you have smoothed the bone and separated it from the peritoneum, if it was attached, and removed it, then it should be cleaned.
Smooth any remaining roughness on the bone with another instrument similar to a saw, but it should be finer and more delicate than the other saws.  If any small bone fragments or splinters remain, remove them gently with your available instruments.
Then treat the wound afterwards with compresses and ointments as will be mentioned.  The other method of treatment is quite straightforward, free from risk, praised greatly by Galen.  This is what he said. First, you should begin by exposing the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and prominent.  Once you have exposed that part, place the tip of this lens-shaped saw beneath it.  This is its image:.
The lens-shaped part of it should be smooth, not cutting anything.  The sharp part is at the ends, extending lengthwise as you see.  The lens-shaped part rests against the peritoneum and the sharp edge of the saw is on the bone. Then strike the saw from one side.
With a small hammer, so that you cut through all the bone gently, as it revolves.  You are safe from injuring the membrane; absolutely no frightening incident will happen, even if the surgeon is the most ignorant and timid person, indeed, even if he were asleep.
If any part of the membrane remains attached to the bone in some places, scrape it off using the tip of the lens-shaped saw itself, and remove it gently.  It will come away without injury or fear.
If the fracture of the bone did not penetrate the membrane, and the broken part of the bone was its surface, remaining with roughness and small fragments, then you should remove that roughness and those fragments with small chisels of various sizes, so that you can use the most suitable one for each place, according to what the work requires.
The broken bone and its shape must be kept in mind.  You should first use the widest of those chisels to scrape the bone, then use a narrower one after that, and continue in this way, successively using narrower and finer ones.
As for the other small, hairline fractures and slight breaks, each one should be treated according to what will lead to its repair.  This is something that is not obscure to anyone with even the slightest knowledge of this craft, and who has considered what we have written and explained about major fractures.
If, after removing the bone, the membrane remains exposed, you should take a linen cloth the size of the wound, soak it in rose oil, and place it over the wound's opening. Then take another cloth, folded or triangular in shape. Soak it in rose water and oil, place it over the first cloth, and do this as gently as possible to avoid placing undue weight on the peritoneum. Then, use a wide bandage on top and tie it, but only as much as necessary to hold the wound together.
Leave it with this treatment for a day or two, until the inflammation subsides.  Then, remove the bandage and use afterwards the drying medicines such as iris roots, caraway flour, kender powder, and za'warand, and the like.  Make pills from these medicines.
And sprinkle them, as they are, dry, onto the wound. In general, use a medicine that cleanses but does not sting.  Strive to keep the wound clean during treatment, free from excess grease, dirt, and pus, and do not allow any pus to collect in it at all.
Because if pus collects on the brain membrane, it corrupts and putrefies it, causing a great affliction to the afflicted, and this can occur in the membranes of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if its treatment is neglected.
If a dark discoloration appears on its surface, and if the afflicted person exhibits the symptoms we have mentioned, know that he is doomed, without fail.  If the discoloration was caused by a medicine applied to it, and if the medicine's potency was to produce such discoloration,
Therefore, take one part honey and three parts rose oil, mix them thoroughly, and apply the mixture to a cloth.  Then place the cloth on the membrane and treat it with the necessary remedies until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the setting of a fractured nose.
Know that only the upper nasal bones, either both or one, can break, because they are bony. The lower portion is cartilaginous and does not break; rather, it is subject to bruises, curvature, and lacerations.
If one of the nasal bones is broken, the little finger should be inserted into the nasal opening.  Straighten the fracture from the inside with your index finger, and from the outside with your thumb, until the nose is restored to its natural shape.  Do this gently, taking care not to cause further pain to the patient.
If the fracture is high up on the nose and the finger cannot reach it, it should be straightened using a blunt, slightly thickened instrument.  If the fracture is in both sides, do likewise.  He should hasten to set it on the first day after the fracture.
If possible, otherwise after the seventh or tenth day, once the acute swelling has subsided, insert a thread of linen into the nasal opening if the fracture is on one side, or two threads if the fracture is on both sides.
The threads should be thick enough to fill the nasal opening.  Some earlier surgeons recommended soaking the threads in ghee and changing them daily.  However, I do not agree with this.  Instead, the threads should be soaked in beaten egg white mixed with mill dust.
Then leave the threads until the bone is set and the cartilage hardens.  After the threads are in place,  goose feather tubes may be inserted into the nasal opening, wrapped with soft linen.  This will hold the nose fracture more firmly, and ensure the patient can still breathe.
This is not essential.  You may do it if you wish, or you may use the threads instead. If, during your treatment, a hot swelling appears on the nose, bandage it with qirūṭī or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or a diachylon ointment.
If no hot swelling develops, then bandage the outside with semolina flour and powdered kandar mixed with egg white, and then place a soft bandage over it.  Do not tie the nose at all, especially if the nose bones are fractured in small pieces.
Or if it splintered, it should be cut and removed using the appropriate instrument. Then sew the opening and treat it with ointments that promote healing and inflammation. If a wound occurs inside the nose, it should be treated with threads and lead tubes used. Until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On setting the lower beard if it is broken.
If the lower beard is broken, and the break is not accompanied by an open wound, if the break is only from the outside and has not been fractured in two pieces and has curved inwards, then its recognition is easier.
If the fracture is on the right side, the index finger of the left hand should be inserted into the patient's mouth. Similarly, if the fracture is on the left side, the index finger of the right hand should be inserted, and gently lift the fracture hump from the inside to the outside.
Your other hand, from outside the bone, controls its alignment. If the jaw fracture has broken into two pieces, then traction must be applied from both sides in a straight line, until alignment is achieved. If it has occurred in the teeth, it will regenerate.
Or if it has separated, bind what you expect to remain with a gold, silver, or silk thread. Then, place a bandaged pad on the broken beard, then place a folded cloth over the pad, and put a large, tight bandage or a piece of leather of equal length to the beard over the cloth. Then tie it from above as you deem appropriate to bind and hold it together, so it does not break.  Instruct the patient to rest and refrain from exertion.  Give him soft, easily digested food. If you suspect any change in shape in any way, resolve to undo it on the third day.
Then tie it from above as you deem appropriate to bind and hold it together, so it does not break.  Instruct the patient to rest and refrain from exertion.  Give him soft, easily digested food. If you suspect any change in shape in any way, resolve to undo it on the third day. Then repair the bandage with flour dust and egg white, or with semolina flour, after removing the bandage.  Place a soft cloth over the dressing, and as long as the dressing remains adhered and the bone's condition has not changed, leave it in place.
Do not remove it until the fracture heals and strengthens.  This fracture often strengthens in three weeks. If, during that time, a hot swelling appears, use the remedies we previously mentioned to soothe it until it subsides.
And if the fracture is accompanied by an open wound, if a fragment or fragments of bone have become detached, carefully remove those fragments using instruments suited to the task.  If the wound opening is narrow, widen it with a surgical scalpel as needed.
Then, once you have removed the fragments and nothing remains, suture the wound opening if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the suitable ointments. And close the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the setting of a fractured clavicle.
The clavicle is most often fractured from the front, near the acromion.  The fracture occurs in one of three ways: either it breaks and splits into two without splintering, which is the easiest to set;
or the fracture may have splinters, making it more difficult to set; or the fracture may occur with a wound. If the fracture is without a wound, have two assistants; one to hold the arm next to the fractured clavicle, and the other to pull the neck in the opposite direction.
Then adjust the fracture with your fingers until it is properly aligned, without any protruding edges or irregularities. If you need to pull more, a ball of linen or wool should be placed under the patient's armpit.
Adjust the bone as needed, pulling and raising the clavicle and pressing the ball with your hand until the fracture is properly aligned. If you are unable to draw the end of the clavicle outward because it has sunk inward, the patient should be placed on their back.
A moderate-sized cushion is placed under the patient's shoulder, and the assistant presses the shoulder downward to elevate the deeply sunk clavicle.  Then correct and align the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel that a piece of the clavicle has fractured and is moving, you should gently separate and remove that fragment. If the fragment is embedded in the bone, use one of the prepared cutting tools to remove it. After placing the instrument that protects the diaphragm (which is made of wood or iron) under the clavicle, this is its image:.
It resembles a spoon, without any ornamentation. Its width should be adjusted to the size of the bone, and its length according to the task. It should have two ends, one wider and the other narrower, as you see.
If the wound you made to extract the bone fragment is wide and there is a risk of inflammation, close the wound edges with stitches. If the wound is small, or if you fear a severe inflammation, suture the wound with sutures in proportion to the wound's size.
If there is a risk of inflammation, soak the wound in rosewater and vinegar or a similar liquid and apply it.  If the bone is dislocated, without a wound or incision, apply a dressing made of mill dust mixed with egg white to the bone. Place a soft splint over it, then place a cushion under his armpit if needed.  Take a very long bandage, about a span or so wide. Apply a folded bandage to the splint and dressing. Then take a splint made from a thin board. Make it three fingers wide and the same in length. Wrap it in a cloth, then wrap that cloth with the splint into the bandage section over the fracture. Then wrap the bandage around the fracture tightly. Secure it around his neck and under his healthy armpit, and under his injured armpit.  Repeat on each side, as you see that the tightness will properly hold the fracture in place, which is something you should know.
The whole point is that the splint should not be removed from the fractured bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient every day.  Whenever the ligaments loosen and you see the splint has shifted, correct it and tighten the bandage.  Then, have the patient sleep on their back, and place a small cushion under their armpit at night.
To elevate his arm from his side, so the clavicle fracture is elevated with the shoulder, or tie his arm to his neck.  Do not loosen the bandage unless a wound or swelling develops within twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see a reason for it. Maintain the pressure and leave it until the fracture of the collarbone is set and consolidated, and it strengthens further over twenty-eight days. And it may be in some people in less than that.
Chapter Six.
Concerning the setting of a fractured shoulder.  The shoulder rarely fractures in its broad area, rather it is its bony prominences which fracture.  So whenever a part or the middle of it is fractured, this is only discernible by palpation.  Then according to the nature of the fracture, apply a splint.
Set it and restore it to its natural shape by any means possible. Then apply mill dust, egg white, and soft linen bandage over a folded cloth pad, and then apply a wide splint of wood.
Make the splint as wide as the entire shoulder, or slightly wider.  If there is a protrusion under the splint in any part of the shoulder, smooth that protrusion with soft linen until the splint is even. Then secure it from above with a long bandage.
Apply a firm bandage and secure the splint so that it does not shift from its position.  Inspect the bandage daily; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint if it moves from its position.  The patient should lie on their healthy side.  The shoulder heals in twenty days.
For a period of one to twenty-five days.  If this period is complete, remove the bandage, and you are safe, as it is one of the bones for which shattering or displacement is not feared. If a piece of bone protrudes and is irritating the skin, cut it and remove it. Do what has been mentioned in the case of a fractured clavicle to relieve any inflammation that may occur.
Chapter Seven.
On the setting of fractured ribs.
The chest can fracture in its middle, though this is infrequent.  Its extremities, however, are more prone to fracture and splintering.  If the middle of the chest fractures, the symptoms include tilting downward, severe pain, and difficulty breathing and coughing.
There may also be blood spitting, and the depression in the fractured bone becomes evident to the touch.  To set it, the patient should lie on their back with a pillow between their shoulders.  Then, their sides should be pressed, and the ribs gathered together by hand from both sides.  Care should be taken to adjust them smoothly on all sides possible.
And maintain this until the bone returns to its proper shape. Then apply bandages and splints, placing a splint made of thin wood from poplar or willow or similar lightweight material on top, after wrapping it in cloth. Then, carefully secure the bandage around the fractured bone so it does not loosen.  Wrap the bandage around the back, making turns, and tighten it firmly.  Then, check the bandage frequently, tightening it whenever it loosens.
If the need arises to remove it due to inflammation, pain, or swelling in the area, promptly remove it, and take off the bandage, correcting any such problems with the appropriate treatment. Then, if you deem it appropriate, reapply the bandage. Apply it and maintain the pressure until healed.
Chapter Eight.
Concerning the setting of broken ribs, know that ribs fracture primarily in the thick parts adjacent to the back and their front ends.  Injuries occur because they are cartilaginous.  This is easily detectable by feeling with the fingers.
Set the fracture by aligning the broken ends with your fingers until the shape is correct; then, bandage and support the fractured bone with a splint if needed. If the fracture is inclined inward, it will cause severe pain for the patient.
And the pain is like that experienced by someone with a hernia, because the bone pierces the diaphragm and also causes shortness of breath, coughing, and heavy spitting of blood.  This is a difficult condition to treat, and early practitioners used many methods to address it.
Some said that the patient should eat foods that cause flatulence and wind, to inflate the abdomen and push the fracture outward.  We reject this, lest it exacerbate or cause inflammation.  If inflammation has already begun, this will only increase and confirm it.
Some said a cupping glass should be placed on the area and pressed firmly.  This seems reasonable, but it is feared that the cupping will draw excessive fluids to the weak area. Others said that the area should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
The ribs are supported, filling the space, so that the ligament is level when wrapped around its circumference.  Then the patient is treated for a hernia with diet and medication. If the condition is severe, it cannot be tolerated. The bone was piercing the diaphragm painfully, and we feared for the patient.  Therefore, it is necessary to cut open the area, expose the fractured rib, and then place the instrument that protects the peritoneum (which has been described previously) beneath it.  Carefully cut the bone and remove it.
Then, if the wound is large, we bring the edges together with stitches and treat it with ointments until it heals.  If a hot abscess develops during this time, immediately apply rose-oil compresses to the area.  Also, treat the patient internally with something to reduce the swelling.
He should lie on the side on which he sleeps more easily until healed.
Chapter Nine.
On setting fractures of the back and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if they are fractured, this is rare.  More often, they are injured.  The same applies to the vertebrae of the back.  If this happens to someone, and you want to know if they will heal or not... Look then. If you see that his hands are relaxed, numb, and lifeless, and he cannot move them, extend them, or clench them, and if you pinch or prick them with a needle, he feels nothing and experiences no pain, know that in most cases, he will not recover; he is doomed.
But if he can move them and feels the pinching and pricking, know that the marrow of the bone is intact, and the patient will recover with treatment.  If a fracture of the back occurs similarly, and you want to know if it will heal as well...
Look at his legs.  If you see that they are relaxed, and what happened to the hands has happened to them, and then, when he lies on his back, wind and feces come out involuntarily, and when he lies on his stomach, urine comes out involuntarily... If he lies on his back and tries to urinate, he cannot.  Know then that he is doomed, and do not bother with treatment. If none of this occurs, the situation is less severe.  The treatment for what has happened is to try to relieve the inflammation.
By placing rose oil on the injured vertebra, alone or mixed with roasted egg yolks, three times a day, until the inflammation subsides.  Then apply one of the strengthening, dry dressings to the area and bind it with a bandage.  Instruct him to rest and remain still.
He should only sleep on the side where he does not feel pain until he recovers. If a fragment or something else has separated from the bone following the injury, it is necessary to cut through the skin and remove that bone fragment. Then, if the wound is large, stitch together the edges of the wound.  Then treat with healing ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the coccyx (tailbone) is broken, insert the little finger of the left hand into the rectum.
Then, with the other hand, align the fractured bone as best as possible, and then apply a bandage and splint, if needed, and then secure it.
If you feel a fractured fragment within it, cut open the area and remove it, and treat the wound with the previous treatment until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the setting of a fractured hip.
Fractures of the hip bones are rare.  If they do occur, the fracture is typically a splintering at the ends, a lengthwise splitting, and an inward tilting.  The affected person experiences pain, throbbing, and numbness in the leg on the fractured side.
To set it, run your hand over it to assess the nature of the fracture. If the fracture is only at the ends, align the ends as best as possible to resemble their normal shape.
If the fracture is lengthwise or has tilted inward, lay the patient on their stomach to facilitate setting the fracture.  Once aligned, apply a dressing, then a splint of wood or leather, and secure it tightly.
Do not fear the fracture shifting or the splint loosening.  Fill the hollows of the waist with what will support the splint's alignment.  Instruct the patient to sleep on their back or healthy side, if a hot swelling develops.
Refrain from stretching or manipulating the fracture until the hot swelling subsides. Apply whatever eases the swelling, as previously described.  Then, return to setting and securing it properly.  If splinters or fragments are visible in the bone, do not remove them.
Do not touch [the splinters], but align them from the outside, as we said.  Maintain the securing until healed.
Chapter Eleven.
On setting a fractured humerus.
The humerus is the bone between the elbow and the shoulder. If it is fractured, set it in one of two ways.  One way is to take a curved, smooth stick of medium thickness, like this:
Tie straps to its ends, then hang it from a high position.  The patient sits in a chair, then lays their fractured arm on the stick until their armpit is pressed against the center of the stick's curve.  Then hang something heavy from above it.
Or a servant pulls it down. Then the physician straightens the fracture with his hands until the fracture is returned to its proper position.  The other way is for the patient to lie on their back and for their arm to be suspended from their neck by a strap.
Then order two servants to hold one the upper part of the fracture in their hands, and the other to hold the lower part and to pull each one in their respective direction. If you want the pull to be stronger, tie a strap below the fracture and one above it, and have each servant pull toward their own side.
If the fracture is near the shoulder joint, the straps should be placed one under the armpit, and the other under the fracture towards the elbow.  Similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, the straps should be placed in that location and on the elbow itself.
Then the fracture is gently straightened without force until the fracture is properly aligned and joined together well.  Then, if no inflammation appears, tighten it.  However, if a hot swelling occurs, refrain from tightening it until the seventh day. Apply soft, moistened wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil to it.
Until the swelling subsides, then tighten it. The method of tightening it is to place a bandage over the fracture, then wrap a new cloth layer over the bandage.  Then, pull the arm onto the upper arm, and place the hand open on the shoulder.
Hold the cloth and tightening on the upper arm and the forearm so that the arm acts as a splint, if nothing prevents you from doing so and there is no change in the broken bone.  If you fear a change in the bone, use splints.
Place a splint over the fracture itself, wider and stronger than other splints.  Maintain a finger's width between each splint.  The length of the splints should be according to the fracture, with an additional three fingers on each side.
Then tighten the splints with the tightening described in the first section.
The tightening should be stronger at the site of the fracture.
