The further from the fracture, the less the tightening. If you see the placement of splints and tightening as we mentioned in the context of setting the limb from your time, then do it.
If you fear inflammation, leave the tightening and splints until the seventh day, as we said.  Then examine the ligaments every three days, so that there is no irritation or swelling, or that food is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightening.  All this is corrected as we mentioned.
If you are certain that none of this will happen, do not loosen the bandage until many days later.  The patient should lie on their back, with their hand on their stomach.  A padded armrest filled with wool, properly positioned, should be placed under the armpit.
Examine the limb at all times, day or night, to ensure that the fractured limb's shape does not change or that the bandage does not loosen.  Correct everything with effort, and make the patient's diet as we previously mentioned, first of all gentle, until the bone is strengthened.
Then the diet should be made thicker.  It is customary for the arm and leg to strengthen in forty days.  At that time, the bandages should be loosened, and the patient should use a bath and be treated with ointments suitable for that, if the fracture is serious and extensive.
Do not loosen the bandage until fifty days or even two months.
Chapter Twelve.
On setting a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, called the ulna. One is small, situated next to the thumb; the other is large, placed beneath the small one at the bottom.  Sometimes only the larger ulna is fractured, or only the smaller one, or both may be broken.
If the upper small ulna is fractured, its setting is easy, and healing is quicker.  If the lower ulna is fractured, the fracture is worse and healing more difficult.  And the worst case is when both bones are broken simultaneously. If the upper small ulna is fractured...
When setting it, the physician should apply gentle, moderate traction to align the bone.  If the larger ulna is fractured, traction should be stronger. If both ulnae are fractured, the traction should be very strong.
The hand should be positioned, during setting and traction, extended on a cushion.  The thumb should be higher than all the other fingers; the little finger should be lower than the other fingers. The patient should be seated.
The cushion should be at a height so that the patient is not inconvenienced.
Then, an assistant supports the arm from below, either with their hand or a bandage, and another assistant supports it from above.  Then, the physician aligns the bone as best as possible.  If the fracture has splinters, they should be treated.
Return each splinter to its place. If a splinter protrudes, piercing the skin, and you cannot set it, make an incision and remove it as described previously.  If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, a separate section is dedicated to this.
For the treatment of this, refer to the relevant section. If a hot swelling appears at the beginning of the setting, apply a bandage made of linen soaked in rose oil and white wax, medium in thickness. Apply a gentle pressure to the bandage until the swelling subsides.
Remove the linen bandage and apply a dressing prepared from mill dust and egg white. Then, apply splints. The splint placed directly on the fracture should be wider and stronger.  Know that an arm usually requires six splints in most cases.
The fracture may be in one forearm bone or both.  Then, make the traction stronger at the site of the fracture.  As you apply traction up or down, gradually decrease the tension as described earlier in the beginning of the chapter.
The bandages wrapped around the fracture should be soft and damp, not too stiff. The thread used for tightening should be linen, medium in thickness as described. Inspect the limb and ligaments after several days.
If any problem arises, such as irritation appearing on the limb, it is necessary to wash the limb with warm water until the irritation subsides.  Leave the limb un-bandaged overnight for rest, then resume the traction.
If the traction has become loose and the bone has shifted, or something similar has occurred, correct everything to the best of your ability. Also, if the limb is not receiving nourishment due to excessive traction, it is necessary to loosen the traction slightly and leave it for a few days to allow nourishment to reach it, and then tighten it again.
If the patient does not experience any of the problems mentioned, the bandage should not be removed for at least twenty days or so.  Then, suspend the patient's arm around their neck, keeping the arm straight.  The patient should avoid sudden movements and sleep on their back.
Know that this arm fracture will heal in thirty days, or perhaps in thirty-two, or even in twenty-eight days, all depending on the individual's constitution and strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On setting the fracture of the hand and fingers.
The metacarpal bones and finger joints rarely fracture, but often suffer contusions.  When the hand sustains a fracture or contusion...
The patient should sit with their legs crossed, and a chair placed before them, level with the ground.
Then, placing his hand outstretched on the chair, the bone setter extends the fractured bone and aligns it, the physician.  When proper alignment is achieved, a bandage and splint should be applied, if no hot swelling occurs.  A brace should then be placed over the injured area.
Wrap it in soft cloth; if the fracture is towards the palm of the hand, make a ball shape from cloth, and instruct the patient to hold it with their injured hand, then tie it with a long cloth.
Let the splint be made of supple leather, so the skin can heal as the whole hand bends.  Tie it securely. If the fracture is outward, a splint should be placed above and another below the hand to keep the hand open and upright.
Then, the splint should be secured as the hand rotates, and the fingers should be tied together with bandages. If the fracture is in one of the fingers, if it's the thumb, align it properly, and then secure it with the hand.  Or, if you prefer, create a small, separate splint to keep the fracture straight.
If the fracture is in any other finger (like the middle, index, ring, or pinky), align it and tie it to the healthy adjacent finger, or tie all the fingers together, which is better, or use a small, separate splint.
As we said for the thumb, and examine after your treatment and removal from the hot swelling, deal with it as needed when such things occur, as described repeatedly.
Fourteenth Chapter.
On setting a fractured thigh.
The thighbone often breaks and is apparent to the touch because it shifts forward and backward.  Its setting involves tying a bandage above the fracture and another below the fracture, with the patient lying on their face.
Then, each attendant pulls the bandage toward its side, evenly, if the fracture is in the middle of the bone.  If it is near the hip, a soft bandage, of wool or similar material, should be applied.
At the hip, toward the pubis, the pull should be upward, and the other bandage below the fracture.  Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, the bandage should be near the knee, so the pull is downward.  Then, the physician aligns the bone with both hands until it is restored to its natural shape.
The bone will knit together well.  At that point, the bandage and pressure should be maintained unless the limb develops a hot swelling.  If swelling occurs, leave it for several days until the hot swelling subsides, then return to treatment.
For the pressure, a wide, firm bandage should be wrapped around the fracture two or three times, leaving some extra.  Then, the leg should be wrapped so that the heel is at the hip joint.  A long thread should be inserted between the thigh and leg near the knee from below, and the ends of the thread should be brought out from above on both sides.
Then, wrap the remaining bandage around the leg and thigh.  Then, place splints on the thigh, at the fracture site itself.  Make one splint on the shin bone.  Then, fill the gap between the thigh and leg with soft cloths so that the pressure is even.
Then begin with strong pressure from the center on the fracture site, wrapping three or four times.  And as the bandage moves away from the fracture site, let your pressure become less, softer, and looser.
Then, take the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and leg, and tie to the adjacent splints from above.  Then, run the ends of the thread down to the heel of the leg, and also tie the ends of the splints together on the other side.
So that the bandage does not move from its place, then leave the pressure on it as long as no sores, swelling, or puffiness appear on the limb. If any of this happens, immediately loosen it and correct anything that happened, as we have mentioned repeatedly.
If there is a sharp fragment in the bone that pierces, then if possible, smooth it out. Otherwise, cut over it and remove it. Treat the wound in the same way that we mentioned earlier until it heals.  Sometimes the thigh fracture can be splinted without adding the leg.
As we mentioned for the humerus and forearm, except that such a treatment will not cause lameness to the patient.  However, if splinted alone, without including the leg, the patient will always become lame.
Know that the thigh is splinted for fifty days, or a little more, or a little less, all of that according to the variations in temperaments and other circumstances.
Chapter Fifteen.
In fractures of the patella.
The patella rarely fractures, but is often bruised. If fracture does occur, it is either a fissure or splintering of its parts, and this may be with or without a wound.
One must rely on observation for all of this, and reduce the fracture by aligning the separated parts with the fingers until they come together and fuse as far as possible through gentle and skillful realignment, then apply a bandage and a circular splint if needed.
Then apply a bandage over this, following the same approach as for other fractures; treat any swelling or other symptoms as described elsewhere, addressing each circumstance with the appropriate remedy until healing occurs.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the treatment of fractured leg.
The leg has two bones: one thick, called the tibia, and the other thin, called the ulna.  Fractures of these bones are similar to those of the arm bones. Therefore, its treatment is like that of the arm, the procedure being the same.  If both bones are fractured, the leg will turn in all directions; if the thinner bone is fractured, the leg will turn forward; and if the thicker bone is fractured, especially from below, this is self-evident.
Therefore, use traction and alignment, and bind the dressings accordingly, just as in the case of an arm.  However, if the fracture of the leg is severe with numerous fragments, the traction should be milder and gentler in your efforts to set it.
In treating the leg, there is something extra compared to the arm.  After you have aligned the dressings and completed all your work, take two pieces of pine wood, used for leveling rooms and placed between the cracks of planks, or similar material such as palm leaves.
Choose one with a moderate thickness, not thin, and let its length extend from the knee down to the end of the leg.  Then wrap two layers of cloth around each one lengthwise, placing one on the leg and the other on the opposite side.
Let the length extend from the knee to the foot, then tie the two pieces in three places, at the ends and in the middle.  With this restraint, the leg will prevent tilting to the sides and be properly held in place.  A groove of wood may also be used along the length of the leg.
Place it [the groove/restraint] to prevent movement.  This is especially important if the fracture is accompanied by a wound.  Examine the leg daily, carefully checking for swelling or other abnormalities. If anything of that sort happens, address it appropriately until healed.  The leg bone will set in thirty days, or thereabouts.
Chapter Seventeen.
Fractures of the leg and fingers.
The heel bone is not prone to fracture.  Leg bones may fracture, and fingers rarely fracture, but are more commonly bruised.
If a fracture of the leg bones occurs and you see the bones overlapping, have the patient stand on the ground, upright as if walking.  Then, place your foot on the protruding bones and press them down, straightening them.
Until they return to their proper positions.  Then, take the bandage and splint, place a small, flat board with two flattened ends beneath the sole of the foot.  Then, tightly bind the foot with bandages and any other necessary materials.
After three or four days, remove the splint.  You will find the bones aligned, whether fractured or dislocated.  The procedure is as described.  If some fingers are fractured, realign and straighten them according to the instructions for realigning fingers of the hand.
Then, create a splint for the fractured finger, making it slightly wider than the finger itself.  Then, place the board, as described, beneath the foot, and bind it tightly. If two, three, or more bones are fractured.
Place a splint on each finger, sized appropriately and wrapped in soft cloth.  Bind the board to the bottom of the foot, positioning each end of the board outside the sole of the foot for proper support.
You should not forget to treat all fractures with the symptoms we have mentioned, and address each symptom appropriately.
Chapter Eighteen.
In the fracture of the woman's pubic bone and the man's penis.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, have her sit in a squatting position, then gently incline her towards her back, holding her from behind.  Then, the midwife should fill the vulva with cotton until it is full and forms a ball within.
Then, have the woman move and gently raise her hips.  The cotton will then emerge, forming a ball at the opening of the vulva, and the fractured bone will be realigned. Then, place a support under her back.  When she wishes to urinate, carefully remove the cotton so she can urinate.
Return it to the position in which you first inserted it, then return to the initial support.  Do this for seven days or so, and it will be set.  If you wish, take a sheep's bladder, tie a tube of reed to its opening, and insert the entire bladder into her vulva.
Then, blow forcefully into the tube, inflating the bladder within the vulva.  The fracture will be realigned. Then, pack it with cotton and keep her in the manner described above until she heals.  As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, this [method] is used for its repair.
And its [treatment] is the same as we described for the hip bone.  The correct procedure for these unusual fractures, which rarely occur, is not difficult for one with some experience and understanding of this book; indeed, the fracture itself often guides you.
Follow the correct procedure for its repair and tightening.  Understand this.  If a man's penis is fractured, take the windpipe of a goose, insert the penis into it, then place a bandage of cloth over it and bandage it. Leave it for three days or so until healed.
Chapter Nineteen.
On the reduction of bone fractures when accompanied by wounds.
If a fracture is accompanied by a wound, especially if the bone is large like the femur or humerus, or similar, one should promptly perform venesection if the conditions for venesection, as previously presented, allow.
If the wound is bleeding, then one should promptly cauterize it with powdered alum, if nothing else is available.  Then, begin reducing the fracture on that very day, and do not delay.
If no hot swelling occurs, then if a hot swelling does occur, postpone the reduction to the ninth day until the hot swelling subsides.  Do not approach it at all on the third and fourth days, for it will expose the patient to poor symptoms.
If the fractured bone is protruding through the skin, exposed, one should attempt to gently reposition and align it with one's hands and a slight extension. If one cannot reposition and align it with one's hands, reposition it with this instrument, which is made of iron, seven or eight fingers in length. And its width should match the size of the wound.
Therefore, the physician should prepare three or four of them, according to the treatment needed for each type of fracture.  Let them be round, with a slight thickness so that they do not bend when pressed during use.
It should be sharp-pointed, with a bend at the tip.  The top should be thick, and from the middle to the bottom, much thinner. This is its image/shape.
It is called a *perim* in Greek, meaning a small lever.  The sharp, bent tip should be placed on the protruding bone end and used to gently push it back until the bone is aligned, and then the ends of the fractured bone should be brought into alignment.
If the fractured end is thin and the instrument does not grip it properly, then the end of that bone should be cut so that the instrument can hold it. If you cannot successfully realign the bone as described, cut it using a similar cutting tool as mentioned before.
Or saw it with one of the saws, as best you can. Then remove any remaining roughness or thin scales from the bone. If, after realigning the bone, the patient experiences severe, painful discomfort, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you can restore it to its natural position, do so, for you will greatly benefit the patient. Once the bone setting is complete, dip a cloth in a dark, astringent drink, especially if it is summer, and do not place any ointments or greasy substances on the wound.
To prevent putrefaction and decay. Then, use splints when finished setting the bone. Leave the wound exposed by cutting a hole in the bandages with scissors, to the size of the wound.
Beware, be very careful, not to tighten the wound with the fracture.  Often, ignorant physicians do this, causing death, gangrene, or chronic inflammation in their patients. Your tightening should be gentle and loose, unlike the tightening of other fractures.
If the wound is bad or large, and you fear some of the adverse effects we have described, and the patient experiences distressing pain at the site, you should not apply splints. Instead, make bandages of strong cloth in the area of the splints, and secure them tightly.
If, after a day or two, you see pus forming in the wound, remove the cloth soaked in the liquid. Then use the dressings and ointments which we customarily use for wounds, like the four-fold ointment, and similar ones. You should loosen the bandages.
Examine the wound every evening and morning until it heals and is cured.  The limb should be positioned so that the pus drains downwards easily. If the wound takes many days to heal and the pus does not stop, know that there are small fragments of bone.
Then you should probe the wound.  Any fragments that are loose, remove them and take them out.  If any fragments are not loose, and are irritating the limb and causing pain, then cut and remove them in whatever way you can.
If the wound develops a cold, or eating away, or another type of corruption or putrefaction, then you should treat each such symptom with the corresponding treatment described in its section.
If a large, prominent bone like the femur or humerus, or similar large bones, is fractured, you should not attempt to pull it out or remove it. Often death results from that.  Leave it until it becomes rotten; perhaps it will fall out on its own after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see an opportunity to do so, otherwise leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
On treating the suppuration that occurs after some fractures.
This suppuration often occurs after the fracture has healed, especially if it is near the joints.  It makes the appearance of the limb unsightly and may prevent the limb from its natural function.
If the suppuration is fresh, use astringent medicines such as dates, frankincense, myrrh, henna, acacia, and the like, taking some or all of them, mixing them with an astringent drink or egg white or vinegar.
Apply it to the suppuration with care, securing it tightly, and leave it on for many days without removing it, then remove it and repeat, until the suppuration disappears, God willing, or apply a tightly sealed sheet of lead to it.
Lead has the property of eliminating all pus in the members. If the suppuration has hardened and become firm, and necessity compels its removal, cut it from above, and remove the excess, or pare it with a rasp until it is gone, and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In treating a fracture, if the limb is afterwards thin, contrary to its original nature.
If a fractured bone is set and the limb afterwards remains thin and weak, this is due to many causes. One cause is either the excessive loosening and tying of the ligaments improperly, or the excessive tightening of the ligaments so that nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb; or excessive poulticing, or excessive movements at inappropriate times, or a lack of blood in the body of the patient and its weakness.
The treatment for this is to nourish the patient and enrich his body so that the blood increases in him; using baths, bringing joy and happiness to him, and similar things; then applying pitch to the limb to attract much nourishment to it, or continuing poulticing with lukewarm water. Until nourishment flows to it and it returns to its natural shape.
Chapter Twenty-two.
On treating fractured bones if they are rejoined crookedly and prevented from acting as they should.
If, after the healing of a limb that has been set, a deformity, a prominence of the fractured bone, or a complication occurs, and the appearance of the limb becomes unsightly. However, if the limb is not prevented from its natural function, one should not accept the claim of those who say to break the limb from the head, as many ignorant physicians and setters do in our country.
This action is highly reprehensible, leading to great risk, and worse, injury. However, if the crookedness and complication are recent, one should apply water in which relaxing herbs such as mallow leaves and root, and crown of thorns, and the like, have been boiled.
And apply relaxing bandages, such as well-made diachylon, or take the sap of the mallow root, mix it with chicken fat and butter, and bandage with it, or take ripe figs, grind them with pigeon dung and similar medicines called resolving remedies.
And the complication will resolve with gentle, continuous rubbing with the hands, and the limb should be moved in all directions all the time.  If the deformity is old, severe, and hardened, and necessity demands treatment with iron.
One should then cut through the top, release the bone connection, and cut off any remaining complication or excess bone with gentle and careful cuts, and then treat the wound as previously mentioned until healed.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
The jaw is a dislocation of a joint from its place, hindering movement, disfiguring the limb, and causing the afflicted severe pain and suffering.  Therefore, when a person suffers a dislocated jaw, one should immediately attempt to return it to its place.
Do not delay it at all. If delayed, the area will swell and become a hard lump, making it difficult to reposition the jaw.  Therefore, it should not be delayed, moved, or stretched while swollen, as this often causes painful spasms and suffering to the patient.
However, if this occurs, one should first bleed the patient, then wait until the swelling subsides somewhat.  Then, apply warm water and ointment to the affected area.  Then, gently reposition the jaw, and treat each affected area as described in its proper section.
The chapters on the jaw are also arranged, as was done previously for fractures, from the upper part of the body downwards.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On treating the lower jaw.
The jaws rarely dislocate, and their dislocation occurs in one of two ways: either they slip slightly out of place, relaxing somewhat, or they dislocate completely, relaxing fully. The jaw will slip towards the chest, causing the patient to drool and unable to retain saliva, or to close his jaw, or to articulate his tongue in speech. If the dislocation is slight, it will usually return to its place easily.
However, if the dislocation is complete, the jaw must be quickly returned and delay is unacceptable.  The attendant should hold the patient's head while the physician inserts one finger into the socket of the jaw inside the patient's mouth if the dislocation is on one side.
Or if the dislocation is on both sides, the physician should insert both thumbs, and the other fingers on the outside to align it.  He should instruct the patient to relax and open his jaw in all directions and the physician should realign and reposition the jaw until it returns to its place.
If returning the jaw is difficult, especially if both are dislocated, use warm water and ointments to ease their return. Do not delay their return, as we have said.  Once they return to their normal alignment, and the patient's mouth closes without relaxing further, apply bandages soaked in a mixture of wax, fat, and rose.
Then gently tie it with a loose bandage. The patient should sleep on his back, with his head propped up between two pillows, so that he does not move it to either side, nor attempt to chew anything.  His food should be soft and easy to swallow.
Until the pain subsides and the jaw sets, he may eat as he wishes.  He should use gentleness and avoid forcing his mouth open for eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw sets and heals.
If the jaws, when dislocated, do not return to their proper positions, considerable fever and persistent headaches often result, and the patient's stomach may become upset, possibly causing repeated vomiting.  If you observe this, know that the condition is severe.
Often, a patient exhibiting this condition dies within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On the reduction of the collarbone and shoulder joint.
The collarbone does not dislocate from the inner side due to its connection to the chest.  It may, however, dislocate outward, and this is apparent to the touch.  To reduce it, the patient should lie on their back with their arms extended.
Then press firmly on the area with your hand; it will return to its place.  Then apply a bandage and splints, and secure them.  As for the end of the collarbone that is near the shoulder and connected to it, it rarely dislocates.  If it ever does dislocate, it should be returned and adjusted as described above, or in whatever way is possible for you.
Then apply a bandage, splints, and a tight wrap.  Instruct the patient to remain still and quiet until healed.  This same treatment will also reduce the shoulder joint if it dislocates.
Chapter Twenty-six.
On reducing the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder joint can dislocate in three ways: one is downwards towards the armpit, another towards the chest, and a third, rarely, upwards toward the shoulder itself.
It does not dislocate behind the shoulder blade or in front of the nerve.  Most commonly, it dislocates downwards towards the armpit, particularly in those with less muscle tissue, as it easily comes out and easily goes back in.
However, those with abundant muscle tissue experience the opposite; it comes out with difficulty and goes back in with difficulty.  Sometimes, a person might experience a blow or fall, causing the shoulder to swell with a hot, painful swelling, and mistakenly believe that it has dislocated.  Therefore, it is necessary to examine it thoroughly to verify its condition.
Then, proceed with the treatment. If the dislocation is downwards toward the armpit, when comparing the dislocated shoulder to the healthy one, you will find an obvious difference. You will find the head of the dislocated shoulder to be deformed, and under the armpit, upon touching, the head of the dislocated shoulder feels like an egg.
The patient cannot raise their arm to their ear, nor perform any other movements with it.  Likewise, if it has dislocated towards the chest or upwards, this will be apparent upon examination.
This dislocation may be easily reduced if it is recent or if the patient is a child.  To reduce it, have a servant raise the arm upward, then place your thumbs under the armpit and forcefully raise the joint upward to its proper position, while the servant raises and extends the arm upward.
Then lower it downwards, and it will return quickly.  If it does not return by the method described, and the dislocation has occurred many days prior, the patient should bathe in hot water and use poultices that relax and soften, such as cooked fennel root, fenugreek, and crown flower in water.
Then the patient lies on their back, and a ball of wool of moderate firmness is placed under their armpit.  The physician then places their heel on the ball and holds the patient's hand, extending it downwards.
And the physician pushes the ball with their foot, and it returns to its proper position. Or, if you prefer, you can reduce it in this way: have a man taller than the patient stand beside him, placing his shoulder under the patient's armpit and lifting the armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. And another servant pulls the patient's hand downwards toward their abdomen. If the patient is light, then another item should be attached to them to weigh them down, and the joint will return immediately.  The dislocation may also be reduced in another way.
Another method involves placing a long, round-headed wooden plank on the ground, similar to the pestle head of a mortar, neither thick nor thin.  A soft cloth is placed on the head of the plank, and the patient, standing lengthwise on the plank, has the plank placed under their armpit.
Then the patient extends their hand downwards on the other side and also extends their body forcefully on the other side. The joint will quickly return to its place. If the reduction is difficult by all the methods mentioned, then use this treatment: take a plank about two cubits long. Its width should be about four fingers and its thickness about two fingers. It should have a round head to easily enter the armpit in this manner:
A soft cloth is then tied to the round head so the plank does not injure the patient. The plank is then placed under the armpit, and the entire hand and arm are extended downwards on the plank. The plank is tied to the upper arm, forearm, and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a crossbar, laid horizontally. The hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is left hanging from the other side. The joint will enter its place immediately. If it enters in any way possible, a moderately sized ball of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then, the prepared bandage made from mill dust, frankincense, and egg white is applied to the entire shoulder, spiraling upwards.  The ball is firmly tied beneath the armpit.  The bandage is tied around the upper part, and the hand is suspended by a bandage to the neck.
The hand should not be moved for seven days. The patient's diet should be kept light, to strengthen the limb and hasten recovery.  After seven, or five, days, the bandage is removed and the patient is encouraged to move the arm. If the arm remains strong and does not weaken, it has healed.
If the joint dislocates frequently due to moisture or other causes, cauterization with three small cauterizing instruments should be applied, as described in the chapter on cauterization. If all of this is done and the bandage is removed after seven days, and the joint does not remain stable,
If the bandage and its tying are repeated many times but the joint remains unstable, dislocating and weakening, and the patient cannot lift the arm, know that the nerve in the shoulder joint head has been severed, stretched, or weakened. In such a case, know that the joint will never stabilize.
For dislocations of the jaw, which occur near the chest and breast and towards the back, it is reduced by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns.  All other binding and treatments should be used until the healing is complete.  If, after healing, there is tenderness in the area and slow movement,
The patient should frequently use baths to soften the tenderness and return it to its original condition.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the treatment of dislocations of the elbow.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty, and also returns with difficulty.  It dislocates in all directions, especially forward or backward.  Its dislocation is not hidden from you, since it is evident to the eye and touch in whatever manner it has been dislocated.
If you compare the dislocated elbow to the healthy one, the dislocation's outward appearance becomes clear to you due to the concave joint.  The arm cannot be bent, nor can the shoulder touch it.  You should act quickly and relocate the dislocation before a hot swelling develops.
If a hot swelling develops, its relocation becomes difficult, and it may never heal, especially if the dislocation is backward.  This is the worst type of dislocation, the most painful, and often results in death.
If it is possible to relocate, the attendant should extend his/her arms fully outstretched, and the physician's hand should be positioned above and below the elbow.  The physician should push the joint back into place using both thumbs or the base of their hands.
If the dislocation is forward, the hand may be bent once so that the palm strikes the shoulder that is next to it. If the dislocation does not return, use very strong and forceful extension; two attendants should extend the arm.
Two attendants should also hold the patient to prevent movement during the extension.  Then, the arm is rotated in every direction after a folded cloth or wide bandage is wrapped around the hand. If the physician works on the joint with their hands, they should anoint the joint with ointment to facilitate its easy movement.
Then the joint is pushed forcefully until it returns.  After its return, a bandage containing a compress, desiccant, and egg white should be firmly applied, and the arm should be suspended from the patient's neck and left for several days.
Then it is removed. If the joint is fixed in its place and the ligament is released, leave it. If you see the joint is not sufficiently strong, reapply the bandage and splint and leave it for several days until it is strong, then remove it. If a swelling occurs after the joint has returned and been treated, [do this].
If movement is difficult, use soaking in a bath, gentle massage, and prodding until it softens. Alternatively, place a thick, soft sheep's fleece on the joint, bind it, and leave it for a day and night.  Then remove it and have the patient enter the bath.  If he sweats, massage the joint moderately.
Then repeat the application of the fleece one, two, and three times with bath treatments until it softens. If you wish to apply moist, finely ground cow dung mixed with ghee, apply it repeatedly, for it will soften and return to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the treatment of a dislocated wrist.
The wrist frequently dislocates, and its reduction is easier than other joints, but it is important to quickly reduce the dislocation at the time of occurrence, before the area becomes inflamed or a hot swelling develops.
To reduce the dislocation, place the patient's wrist on a board. A servant should extend the patient's arm. The physician should place their hand on the protuberance of the joint and push it until it returns.  However, one must examine if the dislocation has gone to the back of the hand.
Let the patient place the palm of their hand on the board during extension and reduction.  If the dislocation is evident on the outside of the hand, then let the patient's back of the hand be on the board so the physician's hand is on the same protuberance of the joint. If it returns immediately, well and good; otherwise, apply a dressing that will soothe the swelling.
Leave it be and do not attempt to reduce it again, for it cannot be tolerated, and you cannot reduce it after several days have passed, except that the joint will remain crooked, causing no harm to the patient, unless the hand weakens and the patient cannot grasp anything.
In that case, you will know that the nerve is severed or compressed; there is no remedy except to apply cauterization.  Perhaps it will be helpful, perhaps not.  Once the wrist is reduced, apply the dressing we described, then bind it tightly and leave it for five days.
Then it is removed, and the hand is exercised.  If its movement is impaired, and some stiffness is apparent, soften it with heated water and massage repeatedly until it becomes supple.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may dislocate in any direction.  If a finger dislocates towards the palm's surface or its underside, extend the finger and push the dislocation back with your thumb until it returns to its place.  Then bind the fingertip and support it toward the direction to which it dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and stretch it until it stands straight on that day. If it is nighttime, bind it in the same way.  Continue to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, binding it at night.  Do this for several days until it strengthens.
Likewise, if it dislocates towards the palm's underside, bind it towards that same direction and do the same as before until it heals.  Likewise, do this for dislocations in any other direction.
Chapter Thirty.
In treating dislocation of the spine.
When a vertebra in the back or neck has completely dislocated, or many vertebrae have left their positions, there is no cure, for death quickly comes to the afflicted person.
A sign of this is that the afflicted person's stool comes out involuntarily; they cannot hold it.  Often, some of their limbs, either legs or arms, or one of them, become relaxed.  If only one vertebra is out of place, it often moves.
And its displacement can be in four directions.  That which moves backward is called a hump.  Its treatment is to observe; if the hump arose in childhood, there is no treatment and no cure at all.  But that which arose from a fall or blow, or similar,
The ancients have described various treatments with lengthy accounts, much of which yields little benefit.  I have condensed this into a small amount that suffices for much of what they have presented, clarifying the meaning and explaining it. I have also depicted the apparatus differently from how they described and explained it.
I say that a hump that occurs in the front of the chest has no remedy and no cure, and likewise for those that occur to the sides.  Only those that occur in the back are treated, specifically as I describe, by lying the afflicted person on their face.
On a level platform near a wall, a moist pad is spread beneath him so as not to harm his chest.  Then, a vertical plank, planted in a hole in the ground, is placed near his head at one end of the platform, and another plank near his feet at the other end of the platform.
One attendant holds one plank, which is not secured in the hole, and another attendant holds the other in the same manner.  Then, a soft, secure bandage is wrapped around the patient's chest and under their armpits.  A portion of the bandage is extended to the plank at the head and tied to it.
Then, another bandage is tightened over his hips, knees, and ankles.  Then, all the bandages are gathered and tied to the other plank near his feet.  Then, each attendant pulls the plank towards their side using the bandage, and the planks remain in their fixed positions.
However, they are not fastened, as we said. The physician places his hands firmly on the protrusion to cause it to return, or he places a board over it and leans on it with his legs until it returns.  If it does not return with this treatment, a board about three cubits long is taken.
A cavity is carved in the wall, near the patient, to receive the end of the board.  The middle of the board is placed on the hump, and the physician places his feet on the other end, pulling firmly to compress the protrusion.
And it returns to its place.  If you wish, you can construct it with a hand-spun coil, by driving two planks, each one cubit long, into the ground at the patient's head and at their feet, at the end of the store.
Let there be one span between each plank.  A hole is made in each plank, through which the coil passes.  All the planks are firmly secured in the ground so that they do not move.  A twisted stick (the coil) is inserted, and the bandage is wound around it in the holes of the two planks.
At its end is a hole securely fastened to a stick one span long, which is wound around it.  The same is done in the other two planks.  Then the bandage tied around the patient's chest is pulled on the coil at the head, and the bandages tied around the patient's legs are pulled on the coil at the feet.
Then a servant stands at each coil, twisting the coil with the twisting tool.  The physician straightens the hump as previously described.  This is the image of the coil, the store, and the patient.
Then, after the curvature returns and the area is even, the dried bandage should be covered with egg white, then with lint.  Then a splint of wood, whose width is about three fingers or so, and whose length corresponds to the extent of the hump, should be placed over the bandage.
And it should be placed over some healthy beads. Then it is tied with the necessary bandage, and the patient should use soft food until he is healed. If some protuberance remains in the area at the end of the healing process, then the medicines that relax and soften should be used, along with the splint that we described, for a long time.
A lead plate may also be used in this case.  A protuberance may appear at the end of the vertebrae of the back, and it may be mistaken for a dislocation.  This might be an extra bone that has protruded.  Do not use this treatment for it, as death might occur.
Chapter Thirty-one.
On the treatment of the dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and are not subject to the slight displacement and subluxation that occur in other joints.  The hip joint dislocates in four ways.
This is because it dislocates inwards, outwards, forwards, and backwards.  It dislocates inwards most often, and rarely forwards or backwards.  A sign of dislocation inwards is that if you compare the healthy leg to the affected one, the healthy leg will appear longer.
The knee will be more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient will be unable to bend their leg at the groin.  The area next to the groin will be swollen and noticeably prominent, indicating that the head of the femur has moved there.
The signs of dislocation outwards will be the opposite of these signs.  A sign of dislocation forwards is that the patient can fully extend their leg, but cannot bend it without pain, and if they try to walk forward, they will not be able to do so.
Urine retention and swelling of the groin occur.  When walking, the patient steps on the heel.  A sign of dislocation backwards is that the patient cannot straighten their knee, nor bend it until after bending their groin, and the leg will also appear shorter than the other.
The groin is relaxed, and the head of the femur is clearly visible near the hip area.  As for the reduction of these dislocations, one must observe if the dislocation has been present for a long time and the patient has not attempted its reduction, remaining in its current state; then there is no treatment at all.
Therefore, it should not be allowed to happen.  As for a recent dislocation, of any of the four types, immediately attempt to twist the joint, stretching it inwards and outwards, and moving it to the right and left; perhaps...
If it returns, it does not require any further treatment. If it does not return this way, then a strong, capable assistant should be positioned to extend the leg from below, either by hand or with a bandage tied above the knee, and another assistant should extend it from above.
By placing their hands under the armpits, then tightening a soft bandage at the root of the thigh. A third assistant holds the end of the bandage, and the extension is either performed from the front, from the groin towards the collarbone, or from behind, towards the back.
All of them should extend at once, until the patient is lifted off the ground and suspended.  This type of extension is common to all four types. If the dislocation returns as we have described, then there is no need for further treatment.  However, if not, each type requires its own specific treatment as I have mentioned.
As for the specific reduction if the dislocation is inwards, the patient should lie on their healthy side, then a bandage is placed around the root of the thigh, between the head of the thigh and the area below the groin.
Then, the bandage is extended upward from the groin, toward the upper part of the body and the collarbone.  A strong assistant grasps the thick part of the affected thigh with both arms and pulls it outward forcefully, and it will return to its place.
This method is easier than other methods for the reduction of this limb.  If this method proves ineffective, and the dislocation does not respond, then the patient's legs should be bound with a strong, soft bandage around the heels and knees.
Each bandage should be positioned four fingers' width apart. The affected leg should be extended further than the other, by two fingers' width.  Then the patient should be suspended from a beam in the house by their head, with their feet approximately two arms' length from the ground.
Then a strong boy should grasp the head of the thigh, and another boy should pull on the patient. The boy holding the thigh should push forcefully, and the joint will quickly return to its place.  As for the specific reduction if the dislocation is outwards...
The patient should lie on a mat, as described for a hunchback, and the bandage should be tightened around the affected leg and chest.  Then, two pieces of wood should be placed, one near the feet and one near the head.  Finally, a further piece of wood should be placed securely in the middle of the mat.
Damp cloths should be wrapped around the wood to prevent injury to the patient.  The wood should be positioned between the patient's thighs to prevent downward pull during stretching.  Each attendant should then pull in their direction, while the physician adjusts the hip with their hands. If the hip returns to its place...
Otherwise, place the board on him and press as we described for the hunchback, with the exception that the patient should lie on his healthy side.  If the dislocation is forward, then the patient's leg should be stretched once while in this same position on the mat.
The physician places the palm of their right hand on the affected hip, then presses it with the other hand.  The pressure should be extended downward toward the knee.  If the dislocation is backward, the patient should not be stretched downward.
He should be elevated off the ground, but placed on something firm.  Also, his hip should be externally rotated, as described for his position on the mat on his face with the bandages tight.
As we said previously, the board should also be used to press on the area where the joint has dislocated.  When the hip joint returns as it should, a sign of its return will be apparent to you: the patient's legs will be stretched.
If you see both legs are even and the patient can flex and extend the leg without difficulty, know that the limb has returned to its proper place.  Then, join the thighs, apply the dressing, and tie it with a bandage so tightly that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain still for three or four days.  Then loosen the bandage and dressing, and compare the legs. If you find them equal in size, know that the fracture has set.  Release the patient to walk; however, if you see any sign of weakness, do not permit walking.
Return and bandage it again, tightening it as you did before. Leave it for three more days, then loosen it.  The patient should walk slowly for several days until the limb is strong, God willing.
The Twenty-third Chapter.
On treating a fractured knee.
The knee fractures in three ways: inward, outward, and downward (i.e., backward).  It does not fracture forward.  A sign of a fracture is to tell the patient to press their leg against their thigh.
If the leg does not adhere to the thigh, know that the knee is fractured.  To set all types of fractures, have the patient sit with their legs extended, if they are strong enough.  A servant sits behind them, holding their waist and gently bending it backward. Then you sit on their thighs.
Press your back against his face, place his leg between your legs, then grasp his knee with both hands, interlocking your fingers on the knee.  Then, firmly press both sides of his knee with your hands.  Another attendant should extend the leg until the knee returns to its place.
A sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh, smoothly, not forcibly. Then bandage it, and secure the leg to the thigh.  Tie them together with a bandage for three or four days, then release them.  Only light walking should be done for several days, until strength returns.
If you cannot return it using the method described, then use strong traction with the bandages previously described for hip treatment until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-three.
On the treatment of a dislocated heel.
The heel may dislocate slightly, or completely.  Its dislocation may be inward or outward. A sign of dislocation is that the heel is swollen and prominent on the side to which it has dislocated.
For a slight dislocation, gentle traction with the hands is sufficient to restore it. However, if it is completely dislocated, the patient should sit and be held by a strong attendant behind his middle.
Then, with your right hand, grasp his foot from above, and with your left hand, grasp it from below, at the ankle. Pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with your left, twice. Then, extend it with your left hand and push the front of the foot towards the leg with your right hand, without force.
Do this twice as described.  Then, on the third time, push the front of the foot towards the leg while pulling on the ankle. If it returns in one or two attempts following this method, and you see the foot is straight, then stop. Otherwise, repeat the procedure, for it will return.
If it does not return using the methods described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground and firmly place a firmly tied peg or stake in the ground, positioned between his thighs and wrapped with a cloth to prevent injury. Then, an assistant should hold the patient's thigh.
Then another assistant stretches the leg, either with their hands or with a bandage tied around the leg.  Each assistant pulls in the opposite direction, while the peg remains between the patient's thighs, holding them to prevent the body from pulling down during the stretch. Then the physician straightens the joint with their hands.
Another assistant holds the healthy leg downwards.  If the joint returns quickly, then take the bandage and splint and tighten them with strong bandages. Tie the foot to the lower leg with a bandage.  Care must be taken to avoid the nerve located above the heel from behind.
So that the bandage is not too tight and causes pain. Then leave it for two or three days. If the bandage loosens, tighten it again, then release it on the third or fourth day. The patient must refrain from walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this time, there is a risk that the joint will dislocate and become damaged, and treatment will no longer be effective.  If a hot swelling appears, the treatments described elsewhere in the text for soothing and poulticing should be used until it subsides.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On the treatment of a dislocated toe.
The dislocated part should be straightened with a gentle pull, without force, which is not difficult but easy. If the dislocation is in one of the toes on the top of the foot, the patient should place their foot on a level surface.
Or on a board, while he stands like a walker. Then, you should place your foot on the protruding joints and press down firmly with your foot until they return to their correct position and no protrusion is visible.
Then place a board under the sole of their foot, encompassing the entire foot, with two ends. Tie it tightly for three days, then loosen it and keep them from walking for many days until it strengthens and a return is avoided, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-five.
On the types of dislocation occurring with a wound, or a fracture, or both.
When such a thing happens and you attempt its treatment and reduction, it often leads to death.
Therefore, one should not undertake the treatment of such a condition unless he is skilled in the art, experienced, gentle, compassionate, careful, and not rash or overconfident.  Initially, he should use only medications to relieve the hot swelling and then release the patient.
By God's will, except where you hope for recovery from injury with the mildness of the illness and some hope appears, then return it to its place at once, before the hot swelling occurs.
If the member returns to its place as desired, use the treatment that relieves hot swellings, and treat the wound with suitable drying ointments.  If the dislocation is with a fracture and splinters have separated from the bone, remove them. Follow in this regard what we have mentioned concerning simple diseases in their respective places.
Strive diligently and purify yourself from entering the path of uncertainty, as I have previously instructed and advised you. This will preserve your reputation and safeguard your standing, God willing.
